Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
advanced
12 min
The Dispute on Whether a Legal Cause Can Be Present Without Its Effect Due to a Preventer
Presenting and analyzing the usul dispute about takhsis al-illa and the role of man'i.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on the role of preventers (man') in qiyas, evaluate the three arguments of the preventers (mani'un), and distinguish between textually indicated and derived causes in terms of specification.

Core Idea
A legal cause can be blocked by a preventer without being invalidated — the cause remains, but its effect is absent.

This passage from Fawatih al-Rahamut presents a foundational usul dispute: can a legal cause (illa) be present without its ruling due to a preventer? The Hanafi school affirms this, distinguishing between the complete cause and the effective cause. The text examines three arguments from opponents and provides responses, clarifying the Hanafi methodology. We will walk through each argument and its rebuttal to understand the precise Hanafi position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central issue
Recognize that the dispute is about whether a legal cause (illa) must always produce its ruling, or whether a preventer (mani') can block the effect while the cause remains.
The text frames this as a semantic dispute, but the practical consequences are significant.
2
Distinguish the two positions
The 'preventers' (mani'un) hold that absence of ruling indicates absence of cause. The 'permissives' (mujawwizun) hold that absence can be due to a preventer.
Hanafis are among the permissives.
3
Examine the first argument
The preventers argue that evidence of causality (daliil al-i'tibar) conflicts with evidence of disregard (daliil al-ihdar) from absence of ruling, leading to dropping both. The Hanafi response: absence is only evidence of disregard if there is no preventer; here a preventer is present.
This preserves the causality evidence.
4
Examine the second argument
The preventers analogize legal causes to rational causes, which are essential and inseparable. The Hanafi response: rational causes are essential (bi al-dhat), while legal causes are posited (bi al-wad') by the Lawgiver, so they can have their effect blocked by a preventer. Even in natural causes, effect can be blocked (e.g., wet wood not burning).
The author critiques a previous answer and provides a deeper analogy with the agent cause.
5
Examine the third argument and the circularity objection
The preventers argue that knowledge of causality depends on knowledge of preventiveness and vice versa, leading to circularity. The Hanafi response distinguishes between initial presumption of causality (from textual paths independently) and its continuation; also between actual and potential preventiveness, breaking the circularity.
This involves a nuanced discussion of epistemic dependencies.
6
Distinguish textually indicated vs. derived causes
The passage concludes that textually indicated causes (illa mansusa) do not admit specification when the text is definitive, because they are like a general text. Derived causes (illa mustanbata) are probabilistic and can be overridden by a preventer.
This distinction affects how qiyas operates in practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

التامة، فتثبت ولا تزل والله أعلم بمراد عباده الكرام. المانعون في المستنبطة (قالوا: لو صحت المستنبطة مع لكان هذا التخلف المانع وإلا) أي وإن لم يكن لمانع بل بلا مانع (فلا اقتضاء) علية (والمانع أنما يكون العلم به، وإلا فيجوز انتفاء المقتضى فلا تعلم المانعية (ولا يعلم الاقتضاء) وقت التخلف (إلا بعد العلم بالمانعية) فإنّ التخلف من غير علم المانع يوجب التردد في اقتضاء المؤثر، فحينئذ لزم الدور (وقد يجاب دور أقول : المانع) أي الظن به في محل التخلف موقوف على ظنها فيه وإلا فيجوز انتفاء الحكم لانتفاء المقتضى (وظنها فيه موقوف على المانع أي على الظن به (فيه) لأن التخلّف من غير مانع دليل عدم المقتضى، المانع في محل التخلف فتكون علة (و) احتمال (عدمه) فيه فلا تكون فلا تعارض بين دليلها ودليل عدمها الذي هو التخلف لرجحان الأول وأجيب بأن الشك في أحد المتقابلين يوجب في الآخر لأنه تجويز الطرفين على السواء (فقولك : العلية مظنونة وعدمها مشكوك تناقض بل العلية أيضاً صارت مشكوكة، فإن قلت : فما معنى قولهم: الظن لا يزول بالشك، قال : (وأما قول الفقهاء الظن لا يرتفع بالشك فمعناه أن حكم الأقوى) الثابت (لا يزول بالأضعف الطاريء ( شرعاً) أي أوجب الشرع العمل بمقتضى الأقوى، وإن طرأ الأضعف المعارض ولا يمكن مثله ،ههنا لأن (الكلام ههنا في نفس الظن هل يحصل عند التخلف أم لا (أقول) يمكن أن يقرر هكذا (التخلف في نفسه مع قطع النظر عن عروض أمر (مشكك، فإذا انضم مع دليل العلية احتمال المانع صارت العلية مظنونة ظناً قوياً) لاضمحلال احتمال انتفاء الاقتضاء بدليله والمشكوك يصير بالمرجح مظنوناً بالضرورة فالصواب) في الجواب (أن عند الانفراد كل من دليل المستنبطة والتخلف (يوجب الظن) لكل من العلة وعدمها (وعند الاجتماع يحصل الشك في الطرفين للتعارض بينهما فلا نسلم قولك التخلف مشكك) بل هو مفيد عدم العلة (وفيه ما فيه فإن احتمال وجود المانع وعدمه كلاهما قائمان على السواء، فالتخلف في نفسه مشكك فلا مجال للمنع إلا أن يدعي أن احتمال عدم المانع بعيد لكثرة انتفاء الحكم لانتفاء المقتضى، وأصالة الظن بعدم العلية فافهم. وأما المنصوصة فلا تقبل النقض) وتخلف الحكم عنه اللزوم بطلان النص العام المفيد للزوم الحكم إياه، فإنّ التنصيص على العلة بمنزلة قوله كل ما توجد العلة فيه يوجد الحكم بخلاف المستنبطة، فإن دليلها الاقتران) أي اقتران الحكم مع عدم المانع فيجوز التخلف لاحتماله وأجيب في المختصر إن كان) النص قطعياً فعدم القبول للتخصيص (مسلم) ولا نزاع فيه وإلا قبل) التخصيص (ويقدر
The cause is absent [in effect] for [that which] does not meet the conditions of effectiveness, and one prevents from the complete effective cause. The author of 'al-Kashf' said: The dispute on the issue of specification of the cause goes back to expression, because the cause in a place of non-absence is valid according to both groups, but in a place of absence the ruling is non-existent, except that the non-existence according to the preventer is attributed to the absence [of the cause], and according to the permissive to the preventer. The author [of the commentary] said: And the truth is that it is conceptual, whose fruit appears in the answer to the refutation: according to the permissive, it is permissible to present the preventer, but not according to the preventer; and in the issue of the disruption of suitability due to the existence of a binding, preponderant, or equal harm, according to the permissive there is no disruption but rather absence due to a preventer, and according to the preventer it is disrupted. End. And this is not as it should be, for the negation of the ruling is necessarily due to the preventer, so the permissive attributes it to it, and the preventer attributes it to the absence of the cause because the absence of the preventer enters into it. As for the disruption of suitability by a preponderant or equal harm, the statement has not been established from the preventer such that it would be its fruit, so understand. (And) they said (secondly): Conflict between the evidence of consideration (i.e., considering the cause, which is one of its paths) and the evidence of disregard (which is the ruling about it), and conflict necessitates dropping, so there is no consideration, so no causality. (We said): The existence of the evidence of disregard is denied; rather, absence is not evidence of disregard except without a preventer, and the discussion is in its presence, so no dropping. And if you reflect, you know that the evidence is complete if the complete cause is intended, for the absence of the ruling is evidence of the incompleteness of the cause. (And) they said (thirdly): The legal cause is like the rational cause in necessitating the ruling, and there is no specification in it, so there is no specification in the legal [cause]. (And it was answered): That rational causes are causes by essence, and what is essential does not separate, so their causality and effect do not separate, so the effect does not separate from them. And these [legal causes] are causes by placement from the Lawgiver, so they may not entail their effect, so they differ. Thus in al-Mukhtasar. I say: This answer is unsatisfactory, because the Lawgiver made them necessitators of the ruling, and His making is true, so they and rational [causes] are equal in necessitation, so they cannot be absent without a preventer by default, and hence the preventer is appreciated in the textually indicated cause by agreement. So the difference between them and rational [causes] is without substance. Rather, the truth in the answer is that the rational effective [cause] (which is the agent cause) is like the legal [effective cause] in that absence due to a preventer is permissible, even if absence in the complete [cause] is not permissible. Do you not see that wet wood does not burn from igniting fire due to moisture preventing burning? And the complete [cause] in legal [causes] is not permissible, and the permissive does not speak about it, but rather about the effective [cause]. And we are confident that you have guided yourself to understand that comparing the legal to the rational is a clear indication that the preventer intends by 'cause' the complete cause, so it is established and does not cease. And Allah knows best the intent of His noble servants. The preventers regarding derived causes said: If the derived [cause] were valid, then this absence would be due to a preventer, otherwise (i.e., if not due to a preventer but without preventer) there is no [causal] efficacy. And the preventer only exists after the cause; otherwise (i.e., if the cause does not exist with the preventer) then the absence of the ruling is due to the absence of the cause, not the preventer. So then the cause depends on the preventer, and it depends on the cause, so there is circularity. (And it was answered): That it is a circularity of concomitance, i.e., a mutual correlation between causality and preventiveness, and the dependency of one on the other is denied. (And it was repelled): That the intended meaning is not that each one depends on the other in reality, but rather that the knowledge of each depends on the knowledge of the other. And it is established that the intended meaning is that preventiveness (i.e., that something is a preventer) is not known except after [knowledge of] causal efficacy; otherwise, it is possible that the requirement is absent, so preventiveness is not known. And causal efficacy (at the time of absence) is not known except after knowledge of preventiveness, for absence without knowledge of the preventer causes hesitation about the efficacy of the effective [cause]. So then circularity is necessary. (And it may be answered): That presumption of causality is obtained from its paths without depending on knowledge of the preventer, and its continuity is dependent on the preventer, i.e., knowledge of it at the time of absence, for absence is suspicious except with the preventer. And the preventer is dependent on the initial presumption of causality, not on its continuity, so there is no circularity. I say: The preventer (i.e., presuming it in a place of absence) is dependent on presuming [causality] in it, otherwise it is possible that the absence of the ruling is due to the absence of the requirement. And presuming it in it is dependent on the preventer, i.e., on presuming it in it, because absence without a preventer is evidence of the absence of the requirement, and the paths do not give presumption of causality where the ruling is absent, even if they give it elsewhere, except when there is a preventer. So there is circularity. And it was also objected when presumption of causality is accompanied by knowledge of absence, such as if two poor people ask and he gives one and prevents the sinful one, then in that case the causality of poverty is not established except after presuming that sin is a preventer, and the paths alone are not sufficient. (And the correct answer): That what depends on causality and knowledge of it is actual preventiveness and presumption of it, and what it depends on is potential preventiveness and presumption of it, which is doubt that a thing is such that if it accompanies a motive, it prevents its requirement, whether this thing exists or not. Thus. The preventers of textually indicated [causes] said: The evidence of the derived [cause] from its paths necessitates presumption of it, and absence is suspicious due to the possibility of a preventer, so it can be a cause, and possibility of its absence [in that place] so it cannot be, so there is no conflict between its evidence and the evidence of its negation which is absence, because the first is preponderant. And it was answered that doubt in one of two opposites necessitates it in the other because it permits both sides equally. So your statement: 'Causality is presumed and its negation is doubted' is contradictory; rather, causality also becomes doubted. If you say: Then what is the meaning of their statement: 'Presumption does not go away with doubt'? He said: And as for the statement of the jurists 'Presumption does not rise with doubt', its meaning is that the ruling of the stronger (established) does not go away by the weaker arising in law, i.e., the Law has obligated acting according to the stronger, even if a weaker opposing arises, and its like is not possible here, because the discussion here is about the presumption itself: does it occur at the time of absence or not? (I say): It can be established thus: Absence in itself, disregarding the occurrence of something, is doubt-inducing. So when the possibility of a preventer is combined with the evidence of causality, causality becomes strongly presumed, due to the disappearance of the possibility of negation of requirement by its evidence, and the doubted becomes, by a preponderator, necessarily presumed. So the correct answer is that when alone, each of the evidence of derived [cause] and absence necessitates presumption for each of the cause and its negation. And when together, doubt occurs on both sides due to conflict between them, so we do not accept your statement that absence is doubt-inducing; rather, it is beneficial for negation of causality. And there is something in it, for the possibility of the existence of the preventer and its negation both stand equally, so absence in itself is doubt-inducing, so there is no room for denial unless one claims that the possibility of the absence of the preventer is remote due to the frequency of the absence of the ruling due to the absence of the requirement, and the principle is presumption of negation of causality. So understand. As for the textually indicated [cause], it does not accept invalidation, and absence of the ruling from it necessitates the invalidity of the general text that benefits the attachment of the ruling to it, for specifying the cause is like saying: 'Everything in which the cause exists, the ruling exists', unlike the derived [cause], for its evidence is conjunction, i.e., conjunction of the ruling with the absence of the preventer, so absence is permissible due to its possibility. And it was answered in al-Mukhtasar: If the text is definitive, then non-acceptance of specification is conceded, and there is no dispute in it; otherwise, it accepts specification and is appreciated.
Source pages p. 330

Commentary
This passage presents a detailed theological and methodological dispute in Hanafi usul al-fiqh regarding the nature of legal causes (ilal) in qiyas. The core issue is whether a cause, once identified, must always produce its legal effect, or whether the effect can be absent due to the presence of a preventer (mani'). The text opens by characterizing the dispute as semantic: both groups agree that the cause is valid in cases where the effect occurs, but they differ on attribution when the effect is absent. The preventers (mani'un) attribute absence to the lack of the cause, while the permissives (mujawwizun) attribute it to the preventer. The author of the commentary (Ibn Abd al-Shakur) critiques a previous commentator's view that the dispute is merely conceptual, asserting that the true fruit appears in answering objections. Three arguments from the preventers are then presented and answered. First, they argue that conflict between evidence of causality and evidence of invalidity leads to dropping the causality claim. The response is that absence is not evidence of invalidity unless there is no preventer; since we assume a preventer, the evidence remains. Second, they analogize legal causes to rational causes, claiming no specification is possible. The answer clarifies that rational causes are essential and inseparable from their effects, while legal causes are posited by the Lawgiver and can be overridden by a preventer. Hence, the analogy fails. The author then provides a deeper analysis, noting that even an effective cause can be blocked by a preventer, like wet wood not burning despite fire. Third, they raise a circularity objection: knowledge of causality depends on knowledge of preventiveness and vice versa. This is resolved by distinguishing between actual and potential preventiveness, and by noting that initial presumption of causality comes from textual paths independently. The passage concludes by distinguishing between textually indicated causes (علة منصوصة) and derived causes (مستنبطة). Textually indicated causes are definitive and cannot be overridden; derived causes are probabilistic and may be blocked by a preventer. The Hanafi position is that the effect of a cause can be absent due to a preventer, but the cause itself remains intact and is not invalidated.

Key Terms
العلة (al-illa): The legal cause in qiyas that is posited by the Lawgiver to necessitate a ruling, but which may be overridden by a preventer.
المانع (al-mani'): A preventer that blocks the effect of a cause, such that the ruling does not apply despite the cause being present.
تخصيص العلة (takhsis al-illa): The specification of a cause such that it does not apply in every instance, typically due to a preventer.
العلة المنصوصة (al-illa al-mansusa): A cause explicitly stated in the Quran or sunnah, which is definitive and cannot be overridden by a preventer.
العلة المستنبطة (al-illa al-mustanbata): A cause derived by juristic reasoning (ijtihad) and thus probabilistic; it can be overridden by a preventer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the key difference between a textually indicated cause (illa mansusa) and a derived cause (illa mustanbata) regarding specification by a preventer?
A) Both can be specified.
B) Neither can be specified.
C) The derived cause can be specified, but the textually indicated cause cannot if the text is definitive.
D) The textually indicated cause can be specified, but the derived cause cannot.

Key Takeaway
A legal cause in Hanafi usul can have its effect blocked by a preventer without being invalidated, and this principle preserves the flexibility of qiyas.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
advanced
12 min
Ta'addud al-'Ilal: Multiple Causes for One Ruling in Hanafi Usul
Analyzing arguments for and against multiple causes for one effect

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the arguments for and against multiple independent 'ilal producing a single hukm, and apply the Hanafi distinction between the type (naw') and the specific individual (shakhs).

Core Idea
Multiple independent causes can coexist for a type, but not for a single specific individual.

This passage from Fawatih al-Rahmūt tackles a foundational usul question: Can two or more independent effective causes (ilal) both be responsible for the same hukm? The debate involves objections from theologians, the example of riba across schools, and the distinction between the type and the individual. By studying this, you will understand the Hanafi position that multiplicity is permissible at the type level.

Guided Path
1
Understand the mutakallimun's objection
Theologians argue that if two causes each produce the same effect, they would produce two like effects (taslimat al-mithlayn). The author clarifies that the discussion assumes the effect is on a single specific individual, so each cause produces the same effect, not a like effect, avoiding duplication.
Focus on the distinction between like (mithl) and same (nafs).
2
Examine the riba dispute
The three schools (Hanafi, Shafi‘i, Maliki) propose different 'ilal for the prohibition of riba. The passage argues this dispute is about nullifying each other's causes, not preference, and it shows the discussion is about the type (naw‘i) because riba has many instances.
Note that the Hanafi cause is kayl + jins, Shafi‘i is ta‘m, Maliki is iqtitat.
3
Analyze al-Qadi's argument on textual independence
Al-Qadi holds that if a text states each cause is independent, we must accept it; otherwise, we rule by partiality (juz’iyya). The counter is that ruling by partiality without causality is also arbitrary.
The text debates whether partiality or causality is less arbitrary.
4
Assess the 'Aks position
Some restrict multiplicity to deduced causes (mustanbata) because they are probabilistic, while textual causes are definitive. The response: textual causes can also be probabilistic, and definitiveness does not negate multiplicity.
This shows that the debate crosses the line between textual and derived causes.
5
Synthesize the author's chosen view
After considering simultaneous causes, the author concludes that the whole group at once is the cause or one unspecified cause. The real dispute is about the type, not the individual. Thus, multiplicity is valid for different individuals of the same type.
The final investigation distinguishes literal and metaphorical independence.

‘Existing in the external world, for according to us, the group of theologians (mutakallimun), it is an external attribute subsisting in the world, so the similarity of two like things would be necessitated there. And even if it is conceded that knowledge does not exist in the external world but in the mind, as the philosophers hold, or it is a relational matter as appears from some words of Imam al-Razi, then there is no dispute about its being established in reality even if it is not called existence in the external world, so the similarity of two like things in reality would be necessitated, so reflect. The correct answer is that the assumed case here is the simultaneous occurrence (tawarud) on one specific individual, because the discussion is about it. Then each cause necessitates what the other necessitates, not its like, so that similarity of two like things would not be necessitated. But you may say: For the existence of the effect, it must depend on the existence of the cause which is effective in it. If one cause is sufficient, it has already effected the existence of the effect, so it does not depend on another. And if another is also assumed sufficient, then there must be another existence for it so that this other cause can effect it, and this existence is produced from it. Multiplicity of existence necessitates multiplicity of the individual, so similarity of two like things is inevitably necessitated, so reflect on it. And they said: Thirdly, they argued about the cause of the prohibition of riba: is it measurement (kayl) with genus (jins) as is our school, or food substance (ta'm) as is the school of al-Shafi‘i, or staple food (iqtitat) as is the view of Malik, by means of preference (tarjih) of one over the other. This is a branch of the validity of each and the necessity of the negation of multiplicity, otherwise each would be a cause and there would be no need for preference. This also indicates to you that the discussion is about the type (naw‘i) because riba is a type with many individuals under it. The answer was that they are engaged in nullification, for each proponent negates the other, not in preference, such that the validity of each is necessitated. And even if it is conceded that they are engaged in preference, then there is consensus on the unity of the cause here, and unity is not necessitated in every ruling. al-Qadi said: If the text stipulates the independence of each cause, acceptance is inevitable due to the obligation to follow the text. As for what is not stipulated in the text, we rule by partiality (juz’iyya) not independence, because ruling by causation (‘illiyya) with multiplicity independently without partiality is arbitrary (tahakkum), so it is invalid in non-textual cases. It was countered by the opposite, meaning that ruling by partiality without causation is also arbitrary, so it should not be ruled by it either. I say: In causation, there is a kind of nullification of the other, for if one alone were isolated, the other would have no role at all. This is not the case with partiality, because if isolated, it would need another to effect, so partiality is preferable. Reflect, it is subtle. But you may counter that in partiality there is nullification of independence and effect from each, and effect and independence belong only to the combination. So in causation, each is effective, and neither nullification has preference, so arbitrariness returns. Moreover, multiplicity is disadvantageous because it is rare and contrary to the norm, so partiality is preferable. The answer to al-Qadi’s argument is that independence for each is deduced rationally by there being a general relationship in one respect, so each cause is found without the other with the ruling, and it is known that the cause is not the combination but each is independent. It will not escape you that this does not apply to the specific individual, for a single specific ruling does not exist in two places with only one. So answering with this answer indicates to you that the discussion is about the type. Unless it is established that when each of the two causes is found with the type of ruling without the other, each is effective in the type. Then if both are found in a single locus, their effect in the type in that locus is necessitated, and the type in a single locus does not exist except in one specific individual, so multiplicity of causes for one specific effect is necessitated by proof, so it cannot be denied. So it is not arbitrary, reflect on it. Then the answer may be formulated as: when each is found without the other with the existence of the ruling, each is independent in isolation, and when together, the cause is the combination. The author (musannif) rejected this as a concession to al-Qadi’s demanded thesis. The fourth principle/qiyas for the Qadi’s demanded thesis. He says: He says partiality in the case of multiplicity, but the truth is that when each is independent in isolation, it is known that togetherness is not a condition for effect. And if it effects when together, then isolation is not a condition. So each cause absolutely, whether together or alone, is a cause when together independently, just as it was when alone, so understand. The ‘Aks (who permits multiplicity in deduced causes but not in textual ones) said: Textual causes are definitive (qat‘iyya), so the possibility of another is negated, thus no multiplicity, unlike deduced causes, which are probabilistic (zanniyya), and each may be preferred by its own proof, so multiplicity is permissible. The answer: first, denial of definitiveness, for textual stipulation of a cause may be probabilistic too. Second, after conceding definitiveness, negation of possibility is not contradictory. The Imam who permits rationally but forbids legally said: If it were not legally forbidden, it would occur customarily, even if rarely, in some rulings, and it has not occurred. If you say: It has occurred in the case of ritual impurity (hadath) with multiple causes, he answers: What is established by the causes of ritual impurity is multiple, so much so that it is said: If he intends to remove one of his impurities, the other is not removed. If it were one, he would not need multiple intentions. This is based on the view of those who maintain that intention is required in removing impurity. The answer: denial of non-occurrence, but it is possible that it is the material of occurrence. And the permissibility of multiplicity as permitted is not sufficient for him, because he is providing evidence, so he must prove it. Then those who affirm multiplicity agree that the effect is by the first in order of existence of the causes. If you say: Imam Abu Hanifa said: For one who swore ‘I will not perform wudu from a nosebleed’, then he urinated, then had a nosebleed, then performed wudu, he breaks his oath. This indicates that wudu is by the nosebleed even though it is later. He said: What is reported from Abu Hanifa about one who swore not to perform wudu from a nosebleed, then urinated, then had a nosebleed, then performed wudu, he breaks his oath – it is based on custom (‘urf), for it is said in custom that he performed wudu from the nosebleed, and oaths are based on custom; it does not necessitate that impurity is actually established from the nosebleed. As for simultaneous occurrence (ma‘iyya), when the causes exist together, it is said that the cause is the combination and each is a part; and it is said that it is one unspecified cause, which is the truth. The chosen view (mukhtar) according to the author (musannif) is that the whole group at once is the cause. It will not be hidden from you that according to the first two views, multiplicity of causes and their simultaneous occurrence on one specific individual is not established, for the occurrence is either sequential – then the first cause, so no multiplicity – or simultaneous – then no multiplicity either, because the cause is the combination or the common denominator, so counting them as multiplicity is invalid. Rather, the truth is what we previously indicated: that the discussion from the beginning was about the type, so the prevention of the multiplicity of its causes is not valid. The chosen view according to the majority is that it is permissible. Then those who permit differ: if two causes occur together, they occur simultaneously on one specific individual as in the latter view; rather, the cause for the specific individual is the combination or the common denominator. Thus the dispute should be formulated, and then most of the earlier talk is invalid, so understand. And it is established for us that if it is not at once, then either by combination with each being a part, or by one, both of which are invalid, because partiality contradicts independence, and each is assumed independent. And in the unity there is arbitrariness. I say: ‘Independence’ (istiqlal) may be used to mean being established by it and not by another, as earlier. This meaning is literal in isolation, i.e., when one of them alone [is present], and metaphorical in togetherness, i.e., when each is present at once, because it is established only on the assumption of isolation. And ‘independence’ may be used to mean being established by itself, i.e., its requiring the existence does not depend on something else, as in the earlier examples. This does not negate being established by something else as well. The investigation is that this and the first are equal in the independent for a specific individual, for existence by a cause is not conceivable except by needing it, and if it needs it, it cannot be realized without it. This is what is meant here, because it is simultaneous occurrence (tawarud).’
Source pages p. 336

Commentary
This passage from Fawatih al-Rahmut analyzes the debate on whether multiple independent 'ilal (causes) can produce a single hukm (ruling). The author presents several arguments and schools of thought. The first part addresses the mutakallimun's objection that multiple causes would necessitate the existence of multiple effects or similarity of two like things. The author clarifies that the discussion is about the effect on a single specific individual (wahid shakhsi), not the type. The third argument concerns the cause of riba: the Hanafi school holds that it is measurement (kayl) with genus (jins), the Shafi‘i holds it is food substance (ta‘m), and the Maliki holds it is staple food (iqtitat). The text argues that this dispute is actually about determining which one is the true cause, and it is a branch of the possibility of multiple causes. The response is that the disputants are not seeking preference but nullification of each other’s claims. The Qadi’s argument about textual independence is countered by showing that when causes are textually independent, they must be accepted; otherwise, we can only rule by partiality. The 'Aks (who restricts multiplicity to deduced causes only) is refuted by noting that textual causes may also be probabilistic. The example from Imam Abu Hanifa about an oath regarding wudu from a nosebleed is explained as based on custom ('urf), not on literal multiple causes. Finally, the analysis of simultaneous causes (ma‘iyya) leads to the author's chosen view: the whole group at once is the cause, or one unspecified cause. The author concludes that the dispute should be understood as about the type (naw‘), not the specific individual, and that multiplicity is permissible at the type level.

Key Terms
العلة (al-‘illa): The effective cause in qiyas that necessitates a ruling.
التوارد (al-tawarud): The simultaneous occurrence of two or more causes on a single effect.
الاستقلال (al-istiqlal): Independence of a cause in producing the effect; the author distinguishes between literal independence (in isolation) and metaphorical independence (when combined).
الواحد الشخصي (al-wahid al-shakhsi): A specific individual instance of a ruling or object.
الواحد النوعي (al-wahid al-naw‘i): A type or category under which many specific individuals fall.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling of Imam Abu Hanifa on the oath example regarding wudu from a nosebleed?
A) It proves that the nosebleed is the actual cause of impurity.
B) It is based on custom ('urf) and does not establish real causation.
C) It shows that multiple causes can occur for a single individual.
D) It contradicts the principle of ta'addud al-'ilal.

Key Takeaway
Multiple independent causes can be valid for different individuals of the same type of ruling, but not for a single specific individual; the author's preferred view is that the whole group at once is the cause when simultaneous.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
advanced
12 min
The Dispute on Multiple Independent Causes for One Ruling
Explains the debate on whether multiple independent causes can coexist for one ruling

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on multiple independent causes for one ruling, distinguish between the different views (partiality, aggregate, unspecified one), and apply the concept of independence (istiqlal) to the case of Imam Abu Hanifa's fatwa on custom.

Core Idea
Multiple independent causes for a single ruling are permissible; the dispute is about their coexistence, not their possibility.

In this passage, the author of Fawatih al-Rahamut delves into a classic usul debate: can two or more independent causes (ilal) produce the same ruling (hukm) for a single instance? The discussion unpacks the concept of independence (istiqlal), the distinction between textually-stated and derived causes, and the crucial role of custom ('urf) in determining causality. You will learn the Hanafi position, the objections from the opposing school, and the resolution through the concepts of type-unity versus individual-unity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core issue and main positions
Pinpoint the central question: Is it permissible for multiple independent causes (each capable of producing the ruling on its own) to coexist and both be considered causes for the same ruling? List the three main positions: (1) those who permit, (2) those who permit only for derived causes, and (3) those who permit rationally but prevent legally.
The passage also mentions proponents of 'partiality' (juz'iyyah) who say each is a part of a combined cause.
2
Understand the key terms
Define the essential terms: ta'addud al-'ilal (multiplicity of causes), tawarrud (concurrence), istiqlal (independence), and juz'iyyah (partiality). Understand the two meanings of istiqlal: (a) established by it and not by another (literal in individuality, metaphorical in combination), and (b) the requirement does not depend on others (the meaning relevant to the dispute).
The text emphasizes that the second meaning is what is meant because it allows for independence even when combined.
3
Analyze the debate between permissibilists and preventers
Examine the preventer's argument that multiple independent causes would lead to conjunction of two likes (mithlayn) or arbitrariness (tahakkum) in choosing one cause. Then see the Hanafi response: the causes concur on a single effect, so there is no conjunction of two likes; the effect is one. The cause may be the aggregate or the common factor when they coexist.
The passage refines the dispute: it is about the effect being one in type (naw') not one individual instance (shakhs?).
4
Examine the fatwa of Imam Abu Hanifa on the oath concerning nosebleed
Consider the case: a person swears 'I will not perform wudu from nosebleed', then urinates and has a nosebleed, then performs wudu. Imam Abu Hanifa rules that he breaks his oath. Explain that this is based on custom ('urf), not on actual causality. It does not prove that the nosebleed is the actual cause of hadath; rather, custom dictates that the oath is broken.
This fatwa is used to illustrate that custom can indicate a cause, but it does not prove multiplicity of actual causes in reality.
5
Resolve the dispute by distinguishing between type-unity and individual-unity
Understand the final position: the discussion is about a ruling that is one in type (i.e., the same legal category), not one specific instance. Multiple causes can exist for the same type of ruling. When they concur on a single individual instance, the cause is either the aggregate or the common factor. The 'chosen view' according to the author is 'all at once' (al-kull daf'atan) as the cause.
This resolution avoids the objections of contradiction and arbitrariness.

The fourth principle: analogy (qiyas) [statement] of the desired judge, for he says with partiality in the case of multiplicity, but the truth is that each, when it is independent individually, it is known that combination is not a condition for effect, and if it is effective upon combination, then individuality is not a condition. So every cause absolutely, whether combined or individual, is a cause upon combination independently as it was upon individuality. So understand. (The contradictor) who permits multiplicity in the derived [causes] but not the textually stated. He said: The textually stated is definitive (qat'i), so the possibility of another is negated, so no multiplicity, unlike the derived, for they are conjectural (zanni). (And perhaps each is preponderated by its evidence, so multiplicity is permissible). (The answer) first: (rejection of definitiveness) for the textual statement of the cause may also be conjectural. And second: after conceding definitiveness from it, (the negation of possibility) of another, for there is no contradiction. (The Imam who permits rationally but prevents legally said: If it were not prevented legally, it would occur usually, rather rarely in some rulings, and it did not occur. (You may say: it has occurred in the hadath [ritual impurity] with various causes. He replied and said: What is established by the causes of hadath is multiple, such that it is said: if one intends to remove one of his hadaths, the other is not removed. And if it were one, he would not need multiple intentions. And this is based on the opinion of one who claims the condition of intention in removing hadath. And the answer is the denial of non-occurrence; rather it is possible that the material of occurrence is hadath and the permission of multiplicity. As he permitted (it does not suffice him because he is a seeker of proof, so he must prove it. Then the multipliers agreed that it) i.e. the effect (is by the first in order in the existence of the causes. (You may say: Imam Abu Hanifa said: regarding one who swore 'I will not perform wudu from nosebleed', then he urinated, then had a nosebleed, then performed wudu, he breaks his oath. This indicates that the wudu is due to the nosebleed despite it being later. He said: What is reported from Abu Hanifa about one who swore 'I will not perform wudu from nosebleed', then he urinated, then had a nosebleed, then performed wudu, he breaks his oath; it is based on custom ('urf). For it is said customarily that he performed wudu due to the nosebleed, and oaths are based on custom. And it does not entail that the hadath is actually established from the nosebleed. (As for simultaneity (ma'iyyah)) meaning when the causes exist together, (it is said) the cause (is the aggregate and each) of the causes (is a part). And (it is said: one unspecified) and it is the truth. And (the chosen view) according to the author (is all at once) is a cause. And it is not hidden to you that according to the first two schools, multiplicity of causes and their concurrence on one individual instance is not established. For concurrence is either successive: then the first cause [applies] so no multiplicity; or simultaneous: then no multiplicity either, because the cause is the aggregate or the common factor. So counting them as multiplicity is not valid. Rather the truth is what we previously indicated, that the discussion was from the beginning about the one in type (type-unity). So one prevented the collection of multiplicity of its causes. And the chosen view according to the majority is that it is permissible. Then the permiters differed: when two causes exist together, they are concurrent on the one individual instance as in the last school; rather the cause for the individual instance is the aggregate or the common factor. Thus should the dispute be formulated. And then most of the talk and going back and forth that passed is nullified. So understand. (And it is established for us) if it is not at once, then either by the aggregate with each being a part, or by one, and both are invalid since partiality contradicts independence) and each was assumed independent. (And in singling out (al-wahda) there is arbitrariness (tahakkum). I say: Independence (istiqlal) may be applied to existence by it and not by another, as passed. And this meaning is literal in individuality, i.e. when one of them is alone, and metaphorical in combination, i.e. when each exists at once, because it is established only on the assumption of individuality. (And it may be applied) independence (to existence by itself, i.e. its requirement does not depend on others, as in the preceding examples. And this does not negate existence by others as well. And the verification is that this and the first are equal in the independent [causes] for the individual instance. For existence by a cause is only conceivable by need of it, and if it needs, it cannot be realized without it. (And that is what is meant here, because the concurrence is complete (taman) of that which is disputed by verification. (And if not) i.e. if the concurrence of the independent [causes] is not with this meaning but with the first meaning, (then would follow concurrence of incomplete causes in this personal instance. For what is established by individuality, i.e. by the single cause initially, (is another person) not what is established by the combined. So each is not independent in this sense; rather the aggregate is independent and each cause is incomplete. So then there is no concurrence of the independent cause and no dispute in it as passed. And from here, i.e. because the disputed point is the concurrence of that whose requirement does not depend on others, the preventers imposed the conjunction of the two likes (mithlayn), contrary to if each is independent upon individuality and part of the independent upon combination, there is no basis for conjunction of the two likes. And then, i.e. when it is verified that the disputed is the concurrence of that which is complete in requirement, what was objected is repelled: that if he meant independence for each upon individuality, it is not useful for you, because partiality upon combination does not contradict independence upon individuality. And if he meant upon combination, i.e. independence upon combination, then the very disputed point is the contradiction; there is no impossibility in it. The way of repulsion is that what is meant by independence is being in the requirement, and that meaning is established by what is established for it always, whether combined or individual. So reflect. And you may object with another way: that the conceded amount is the requirement of establishing the type of ruling in the absence of a preventer, established for each cause individually, not forever. And this does not necessitate the requirement of establishing the individual ruling for each upon combination. So reflect. Then I say: Perhaps one may prevent the arbitrariness on the assumption of unity (al-wahda) as is the last part of the second. (And arbitrariness would only be if the unity were specific (mu'ayyana); rather the unity is not specific. For it is possible that the cause in reality is one of them unspecified. (And the answer is that the discussion here is when there is no common factor between them that is the cause in reality, as in the non-existence of the two parts, for the nature of the non-existence of the part) of the cause (with any determination it is the cause) in reality. (Then if what is meant by the specified non-specific (al-mu'ayyana la bi-'aynih) is the ambiguous (al-mubham), i.e. the one from the determined things, ambiguous, existing with the existence of each, then its non-determination would follow, while the known is determined, and the mind is averse to permitting it. So it must be intended as a specific particular, whichever it is. And in it there is arbitrariness, so ponder.) And this answer is nothing, for assuming the lack of a common factor is an impossible assumption. And at least [there is] one of them not specifically. And aversion to causality of the ambiguous for the determined is only for the real maker (al-'illa al-ja'ila) not for the effect whose effect is by consideration of the Law and its institution. How not? For the wise One does not forbid adjudging a type of ruling, and considering the effect of multiple descriptions on this type, and the effect of one of them not specifically on a particular instance of its instances. As for considering the effect of each on its particular instance, it is impossible because it leads to contradiction as you have known. So understand. The proponents of partiality (ashab al-juz'iyya) said: If each were a cause, conjunction of the two likes would be necessary as passed; or one, then arbitrariness, i.e. if one of them were a cause, arbitrariness is necessary, and both are invalid. So partiality is determined. (I say) in the answer: we do not accept the necessity of conjunction of the two likes, for the two causes concur on one not two things, and meaning upon each: each derives from each. So the effect is one, with no duality at all. (And its standard) i.e. the standard of this derivation (is the correctness of the letter fa') such that it is said: the cause is found so the ruling is found. And it is correct here in each relative to it by independence, for it is correct in the case of urinating and nosebleeding together: he urinated so he became impure, and had a nosebleed so he became impure. This is so. But you may say: The meaning of the fa' cannot be interposed here except due to each encompassing the one unspecified which is that which is derived from it in reality not specifically. How not? About 6.

Commentary
This passage is a detailed usul dispute about the possibility of multiple independent causes (ta'addud al-'ilal) for a single ruling (hukm). It presents several positions: (1) Some permit multiple causes only for derived (mustanbatah) causes but not for textually stated (mansusah) causes, because the latter are definitive. (2) An 'Imam' permits rationally but prevents legally, arguing that if it were not legally prevented, it would occur. (3) The majority of Hanafis choose permissibility. The discussion distinguishes between successive and simultaneous concurrence of causes. A key example is the fatwa of Imam Abu Hanifa about a person who swore not to perform wudu from nosebleed, then urinated and had a nosebleed and performed wudu, and broke his oath. The text explains that this ruling is based on custom ('urf) and does not prove actual multiplicity of causes in reality. The concept of independence (istiqlal) is refined: a cause is independent if its requirement does not depend on others, and this quality remains whether it exists alone or with others. The passage refutes the objection that multiple causes would lead to conjunction of opposites (similar to two likes). The proper formulation of the dispute is that it concerns a ruling that is one in type (wahid bi al-naw'), not one individual instance (wahid shakhsi). The teacher should guide students to understand that the Hanafi position allows multiple causes for the same type of ruling, and the debate is about the mechanism when they concur.

Key Terms
تعدد العلل (ta'addud al-'ilal): إمكانية وجود أكثر من علة مستقلة لحكم واحد
توارد (tawarrud): ورود علتين أو أكثر على محل واحد في وقت واحد أو على التعاقب
استقلال (istiqlal): كون العلة تامة الاقتضاء بنفسها لا بغيرها
جزئية (juz'iyyah): كون كل علة جزءاً من علة مجموعية عند الاجتماع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, why does the fatwa of Imam Abu Hanifa about the person who swore not to do wudu from nosebleed then urinated and had a nosebleed and did wudu, and broke his oath, not prove that the cause is actually the nosebleed?
A) Because the cause is the urination alone.
B) Because the ruling is based on custom ('urf), not on the actual reality of the cause.
C) Because he urinated first.
D) Because he intended to break the oath.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school allows multiple independent causes for one ruling (type-unity), as long as each cause is inherently capable of producing the ruling, and custom may indicate a cause even if not the actual one.

Condition: The Derived Cause Must Not Oppose a Companion's Saying
States a condition for derived cause validity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition that a derived illa must not oppose a Companion's saying and explain its basis in Hanafi usul.

Core Idea
A derived illa is invalid if it contradicts a Companion's saying—when the saying is given precedence over qiyas.

This passage presents a crucial condition for the validity of a derived cause (illa mustanbata): it must not contradict the statement of a Companion (qawl al-sahabi) for those scholars who prioritize the Companion's saying over analogical reasoning. The author refers to an earlier discussion, indicating that the weight of Companion sayings is a settled point in Hanafi usul. This condition reflects the school's integration of multiple sources of law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Condition
Read the Arabic: 'آن لا تخالف قول صحابي'. The condition states that the derived illa must not oppose a Companion's saying.
This is one of the conditions for a valid illa.
2
Understand the Qualification
The phrase 'عند من قدمه' (according to those who give it precedence) indicates that this condition applies only to scholars who prioritize qawl al-sahabi over qiyas. Not all schools agree.
This qualifies the scope of the condition.
3
Relate to the Source Hierarchy
In Hanafi usul, qawl al-sahabi is considered a source of law that can override qiyas. Therefore, a derived illa that contradicts it is invalid.
The earlier discussion (qad taqaddam) likely covered the authority of Companion sayings.
4
Recall the Earlier Discussion
The author says 'وقد تقدم تحقيقه' (its verification has already preceded). This reminds students that the weight of qawl al-sahabi was already established, so only a brief reminder is needed here.
This condition builds on prior knowledge.

Source Text

(و) منها أن لا تخالف قول صحابي عند من قدمه) على القياس (وقد تقدم تحقيقه
And among them: that it not oppose the saying of a Companion according to those who give precedence to it over analogy, and its verification has already preceded.
Source pages p. 341

Commentary
This is a condition for the derived cause: it must not contradict a statement of a Companion (qawl al-sahabi) for those scholars who prioritize the Companion's saying over qiyas. The author mentions that the detailed discussion of this issue has already occurred earlier in the text, so only a brief reminder is given here. This condition reflects the Hanafi view that a Companion's saying is a source of law that can outweigh analogy.

Key Terms
قول الصحابي (qawl al-sahabi): A statement of a Companion of the Prophet, considered a source of law in some circumstances

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In Hanafi usul, what happens if a derived 'illa contradicts a Companion's saying?
A) The 'illa is invalid because the Companion's saying takes precedence
B) The 'illa remains valid if supported by qiyas
C) The Companion's saying is always abandoned
D) Both are considered equally

Key Takeaway
A derived cause is invalid if it opposes a Companion's saying for those who give precedence to such sayings over qiyas.

Debate on Necessity of Proof for 'Illah in Qiyas
Dispute about requiring a separate proof for 'illah before qiyas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on requiring a separate proof for 'illah and evaluate the opposing arguments using the maslak concept.

Core Idea
The maslak itself establishes the 'illah; a separate proof is unnecessary and would block qiyas.

In Hanafi usul, the method (maslak) for identifying the 'illah is considered sufficient. Here, we examine a dispute about whether a separate proof is needed to confirm that a text's ruling is based on an 'illah before using it for qiyas. The passage defends the Hanafi view that requiring such proof would render most qiyas impossible.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute
Recognize that the controversy centers on whether a separate dalil is necessary to establish that a text is 'illah before applying qiyas.
The fourth group argues yes; the Hanafis say no.
2
State the Hanafi position and its proofs
The Hanafi position relies on: absence of such practice among Companions, analogy with following the Prophet's actions, and the sufficiency of the maslak.
Sadr al-Shariah's statement is key: requiring proof blocks qiyas.
3
Analyze the fourth group's objection
They argue that since some texts are not 'illah, a proof is needed to discriminate. The Hanafi response: the maslak removes doubt; certainty not required.
The example of exit from two passages illustrates the point.
4
Understand the role of maslak
The maslak (textual indication, consensus, etc.) directly identifies the 'illah without extra proof. This is the core of the Hanafi argument.
Maslak makes the 'illah known and suffices for qiyas.
5
Evaluate the implication
Requiring a separate proof would close the door of qiyas in most texts, which is contrary to the Lawgiver's intent.

فقال: بأن الأوصاف في المنصوص عليه كثيرة، فالتعليل إما بالكل وهو باطل، لأن الكل مختص به لا يتجاوز غيره، وإما بكل واحد وهو موجب للتناقض، وإما بواحد ما وهو مجهول لا يصلح لابتناء الحكم، وإما بمعين وعليته مشكوكة فبطل الأقسام فوجب التوقف، قلنا: التعليل بواحد معين، والشك ممنوع، بل المسلك معرف فافهم واحتج الفريق الثاني بأن الشارع لما جعل القياس حجة صار الأصل في النصوص كلها التعليل لعدم كون الدلائل فارقة، فالنصوص كلها معلولة بوصف صالح، فالتعليل إما بجميع الأوصاف الموجودة في الأصل وهو سادّ لباب القياس لاختصاصها به وإما بواحد ما وهو مجهول وإما بواحد معين وهو الترجيح من غير مرجح، وإما بكل وهو الشق الباقي، فإذا كل علة وصالح لأن يعلل به ويقاس عليه غيره إلا لمانع من التعارض وغيره وهذا كما أن خبر الواحد العدل مطلقاً حجة إلا لمانع من التعارض ومخالفة القاطعة وغير ذلك، قلنا : سلمنا أن الأصل في النصوص التعليل لكن التعليل بمعنى صالح يكون هو منبئاً عن حكمة الحكم فلا ترجيح من غير مرجح، ويعرف بمسلكه، واحتج الفريق الرابع بأن من النصوص ما هو معلول وما هو ليس كذلك، الاحتمال في كل نص أنه مما ليس معلولاً، أصلاً، فلا بد من دليل دال عليه إجمالاً، ولا تكفي الأصالة للالتزام فإنما تكفي للدفع؟ قلنا: إن أرادوا بالاحتمال احتمالاً يكون في المشكوك فممنوع وكيف لا وإذا ذل المسلك على أنه معلول بالعلة الفلانية فقد لزم كونه معلولاً، وزال هذا الاحتمال وإن أرادوا مطلق الاحتمال فلا يضر إذ القطع بالعلية ليس بشرط في العمل بالقياس ونوقضوا بتعليل نص، كون الخارج من أحد السبيلين حدثاً مع عدم قيام الدليل على العلية وأجابوا أنه أجمع على أن مقطوع السرة التي يخرج منها البول والغائط يصير محدثاً، فعلم أن الحكم غير مقتصر على الخروج، بل معلل بعلة متعدية فافهم واحتج الفريق الثالث أولاً بأنه لم ينقل في مناظرات الصحابة رضوان الله تعالى عليهم ولا في مناظرات التابعين طلب الدليل أوّلاً على كون النص معلولاً، ولو كان شرطاً لوقع أحياناً فتفكر واحتجوا ثانياً بأن أفعال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم بعضها مخصوص والبعض الآخر، وهو الأكثر غير مختص والتأسي به صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم واجب مع احتمال كونه من الخواص كذا هذا، فإن الأصل التعليل فيجب أخذه إلا إذا دل دليل على الاختصاص بالمنصوص، أجاب الفريق الرابع بأنه فرق بين الاقتداء وبين ما نحن فيه، فإن رسالته عليه وآله وأصحابه الصلاة والسلام مقطوع من غير شبهة، فلا يضر طريان شبهة الاختصاص بالعمل والاقتداء بأفعاله وأما ههنا فالنصوص نوعان : منها معلولة، فيجوز طلب العلة، ومنها غير معلولة فلا يجوز، فلا بد من دليل مميز بين النوعين، وأن النص المبتلى به من أي نوع هو، وهذا الجواب غير شاف فإنه هب أن الرسالة قطعية من غير شبهة، لكنها غير موجبة للاقتداء بأفعاله بل الأفعال نوعان : منها مختصة ومنها غير مختصة كما أن النصوص نوعان والعمل والاقتداء هناك لأصالة عدم الاختصاص، فكذا ههنا الأصالة للتعليل ،موجودة فيجب طلب العلة لهذه الأصالة وليس هذا من قبيل العمل بالاستصحاب بل الشرع جعل النصوص معلولة ليقاس عليها ،غيرها واحتجوا ثالثاً بأن المسلك لما دل على أنه معلول بعلة معينة، فبهذا علمنا أنه ليس من النوع الغير المعلل، فلا يحتاج إلى إقامة الدليل من النصوص المعللة بل يكاد يكون فضلاً ولغواً، فالأصالة كافية للطلب والنظر أولاً، على أنه في تعيين العلة فإن دل على التعيين فقد لزم وثبت إنه معلل وإلا لا تعليل فافهم؛ ولنعم ما قال صدر الشريعة إن اشتراط إقامة هذا الدليل أولاً مما يسد باب القياس في أكثر النصوص فافهم
He said: Because the attributes in the text are many, so making 'illah either by the whole (which is invalid because the whole is specific to it and does not extend to others), or by each one (which leads to contradiction), or by an unspecified one (which is unknown and not suitable for building a ruling), or by a specified one with its causality doubted (so the divisions are invalid and one must stop). We said: The 'illah is by one specified one, and doubt is denied; rather, the maslak makes it known, so understand. And the second group argued: Because the Lawgiver made qiyas a proof, the original principle in all texts is that they are subject to 'illah, since there are no differentiating indications. So all texts have a suitable 'illah. Making 'illah either by all attributes existing in the original (which blocks the door of qiyas because they are specific to it), or by one unspecified (which is unknown), or by one specified (which is a preponderance without a preponderant), or by each (which is the remaining branch). So every 'illah is suitable for deriving 'illah and for analogy to others, except for a prevening cause such as conflict etc. This is like that a single just narrator is an absolute proof except for a prevening cause like conflict and opposition to a definitive, etc. We said: We accept that the original principle in texts is 'illah, but making 'illah by a meaning that is suitable and indicative of the wisdom of the ruling, so there is no preponderance without a preponderant, and it is known by its maslak. And the fourth group argued: Among texts, some are 'illah and some are not. The probability in every text is that it is not 'illah at all, so there must be a proof indicating it in general, and the presumption is not sufficient for commitment; rather it suffices for repulsion? We said: If they mean a probability that exists in the doubtful, it is denied. How so? When the maslak indicates that it is 'illah by such-and-such 'illah, then its being 'illah is established and this probability is removed. If they mean absolute probability, it does not harm, because certainty in causality is not a condition for acting on qiyas. They were refuted by the ruling that the exit from one of the two passages is an hadath, without proof of causality. They answered: There is consensus that one whose navel is cut and urine and feces come out becomes hadath, so it is known that the ruling is not confined to exiting, but is 'illah by a transitory cause. So understand. And the third group argued first: Because it is not reported in the debates of the Companions or the Followers that they demanded proof first for a text being 'illah; if it were a condition, it would have occurred sometimes. So reflect. And they argued second: Because the actions of the Messenger of Allah and his Companions are some specific, and others (the majority) are not specific, and following him is obligatory, despite the probability that it is from his special characteristics. Similarly here, the original principle is 'illah, so it must be taken, unless the proof indicates specificity. The fourth group answered: There is a difference between following and what we are discussing. His messengership is certain without doubt, so the probability of specificity does not harm acting and following his actions. Here, texts are two types: some are 'illah, so seeking the 'illah is permissible, and some are not 'illah, so it is not permissible. Therefore, there must be a proof distinguishing between the two types, and identifying which type the current text belongs to. This answer is not sufficient, for even if the messengership is certain without doubt, it does not necessitate following his actions; rather actions are two types: specific and non-specific. Similarly, texts are two types, and acting and following there is based on the presumption of non-specificity. So here the presumption of 'illah exists, so seeking the 'illah is obligatory due to this presumption. This is not like acting on istishab; rather the Lawgiver made texts 'illah so that other things can be analogized to them. And they argued third: Because when the maslak indicates that it is 'illah by a specific 'illah, then we know that it is not from the type of non-'illah, so there is no need to establish proof from the 'illah texts; it is almost superfluous and redundant. The presumption is sufficient for seeking and examining. First, regarding the specification of the 'illah: if it indicates the specification, then it is established that it is 'illah, otherwise not. So understand. And excellently said Sadr al-Shariah: 'Requiring this proof first blocks the door of qiyas in most texts.' So understand.
Source pages p. 346

Commentary
This passage presents a detailed usul dispute about whether one must first establish that a textual ruling is based on an 'illah (cause) before using it in qiyas (analogy). The text begins by reporting the argument of the first group (likely the opposition) that the attributes in the text are many, and any attempt to identify the 'illah leads to contradictions or unknowns, so one must stop. The responding speaker (the Hanafi position using 'قلنا') refutes this, asserting that the 'illah is a single specified one known through the maslak (method of 'illah). Then the second group argues that since the Lawgiver made qiyas a proof, all texts are presumed to have a suitable 'illah. The response clarifies that the 'illah must be a meaning indicative of the wisdom of the ruling. The fourth group objects that texts may be non-illah, so a proof is needed. The response counters that once the maslak indicates the 'illah, the probability is removed, and certainty is not required for qiyas, citing the case of exit from the two passages as an 'illah without explicit proof. The third group presents three proofs for not requiring a separate proof: absence of such practice among the Companions, analogy with actions of the Prophet (following is obligatory despite possible specificity), and the sufficiency of the maslak itself. The fourth group's rebuttals are answered. The passage concludes with approval of Sadr al-Shariah's statement that requiring such a proof would block qiyas in most texts. This section underscores the Hanafi approach that the maslak (whether text or consensus) directly indicates the 'illah, and a separate proof is unnecessary.

Key Terms
مسلك (maslak): The method or proof for identifying the 'illah of a ruling, such as nass (text) or ijma' (consensus).
علة (illah): The effective cause or attribute in the original case that justifies extending the ruling via qiyas.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What was the key objection of the fourth group against not requiring a separate proof for 'illah?
A) That it leads to an infinite regress of proofs.
B) That texts may not be 'illah at all, so a discriminating proof is needed.
C) That qiyas itself would become invalid without such proof.
D) That it contradicts a specific hadith.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, the maslak itself establishes the 'illah, and requiring a separate proof would block the door of qiyas in most texts.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
advanced
10 min
Ima' from Description or Ruling Alone
Usul dispute over ima' from description vs ruling

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a text mentioning only a description or only a ruling is considered ima' according to the Hanafi usul, and evaluate the three madhhabs on this issue.

Core Idea
Mentioning only the description (wasf) is ima'; mentioning only the ruling (hukm) is not.

This passage investigates a nuanced question in usul al-fiqh: when a legal text mentions only the causal description or only the ruling, can either be considered an indirect indication (ima') of the cause ('illah)? The author presents three scholarly positions, critiques their logic, and ultimately favors the Hanafi view that the description alone suffices for ima'. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly identifying textually indicated causes from the Quran and Sunnah.

Guided Path
1
Define ima' and its role
Ima' is an indirect indication of causality in a text, as opposed to explicit (sarih) statement. Recognize that ima' can co-exist with explicit indication.
The author gives examples like the hadith about dates where the word 'idha' is explicit and context provides ima'.
2
Identify the three madhhabs
State the three positions: (1) Both description alone and ruling alone are ima'. (2) Neither is ima'. (3) Only description alone is ima' (the preferred view).
The Hanafi preferred position is the third.
3
Analyze the Hanafi dalil
Examine the hadith examples used to support the third madhhab: the prohibition of judging while angry (description = anger, ruling = prohibition). The juxtaposition suggests causality.
The author notes that tanqih al-manat refines the cause to 'busyness of heart'.
4
Examine the critique of the third madhhab
The author raises an objection: even if mentioning the description intellectually implies the ruling, ima' requires actual or virtual verbal articulation of both. Causality must be established independently.
This critique shows the preferred position is not without difficulty.
5
Determine the practical thamarah
The thamarah (practical difference) is that a cause derived solely from a description in a text (e.g., 'Allah permitted trade') is considered textually indicated and thus stronger than a cause inferred without any textual hint.
A ruling alone (e.g., 'Alcohol is prohibited') does not give a textually indicated cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإيماء معطوف على قوله صريح على العلية بالقرينة وقد يجتمع الإيماء والصريح كما قال صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم لما سئل عن جواز بيع الرطب بالتمر أينقص إذا جف؟ قالوا نعم؟ قال: «فلا إذا» رواه الشيخان، ونقل التكلم عليه عن الإمام أبي حنيفة، فإنه صريح بكلمة إذا وإيماء أيضاً، فإنه لولاها لكانت الدلالة على العلية تامة باقية كما كان وإلا لما كان لمقارنة قوله صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم لا بقولهم نعم معنى فمنه الوقوع موقع الجواب، كقوله صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم (للأعرابي) حين قال: هلكت وأهلكت وقعت على فراشي نهار رمضان (أعتق رقبة) وقد مر تخريجه ولو لم تكن جناية الأعرابي علة لما كان للجواب معنى (و) قوله صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم (لابن مسعود) حين سأل عن التوضئ بنبيذ التمر «تمرة طيبة وماء طهور واختلف في تصحيحه وشرحه في فتح القدير فلو لم يكن بقاء اسم الماء علة لجواز التوضئ واختلاط الشيء الطاهر غير مانع كان بعيداً (ومنه مقارنة الوصف بالحكم) فإنها تشعر بالعلية (مثل) قوله صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم : (لا يقض القاضي وهو غضبان) رواه البخاري : ولفظه لا يقضين حكم بين اثنين وهو غضبان فهذا يومىء إلى أن العلة لوجوب الاجتناب عن القضاء الغضب ثم تنقيح المناط يدل على أنها شغل القلب (وهذا) أي قرآن الوصف بالحكم (إيماء) بالاتفاق فإن ذكر الوصف فقط دون الحكم كأحل الله البيع فذكر الحل وهو علة الصحة (أو) ذكر (الحكم فقط دون الوصف: (نحو: حرمت الخمر) فالمذكور الحرمة، وهو الحكم دون الوصف وهو الشدّة المطربة، ومنه أكثر العلل المستنبطة ففي كونهما إيماءُ فيقدّم على العلة المستنبطة بلا إيماء) لكون المنصوصة كذلك (مذاهب الأول كلاهما (نعم) كذلك لكفاية ذكر أحدهما للإيماء، وفيه ما فيه (و) الثاني (كلاهما لا) إيماء فيهما إذ لا بد من ذكرهما لأنه بالقرآن يومىء إلى العلية فتأمل فيه (و) الثالث (الأول) وهو ما ذكر فيه الوصف (إيماء دون الثاني وهو ما ذكر فيه الحكم (وهو الأشبه) بالصواب (لأن الاقتران بالذكر) إيماء البتة وذكر الملزوم ذكر اللازم فذكر الوصف بعينه ذكر الحكم لأنه لازم له، وفيه نظر ظاهر، فإنه هب أن ذكر الملزوم يذكر اللازم لكنه من أين علم أن الحكم لازم للوصف المذكور، وإنما يثبت اللزوم ولو ثبت العلية وبعد فيها الكلام ثم إن ذكر الملزوم وإن كان يفيد ذكر اللازم عقلاً إلا أن الإيماء إنما يكون إذا كانا ملفوظين حقيقة أو حكماً، كما إذا كان أحدهما مقدراً حتى يكون الكلام دالاً عليهما ولو التزاماً، فتأمل
And Ima' is conjoined to his saying 'explicit', meaning indication of causality through a clue. And ima' and explicit can combine, as he, peace and blessings be upon him and his family and companions, said when asked about the permissibility of selling ripe dates for dried dates: 'Does it decrease when it dries?' They said yes. He said: 'Then no.' Reported by the two shaykhs. And discussion of it is attributed to Imam Abu Hanifa. For it is explicit with the word 'idha' and also ima', because without it the indication of causality would be complete as it was, and otherwise the juxtaposition of his saying, peace and blessings be upon him, with 'no' after their saying 'yes' would have no meaning. Hence it comes in the position of an answer, like his saying, peace and blessings be upon him, (to the Bedouin) when he said: 'I am destroyed and have destroyed others, I had intercourse with my wife during the day in Ramadan.' He said: 'Free a slave.' Its derivation has been mentioned. And if the Bedouin's offense were not the cause, the answer would have no meaning. And (and) his saying, peace and blessings be upon him, (to Ibn Mas'ud) when he asked about performing wudu with date wine: 'A good date and pure water.' There is dispute about its authenticity and explanation in Fath al-Qadir. For if the persistence of the name 'water' were not the cause for the permissibility of wudu and the mixing of a pure thing not being an impediment, it would be far-fetched. (And from it is juxtaposing the description with the ruling) for it suggests causality. (Like) his saying, peace and blessings be upon him: 'A judge should not judge while he is angry.' Reported by Bukhari: its wording is 'Let not a judge judge between two while he is angry.' This indicates that the cause for the obligation to avoid judging is anger, then tanqih al-manat indicates that it is the busyness of the heart. (And this) i.e., conjoining the description with the ruling, is (ima') by agreement. For mentioning the description alone without the ruling, like 'Allah permitted trade' — mentioning the permissibility, which is the cause of validity. (Or) mentioning (the ruling alone without the description, like: 'Alcohol is prohibited') — what is mentioned is the prohibition, which is the ruling, without the description, which is the intoxicating strength. And most extracted causes are of this kind. Regarding whether both are considered ima' (so that they take precedence over a cause inferred without ima') because the textually indicated cause is like that, there are (madhhabs: first: both are (yes) like that, because mentioning one of them is sufficient for ima', but there is something in it. (And) second: (both are not) ima' in them, because both must be mentioned, as the Quran indicates causality, so reflect on it. (And) third: (the first) which is that what mentions the description is ima' without the second, which is what mentions the ruling, and (it is the more similar) to correctness (because the conjoining of mention) is ima' absolutely, and mentioning the necessary implies mentioning its necessity. So mentioning the description itself mentions the ruling because it is necessary for it. But there is an apparent objection: even if we grant that mentioning the necessary mentions the necessary, from where is it known that the ruling is necessary for the mentioned description? The necessity is only established if the causality is established, and that is disputed. Moreover, mentioning the necessary, even if it intellectually implies mentioning the necessary, ima' only occurs when both are verbally articulated actually or virtually, as if one of them is implied so that the speech indicates both even by implication. So reflect.
Source pages p. 348

Commentary
This passage is a dispute (مذاهب) over whether mentioning only the description (e.g., 'Allah permitted trade') or only the ruling (e.g., 'Alcohol is prohibited') is considered ima' (indication of causality). The author first gives examples where ima' and explicit statement combine, like the hadith about selling ripe dates for dried dates, where the word 'idha' is explicit and the context provides ima'. He also gives examples of juxtaposing description and ruling (like the hadith of the angry judge). Then he states that by agreement, conjoining the description with the ruling is ima'. The dispute is when only one is mentioned. Three madhhabs: (1) Both are ima' because one is sufficient. (2) Neither is ima' because both must be mentioned. (3) The preferred (أشبه) is that mentioning only the description is ima' on the grounds that the description implies the ruling. However, the author then raises a critique: how do we know the ruling is necessarily implied? That would require establishing causality first. Moreover, ima' requires actual verbal articulation of both, not just intellectual implication. Thus the passage presents the Hanafi position that the description alone can be ima' but with caution.

Key Terms
الإيماء (al-ima'): An indirect indication of causality in a legal text, as opposed to an explicit statement.
الوصف (al-wasf): The description or attribute that serves as the cause ('illah).
الحكم (al-hukm): The legal ruling that is the result of the cause.
تنقيح المناط (tanqih al-manat): The process of refining the effective cause by removing irrelevant attributes.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred position in this passage, which of the following is considered ima'?
A) Mentioning only the description
B) Mentioning only the ruling
C) Both
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, mentioning only the description (wasf) can serve as ima' for causality, but mentioning only the ruling (hukm) does not — the causal link must be independently established.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
