Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
advanced
8 min
Legal Authority of Ilham in Hanafi Usul
Discussion of the legal authority of ilham and its defense

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three positions on saintly ilham in Hanafi usul and evaluate the Hanafi defense of its validity as a source of definitive proof.

Core Idea
Genuine inspiration creates necessary knowledge; it is a definitive proof, not a mere subjective impression.

This passage from Fawatih al-Rahamut explores a debated issue in usul al-fiqh: can the inspiration (ilham) of saints serve as legal evidence? The text presents three views, defends the strongest position, and refutes Ibn al-Humam's rejection. Understanding this dispute clarifies the epistemological hierarchy of proofs in the Hanafi school and the role of mystical knowledge in legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish prophetic from saintly ilham
Note that prophetic ilham is undisputed definitive proof, like the Quran. Saintly ilham is the subject of disagreement. The text sets this distinction first.
Prophetic ilham can abrogate; saintly ilham must conform to established Shari'a.
2
Identify the three views on saintly ilham
List the three positions: (1) proof in legal rulings for all, (2) proof only for the inspired person, (3) not proof at all. The text attributes the first to some Sufis, the second to many scholars, and the third to Ibn al-Humam.
Each view has different implications for using ilham as evidence.
3
Examine the Hanafi defense of the first view
The author argues that genuine ilham involves the creation of necessary knowledge from Allah or the Muhammadan spirit, making it infallible. He cites examples of major awliya and their undeniable spiritual experiences.
This aligns with the Hanafi emphasis on certainty in sources of knowledge.
4
Analyze Ibn al-Humam's objection and the refutation
Ibn al-Humam claimed there is nothing to attribute ilham to Allah, so it cannot be a proof. The author counters that ilham comes with necessary knowledge, so it is indeed from Allah. He also suggests Ibn al-Humam may have confused ilham with passing thoughts.
The refutation relies on the nature of necessary knowledge as directly from Allah.
5
Evaluate the superiority argument
The author invokes the religious necessity that saints of this Ummah are superior to those of previous nations (like Mary and Asiya who received inspiration). Denying the authority of saintly ilham would imply these saints are inferior, which is absurd.
This argument uses both theological principle and logical consistency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الهامه صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم (حجة قطعية عليه وعلى غيره يكفر منكر حقيته ويفسق تارك العمل به كالقرآن (وأما إلهام غيره من الأولياء الكرام فقيل : حجة في الأحكام ونسب إلى قوم من الصوفية وفرقوا بين إلهامهم وإلهام الأنبياء أن إلهامهم لا يكون إلا موافقاً لما أسسه شرع نبيهم المتبوع ومؤيداً منه لا يتلقون العلوم إلا بوساطة روح نبيهم المتبوع، وينالون هذا الشرف بالتبعية، وأما الأنبياء فيلهمون موافقاً لما شرع سابقاً فيقرره أو مخالفاً فينسخه وليس لهم حاجة إلى التأييد، يأخذون من الله تعالى من غير وساطة فافهم (والجعفرية) من الروافض بل الروافض كلهم يرون الأئمة الاثني عشر كرم الله وجوههم معصومين من الخطأ مثل الأنبياء، فإن أراد هذا فلا وجه للتخصيص بالجعفرية وإن أراد نحو الإلهام فلا يفهمونه، وقد ختم الله على قلوبهم فكيف يكون مذهبهم اتباعه (وقيل) الإلهام (حجة عليه) أي على الملهم عليه (فقط) دون غيره ونسب إلى عامة العلماء ولعل وجهه أن الهامهم وإن كان حجة قاطعة إلا أنه لا يجب عليهم دعوة الخلق إليه من حيث إنه إلهامه ولا على الخلق تصديقهم في كونهم ملهماً عليهم، والحجة فرع التصديق، وإلا فيرد عليهم أنه إما حجة يقيد كونه حاكياً عما في الواقع، فالكل في التمسك به سواء وأما ليس حجة فلا يكون حجة في حق نفسه أيضاً (وقيل : ليس حجة أصلاً، واختاره الشيخ ابن الهمام، وعلل بانعدام ما يوجب نسبته إليه (تعالى) أي ليس هناك ما يدل أنه من عند الله تعالى حتى يكون مطابقاً حجة (وفيه ما فيه فإن الإلهام لا يكون إلا مع أنه خلق علم ضروري من عند الله تعالى أو من عند الروح المحمدي، فحينئذ لا يتطرق إليه شبهة الخطأ، وهذا النحو من العلم أعلى مما يحصل بالأدلة الغير القاطعة، فالعجب كل العجب من مثل هذا الشيخ قد رفض وعاءً من العلم، ولعله زعم أن الإلهام ما يحدث في القلب في قبيل الخطرات وليس كذلك، أما سمعت ما كتب الشيخ قطب وقته أبو يزيد البسطامي قدس سره الشريف لبعض من المحدثين : أنتم تأخذون عن ميت فتنسبون إلى رسول الله صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم، ونحن نأخذ من الحي الذي لا يموت، وإن تأملت في مقامات الأولياء ومواجيدهم وأذواقهم كمقامات الشيخ محيي الدين، وقطب الوقت الملة والدين السيد عبد القادر الجيلاني الذي قدمه على رقاب كل ولي، والشيخ سهل بن عبد الله التستري والشيخ أبي مدين المغربي والشيخ أبي يزيد البسطامي، وسيد الطائفة الجنيد البغدادي والشيخ أبي بكر الشبلي والشيخ عبد الله الأنصاري، والشيخ أحمد التامقي وغيرهم قدست أسرارهم علمت أن ما يلهمون به لا يتطرق إليه احتمال وشبهة، بل هو حق حق حق مطابق لما في نفس الأمر. ويكون مع خلق علم ضروري أنه من الله تعالى، لكن لا ينالون هذا الوعاء من العلم إلا بالمدد المحمدي وتأييده لا بالذات من غير وسيلة أصلاً، وإن تأملت في كلام الشيخ الأكبر خليفة الله في الأرضين خاتم فص الولاية الشيخ محيي والدين الشيخ محمد بن العربي قدس سره ووفقنا لفهم كلماته الشريفة : لما بقي لك شائبة وشك في أن ما يلهمون به من الله تعالى. ومما يصلح ههنا أنه على ضرورة من الدين أن أولياء هذه الأمة أفضل من أولياء الأمم السابقين، كما أن نبيهم أفضل من نبي السابقين، ولا شك أن الأولياء الذين كانوا في بني إسرائيل مثل مريم وأم موسى وزوجة فرعون كان يوحى إليهم، ولا أقل من أن يكون إلهاماً، ولا يكون إلا مع خلق علم ضروري أنه من الله تعالى، فهو حجة قاطعة، ولو لم يكن أحد من هذه الأمة المرحومة الفاضلة منهم أفضل في تحصيل العلم القطعي، فتكون مفضولة عنهم غاية المفضولية، لأن التفاضل ليس إلا بالعلم والفضل بما عداه غير معتد به ولا خلف أشنع من هذا اللازم فافهم.
Then the inspiration of him (the Prophet) is definitive proof upon him and others; one who denies its truth becomes a disbeliever and one who abandons acting upon it becomes a sinner, like the Quran. As for the inspiration of other righteous saints, it is said: it is proof in legal rulings, attributed to some Sufis. They distinguish between the inspiration of the saints and that of the prophets: the saints' inspiration is only in accordance with the established law of their prophet to follow, and they are supported by it; they receive knowledge only through the mediation of the spirit of their prophet. They attain this honor by following. As for prophets, they are inspired in accordance with what was previously legislated, confirming it, or contrary to it, abrogating it. They have no need for support; they take directly from Allah without mediation, so understand. The Ja'fariyya (from the Rawafidh) – actually all Rawafidh consider the twelve Imams to be infallible like prophets. If he intends this, there is no reason to specify the Ja'fariyya; if he intends something like inspiration, they do not understand it, and Allah has sealed their hearts, so how could their madhhab be following it? And it is said: inspiration is proof only upon the one who receives it, not upon others. This is attributed to many scholars. Perhaps the reasoning is that although their inspiration is definitive proof, it is not obligatory for them to call people to it, nor for people to believe that they are inspired. Proof depends on belief; otherwise, it is either a proof that describes reality, then everyone can use it, or it is not proof at all, so it is not proof for themselves either. And it is said: it is not proof at all, and Sheikh Ibn al-Humam chose this, reasoning that there is nothing to attribute it to Allah so that it would be a proof. But this is questionable, because inspiration only occurs with the creation of necessary knowledge from Allah or from the Muhammadan spirit; then it is not subject to error. This type of knowledge is higher than that obtained through non-definitive proofs. It is amazing that such a sheikh rejected a vessel of knowledge. Perhaps he thought inspiration occurs in the heart like passing thoughts, but it is not so. Have you not heard what Sheikh Abu Yazid al-Bistami wrote to some hadith scholars: 'You take from a dead person and attribute to the Prophet, while we take from the Living who does not die.' If you reflect on the stations of the saints and their experiences, like the stations of Sheikh Muhyi al-Din, the polar saint 'Abd al-Qadir al-Jilani, etc., you will know that what they are inspired with is not subject to doubt, but is true and conforms to reality. It comes with necessary knowledge that it is from Allah, but they attain this vessel of knowledge only through the Muhammadan aid, not directly... And it is a religious necessity that the saints of this Ummah are superior to the saints of previous nations. If not, this Ummah would be inferior, which is absurd. So understand.
Source pages p. 410

Commentary
This passage addresses the legal status of ilham (inspiration). It first establishes that the Prophet's ilham is definitive proof (hujja qat'iyya), equivalent to the Quran in authority. Then it discusses the ilham of awliya (saints), presenting three views: 1) It is proof in legal rulings (attributed to some Sufis), who distinguish between prophetic and saintly ilham. 2) It is proof only for the inspired person (attributed to most scholars). 3) It is not proof at all (chosen by Ibn al-Humam). The author strongly defends the first view, arguing that genuine ilham involves necessary knowledge from Allah and is therefore infallible. He refutes Ibn al-Humam's objection by citing the experiences of major awliya and arguing that denying their authority leads to the absurd conclusion that the saints of this Ummah are inferior to those of previous nations, contradicting the superiority of this Ummah.

Key Terms
الإلهام (ilham): Inspiration from Allah or the Muhammadan spirit that creates necessary knowledge.
الحجة القطعية (al-hujja al-qat'iyya): Definitive proof that must be accepted; denial entails disbelief.
العلم الضروري (al-'ilm al-daruri): Necessary knowledge that is self-evident and cannot be doubted.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the main reason the author rejects Ibn al-Humam's view that saintly ilham is not proof?
A) Because Ibn al-Humam was not a Sufi.
B) Because ilham creates necessary knowledge from Allah, making it infallible.
C) Because the majority of scholars hold the opposite view.
D) Because the Quran explicitly commands following saintly ilham.

Key Takeaway
Genuine saintly ilham, involving necessary knowledge from Allah, is a valid definitive proof in Hanafi usul, contrary to the objections of Ibn al-Humam.

The Probative Value of Ilham: A Hanafi Rational Defense
Defending ilham as a source of certain knowledge against Ibn al-Humam's criticism

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the Hanafi defense of ilham as a source of certain knowledge and identify the rational argument (dalil aqli) used to counter Ibn al-Humam's objection.

Core Idea
Ilham is a vessel of certain knowledge, not a mere thought — rejecting it implies the superiority of previous nations' saints over this ummah's, an impossible consequence.

In this passage, the commentator vigorously defends ilham (divine inspiration) against the criticism of Ibn al-Humam, who denied its probative value due to the lack of a clear indicator of its divine origin. The commentator argues that ilham is accompanied by necessary knowledge, making it immune to error, and cites the superiority of this ummah's saints as a conclusive rational proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two opposing positions
Clearly state Ibn al-Humam's denial of ilham's evidentiary value and the commentator's affirmation. Note that both are Hanafi scholars.
This is an intra-madhhab dispute within Hanafi usul.
2
Understand the commentator's definition of ilham
Ilham is not a random thought (khatir) but a form of necessary knowledge ('ilm daruri) created by God in the heart, making it indubitable.
The commentator distinguishes ilham from mere thoughts.
3
Follow the rational argument from superiority
The commentator argues that if ilham were not certain, then the awliya of this ummah would be inferior in knowledge to those of previous nations (e.g., Mary, mother of Moses). This is impossible because this ummah is superior. Therefore ilham is certain.
This is an argument a fortiori (qiyas al-awlawi).
4
Evaluate the strength of the proof
Assess whether the commentator's argument constitutes a decisive proof (hujjah qati'ah) for the individual saint, or merely a persuasive argument. Consider the role of personal experience versus public verifiability.
The commentator claims ilham produces personal certainty, not necessarily a public proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
خاتمة في الاجتهاد والتقليد على محيي، واختاره الشيخ ابن الهمام، وعلل بانعدام ما يوجب نسبته إليه (تعالى) أي ليس هناك ما يدل أنه من عند الله تعالى حتى يكون مطابقاً حجة (وفيه ما فيه فإن الإلهام لا يكون إلا مع أنه خلق علم ضروري من عند الله تعالى أو من عند الروح المحمدي، فحينئذ لا يتطرق إليه شبهة الخطأ، وهذا النحو من العلم أعلى مما يحصل بالأدلة الغير القاطعة، فالعجب كل العجب من مثل هذا الشيخ قد رفض وعاءً من العلم، ولعله زعم أن الإلهام ما يحدث في القلب في قبيل الخطرات وليس كذلك، أما سمعت ما كتب الشيخ قطب وقته أبو يزيد البسطامي قدس سره الشريف لبعض من المحدثين : أنتم تأخذون عن ميت فتنسبون إلى رسول الله صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم، ونحن نأخذ من الحي الذي لا يموت، وإن تأملت في مقامات الأولياء ومواجيدهم وأذواقهم كمقامات الشيخ محيي الدين، وقطب الوقت الملة والدين السيد عبد القادر الجيلاني الذي قدمه على رقاب كل ولي، والشيخ سهل بن عبد الله التستري والشيخ أبي مدين المغربي والشيخ أبي يزيد البسطامي، وسيد الطائفة الجنيد البغدادي والشيخ أبي بكر الشبلي والشيخ عبد الله الأنصاري، والشيخ أحمد التامقي وغيرهم قدست أسرارهم علمت أن ما يلهمون به لا يتطرق إليه احتمال وشبهة، بل هو حق حق حق مطابق لما في نفس الأمر. ويكون مع خلق علم ضروري أنه من الله تعالى، لكن لا ينالون هذا الوعاء من العلم إلا بالمدد المحمدي وتأييده لا بالذات من غير وسيلة أصلاً، وإن تأملت في كلام الشيخ الأكبر خليفة الله في الأرضين خاتم فص الولاية الشيخ محيي والدين الشيخ محمد بن العربي قدس سره ووفقنا لفهم كلماته الشريفة : لما بقي لك شائبة وشك في أن ما يلهمون به من الله تعالى. ومما يصلح ههنا أنه على ضرورة من الدين أن أولياء هذه الأمة أفضل من أولياء الأمم السابقين، كما أن نبيهم أفضل من نبي السابقين، ولا شك أن الأولياء الذين كانوا في بني إسرائيل مثل مريم وأم موسى وزوجة فرعون كان يوحى إليهم، ولا أقل من أن يكون إلهاماً، ولا يكون إلا مع خلق علم ضروري أنه من الله تعالى، فهو حجة قاطعة، ولو لم يكن أحد من هذه الأمة المرحومة الفاضلة منهم أفضل في تحصيل العلم القطعي، فتكون مفضولة عنهم غاية المفضولية، لأن التفاضل ليس إلا بالعلم والفضل بما عداه غير معتد به ولا خلف أشنع من هذا اللازم فافهم.
Conclusion on Ijtihad and Taqlid. He (Ibn al-Humam) chose the opinion that there is nothing that necessitates its attribution to God, meaning there is no indication that it is from God so as to be a proof that conforms [to reality]. There is much to discuss in this, for ilham only occurs with the creation of necessary knowledge from God or from the Muhammadan spirit; then no doubt of error reaches it, and this type of knowledge is higher than that obtained by non-definitive proofs. It is amazing that such a scholar rejected a vessel of knowledge. Perhaps he claimed that ilham is what occurs in the heart among the category of thoughts, but it is not so. Have you not heard what the spiritual master Abu Yazid al-Bistami wrote to some hadith scholars: 'You take from a dead person and attribute to the Messenger of God, but we take from the Living who never dies.' If you reflect on the stations of the saints, their states and experiences, such as those of Muhyi al-Din, the spiritual pole of the religion Sayyid Abd al-Qadir al-Jilani, whom he preferred over every saint, and Sahl ibn Abd Allah al-Tustari, Abu Madyan al-Maghribi, Abu Yazid al-Bistami, the master of the sect Junayd al-Baghdadi, Abu Bakr al-Shibli, Abd Allah al-Ansari, Ahmad al-Tamighi and others, may their secrets be sanctified, you will know that what they are inspired with is not reached by possibility or doubt; rather it is true, true, true, conforming to what is in actuality. It is accompanied by the creation of necessary knowledge that it is from God, but they attain this vessel of knowledge only through Muhammadan aid and support, not by essence without any intermediary. If you reflect on the speech of the Greatest Master, the Caliph of God on earth, the Seal of the Seal of Sainthood, Muhyi al-Din Muhammad ibn al-Arabi, may God sanctify his secret and grant us understanding of his noble words: 'If any trace of doubt remains in you that what they are inspired with is from God...' And what is suitable here is that it is a necessary matter of religion that the saints of this ummah are superior to the saints of previous nations, just as their prophet is superior to the prophets of previous nations. There is no doubt that the saints among the Children of Israel, such as Mary, the mother of Moses, and the wife of Pharaoh, were inspired by revelation, or at least by ilham. This only occurs with the creation of necessary knowledge that it is from God, so it is a decisive proof. If there were not anyone from this merciful, virtuous ummah superior to them in acquiring certain knowledge, then this ummah would be extremely inferior, because superiority is only through knowledge and virtue; anything else is not considered. There is no uglier consequence than this, so understand.
Source pages p. 411

Commentary
This passage is an extensive refutation of Ibn al-Humam's position that ilham (divine inspiration) lacks evidentiary value because there is no way to ascertain its divine origin. The commentator argues vigorously that ilham, when it occurs to the awliya (saints), is accompanied by the creation of necessary knowledge (ilm daruri) in the heart, either from God directly or from the Muhammadan spirit, making it immune to error. He cites examples of great Sufi masters—Abu Yazid al-Bistami, Abd al-Qadir al-Jilani, Junayd al-Baghdadi, and others—to demonstrate that their inspirations are absolutely certain and conform to reality. He also refutes the notion that ilham is merely a type of passing thought (khatir). The commentator further employs an argument a fortiori: if the saints of previous nations like Mary, the mother of Moses, and the wife of Pharaoh received ilham, and if the awliya of this ummah are superior to them (as is a matter of religious necessity), then the awliya of this ummah must also attain certain knowledge through ilham; otherwise, the ummah would be inferior in knowledge, which is unacceptable. Thus, ilham is a decisive proof (hujjah qati'ah) for those who receive it.

Key Terms
الإلهام (al-ilham): Divine inspiration that occurs in the heart of a saint accompanied by necessary knowledge that it is from God
علم ضروري ('ilm daruri): Necessary knowledge that is self-evident and indubitable, created by God in the heart
حجة قاطعة (hujjah qati'ah): A decisive proof that leaves no room for doubt or error

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, what makes ilham a decisive proof (hujjah qati'ah)?
A) It is based on the consensus of scholars
B) It is accompanied by the creation of necessary knowledge that it is from God
C) It is mentioned in the Quran
D) It is supported by analogical reasoning

Key Takeaway
Ilham, when accompanied by necessary knowledge, is a decisive proof for the individual saint, but its probative value in deriving shar'i rulings remains contested within legal theory.

The Permissibility of Prophetic Error in Ijtihad: Majority View and Refutation
Examining the majority view that prophets can err in ijtihad with arguments and counterarguments

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on prophetic error in ijtihad, identify the primary evidence (Badr incident and hadith), and evaluate the three main objections and their responses.

Core Idea
Prophets may err in ijtihad but are not confirmed in error; divine confirmation (taqrir) restores certainty.

Prophets, despite their infallibility in conveying revelation, engage in ijtihad for non-revealed matters. A key question in Usul al-Fiqh is whether such ijtihad can contain error. This lesson examines the majority Sunni view that error is possible, supported by textual evidence and logical arguments, and refutes the objections raised by those who deny it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Disputed Issue and the Majority View
Read the opening of the passage to identify the question: Can the Prophet (and other prophets) err in ijtihad? Note the majority position (yes, but not confirmed in error) and the dissenting view.
The minority is identified as some Rawafid and a few Sunni scholars.
2
Analyze the Primary Evidences: Badr Captives and Hadith
Examine the incident of Badr captives: the Prophet initially refrained from taking ransom, then divine revelation permitted it. Note how this is used as evidence for error in ijtihad. Then study the hadith about judging based on apparent evidence—how it implies error in application.
The hadith is from Sahih al-Bukhari and Muslim.
3
Understand the Refutation of the First Objection
The first objection: if error is possible, doubt undermines the purpose of prophethood. Trace the response: divine confirmation (taqrir) cuts off doubt. The error does not relate to the message itself.
The text also references the example of David and Solomon to show prior prophetic errors.
4
Evaluate the Second and Third Objections and Their Refutations
Second objection: following an erroneous ruling would be commanded. Third objection: prophetic ijtihad should be more infallible than consensus. Work through the Hanafi responses: we follow prophets like laymen follow mujtahids, and superiority in general does not guarantee infallibility in every particular.
The response to the third objection includes the example of Umar's excellence in the Badr captives case.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فرع: هل يجوز عليه صلى الله عليه وعلى آله وأصحابه وسلم الخطأ في اجتهاده، وكذا في اجتهاد سائر الأنبياء فالأكثر من أهل السنة قالوا : نعم يجوز وقيل: لا يجوز ونقل هذا النفي عن الروافض أيضاً وأما إنه لا يقرر عليه فاتفاق لنا مفاداة أسارى بدر كان بالرأي وكان خطأ لنزول العتاب كما مر وأما عدم نقضه فلأن حكم الاجتهاد لا ينقض . روي أن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم تحرج عن أخذ ما فدوا به فلما نزل: فَكُلُوا مِمَّا غَنِمْتُمْ حَلَالًا طَيِّباً ﴾ [الأنفال: ٦٩] أخذ قال القاضي الإمام أبو زيد رحمه الله تعالى : إن هذا لم يكن خطأ كيف وقد قرر عليه، والخطأ مما لا يقرر عليه بل كان أخذ الفداء جائزاً إلا أن قتلهم كان عزيمة والمفاداة رخصة فعوتب باختيار الرخصة، وهذا لا يكاد يفقهه وأيضاً أمثال هذا العبد فإنه قد ورد التنبيه به فأين التقرير والعمل بالرخصة لا يستحق أن ينزل فيه العتاب، فكيف دنو العذاب من الشجرة. وأما الأخذ بعد ظهور الخطأ، فإما لورود التحليل بعد ذلك ابتداء أو لعدم انفساخ حكم الاجتهاد بعد ظهور الخطأ، ولنا أيضاً أنه أخطأ داود على نبينا وآله وأصحابه وعليه الصلاة والسلام في الحكم في الحرث وفي القضاء في الولد وفي كليهما أصاب سليمان وغير ذلك من الوقائع واستدل على المختار أيضاً أولاً لو امتنع الخطأ لكان لمانع والأصل عدمه وفيه نظر ظاهر فإنه لا بد من وجود مقتض وهو ممنوع في محل النزاع وأجيب أيضاً بعد تسليم أن الامتناع لمانع بأنه أي المانع كمال فهمه وعلو درجته فإن قلت : وقع السهو مع وجود كمال الفهم قال ونحو سها فسجد ليس مما نحن فيه لاشتراط استفراغ الوسع كما في الاجتهاد ولا استفراغ ههنا واستدل ثانياً قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وعلى آله وأصحابه وسلم إنكم تختصمون إلي إلى آخر الحديث وهو ما في الصحيحين إنكم تختصمون إلي فلعل بعضكم ألحن بحجته من بعض فأقضي له على نحو ما أسمع فمن قضيت له بشيء من حق أخيه فلا يأخذ منه شيئاً فإنما أقطع له قطعة من النار وأجيب بأن الكلام في استنباط الكليات من الأحكام لا في تطبيق الجزئيات عليها، لا والحديث يدل على الخطأ في الثاني لا الأوّل، ولو تشبث بدلالة النص وتنقيح المناط لم يبعد. المنكرون قالوا : أولاً الشك في الإصابة يخل بمقصود البعثة فإن المقصود منه أن يصدقوه فيما بلغ ويعملوا به قلنا الإخلال مطلقاً ممنوع ، وإنما يكون الإخلال ولو كان الشك في الرسالة وليس كذلك أقول في السند ثانياً على أن التقرير حاسم للشك فلا إخلال وإنما الإخلال لو بقي الشك وقالوا : ثانياً : لو جاز الخطأ لزم الأمر من قبل الشارع باتباع الخطأ لأنا مأمورون بالاتباع له عليه وعلى آله وأصحابه الصلاة والسلام في الأمور كلها قلنا: نحن معشر المجتهدين منه كالعوام من المجتهد بل ليس هذه النسبة، فإن العامي يجوز له إنكار هذا المجتهد واختيار غيره وليس لنا اختيار نبي آخر، وإذ قد أمر العوام باتباع الخطأ المجتهد لكونه أفضل منهم فالأمر باتباع الخطأ الصادر من سيد البشر أولى بالتحقق وقالوا ثالثاً : اجتهاده أولى بالعصمة من الإجماع فإنه أفضل من أهل الإجماع أقول : لو تم هذا لم يكن الإجماع مقدماً على النص عند التعارض هذا وليس بشيء، فإن تقدم الإجماع على النص ليس لأنه أولى بالعصمة من النص، بل لأن الإجماع كاشف عن وجود ناسخ أو ضعف في ثبوت النص، أو أنه مؤوّل وإلا لزم المعارضة بين القاطعين بل الحق في الجواب أن الأولوية دعوى من غير برهان وكونه أفضل من أهل الإجماع لا يوجب الفضل من كل الوجوه الجزئية، فإن الفضل الجزئي لا ينافي الفضل الكلي، ألم تر أنه كيف فضل أمير المؤمنين عمر في أسارى بدر فافهم. فرع على هذا الفرع وإذا جاز صدور الخطأ في الاجتهاد من الأنبياء والعمل بحكم من خطأ.
Branch: Is it permissible for the Prophet, peace be upon him, to err in his ijtihad, and likewise in the ijtihad of other prophets? The majority of Ahl al-Sunna said: Yes, it is permissible. It has been said: Not permissible, and this negation is also transmitted from the Rawafid. As for the fact that he is not confirmed in error, there is agreement on that. The ransoming of the captives of Badr was by opinion and was an error due to the revelation of reproach, as passed. As for why it was not invalidated, it is because the ruling of ijtihad is not invalidated. It is narrated that the Messenger of God, peace be upon him, hesitated to take the ransom, but when was revealed: 'Eat from what you have gained as lawful and good' (al-Anfal 69), he took it. The judge Abu Zaid said: This was not an error, as it was confirmed upon him, and error is not confirmed. Rather, taking ransom was permissible, but killing them was the determination and ransom was a concession, so he was reproached for choosing the concession. This is hardly understood. Also, for such as this servant, admonition has occurred; so where is the confirmation? Acting on a concession does not deserve reproach; how then the nearness of punishment from the tree? As for taking after the appearance of error, it was either due to the revelation of permissibility anew or because the ruling of ijtihad did not dissolve after the appearance of error. We also have that David, peace be upon him, erred in his judgment about the tillage and in the judgment about the child, while Solomon was correct in both, and other incidents. He also argued for the preferred opinion: first, if error were impossible, it would be due to a preventer, and the principle is its absence. This has an apparent flaw: a cause must exist, and its existence is denied in the matter of contention. It was answered, after accepting that impossibility is due to a preventer, that the preventer is his perfect understanding and high rank. If you say: inadvertence occurred despite perfect understanding, he said: inadvertence and prostration are not our concern because ijtihad requires maximal effort, which does not exist here. Second, he argued: the Prophet, peace be upon him, said: 'You present disputes to me; perhaps some of you are more eloquent in argument than others, so I judge in their favor based on what I hear. Whoever I have awarded something of his brother's right, let him not take it, for I am cutting off a piece of the Fire for him.' This is in the Two Sahihs. The answer is that the discussion is about deduction of general principles, not application of particulars. No, the hadith indicates error in the second, not the first. If one insists on literal indication and refinement of the ratio legis, it is not far. The deniers said: First, doubt in correctness undermines the purpose of prophethood, for the purpose is that they believe him in what he conveys and act on it. We say: undermining in absolute sense is denied; undermining would occur if the doubt were in the message, which is not the case. I say: in the second place, confirmation cuts off doubt, so there is no undermining; only if doubt remains. They said: Second, if error were permissible, it would necessitate the command from the Lawgiver to follow error, for we are commanded to follow him in all matters. We say: We, the mujtahids, are like the common folk to the mujtahid, rather this analogy is false. The layman may reject this mujtahid and choose another; we do not have the option to choose another prophet. Since the layman is commanded to follow the error of a mujtahid because he is superior to them, the command to follow error from the master of mankind is more likely. They said: Third, his ijtihad is more entitled to infallibility than consensus, for he is superior to the people of consensus. I say: If this held, consensus would not take precedence over the text when they conflict. But that is nothing, for consensus takes precedence over text not because it is more infallible than text, but because consensus reveals the existence of an abrogator or weakness in the text's authenticity, or that it is interpreted; otherwise, conflict between two certainties would arise. Rather, the truth is that priority is a claim without proof; his being superior to the people of consensus does not necessitate superiority in every particular aspect, for particular superiority does not negate overall superiority. Do you not see how the Commander of the Faithful, Umar, excelled in the case of the captives of Badr? So understand. A branch of this branch: If error in ijtihad is permissible from prophets and acting upon a ruling of error.
Source pages p. 411

Commentary
This passage addresses the controversial question of whether prophets can err in their ijtihad (independent reasoning). The author presents the majority view among Ahl al-Sunna that it is permissible for prophets to err in matters subject to ijtihad, though they are not confirmed in error (i.e., God does not allow them to persist). A minority view denies this. The incident of the captives of Badr is cited as an example: the Prophet initially refrained from taking ransom, but after divine reproach and subsequent permission, he acted. Some scholars like Judge Abu Zaid argue this was not an error but a choice of a concession over a stricter ruling, for which he was gently reprimanded. The author then provides arguments supporting permissibility: (1) the absence of a clear preventer, (2) the hadith about judging based on apparent evidence (which implies the possibility of error in application), and (3) examples of previous prophets like David and Solomon. The counterarguments from deniers are presented and refuted: that doubt in his correctness would undermine prophethood (refuted: confidence is restored by divine confirmation), that following error would be commanded (refuted: the obligation to obey is akin to obeying a mujtahid, not absolute infallibility), and that his ijtihad should be more infallible than consensus (refuted: consensus reveals textual conditions, not superiority). The passage concludes with a branch: if prophets can err in ijtihad, what of acting upon an erroneous ruling?

Key Terms
الخطأ في الاجتهاد (al-khata' fi al-ijtihad): An incorrect ruling derived through independent reasoning, distinguished from error in matters of definitive revelation
التقرير (al-taqrir): Divine confirmation or approval that removes the harmful effects of an error and indicates the correct ruling
الاجتهاد (al-ijtihad): Maximal effort by a qualified scholar to derive a ruling from the legal sources

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the main evidence from the hadith that the majority uses to argue for the permissibility of prophetic error in ijtihad?
A) The story of David and Solomon
B) The verse about the captives of Badr
C) The hadith: 'You present disputes to me; perhaps some of you are more eloquent...'
D) The consensus of the Companions

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits prophetic error in ijtihad, but divine confirmation ensures the final ruling is correct and authoritative.

Possibility of Error in Prophetic Ijtihad
Examining arguments for and against error in prophets' ijtihad

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on prophetic ijtihad error, identify the key proofs, and evaluate the opposing arguments using Hanafi usul principles.

Core Idea
Prophets can err in ijtihad but not in delivering revelation—uncertainty is about rulings, not message.

This lesson examines a foundational usul dispute: Can a prophet err when exercising ijtihad (independent legal reasoning)? The Hanafi school answers affirmatively, drawing on Quranic incidents and a Prophetic hadith. We will work through the proofs, the objections of those who deny this possibility, and the Hanafi responses that establish the chosen (mukhtar) position. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to distinguish between infallibility in conveying revelation and the fallibility of human reasoning even at the highest level.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core dispute
Recognize that the issue is whether prophets can err in their ijtihad—not in receiving or delivering revelation. The Hanafi school says yes; some theologians say no.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf (difference of opinion) within the Ummah.
2
Examine the Hanafi proofs
The author cites two main proofs: (1) the Quranic story of Dawood (AS) and Sulayman (AS) where Dawood's judgment was corrected by Sulayman; (2) the hadith 'I judge according to what I hear' which implies the Prophet's judgment could be based on incomplete evidence.
The hadith is from Sahih al-Bukhari and Muslim.
3
Analyze the deniers' objections
The opponents argue: (i) uncertainty defeats the purpose of prophethood; (ii) if error is possible, we would be commanded to follow error; (iii) prophetic ijtihad is more immune than consensus.
Each objection has a theological and logical underpinning.
4
Evaluate the Hanafi responses
The responses clarify: (i) doubt is about rulings, not the message; (ii) following a prophet's erroneous ruling is obligatory because of his superiority and because we cannot choose another prophet; (iii) immunity is not absolute—partial superiority does not preclude partial error.
The tarjih (choice) of the Hanafi position is based on textual evidence and rational argument.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولنا أيضاً أنه أخطأ داود على نبينا وآله وأصحابه وعليه الصلاة والسلام في الحكم في الحرث وفي القضاء في الولد وفي كليهما أصاب سليمان وغير ذلك من الوقائع واستدل على المختار أيضاً أولاً لو امتنع الخطأ لكان تطبيق الجزئيات عليها، لا والحديث يدل على الخطأ في الثاني لا الأوّل، ولو تشبث بدلالة النص وتنقيح المناط لم يبعد. المنكرون قالوا أولاً الشك في الإصابة يخل بمقصود البعثة فإن المقصود منه أن يصدقوه فيما يجوز له إنكار هذا المجتهد واختيار غيره وليس لنا اختيار نبي آخر، وإذ قد أمر العوام باتباع الخطأ المجتهد لكونه أفضل منهم فالأمر باتباع الخطأ الصادر من سيد البشر أولى بالتحقق وقالوا ثالثاً اجتهاده أولى بالعصمة من الإجماع فإنه أفضل من أهل الإجماع أقول لو تم هذا لم يكن الإجماع مقدماً على النص عند التعارض هذا وليس بشيء، فإن تقدم الإجماع على النص ليس لأنه أولى بالعصمة من النص، بل لأن الإجماع كاشف عن And we also have that David (peace be upon him) erred in the judgment regarding the cultivation and in the ruling about the child, and in both Solomon was correct, and other incidents. And he (the author) adduced evidence for the chosen position: First, if error were impossible, there would be a preventer, and the default is absence of a preventer. This is apparently problematic, because a cause is necessary, and that is denied in the disputed case. It was also answered, after conceding that impossibility is due to a preventer, that the preventer is the perfection of his understanding and his high rank. If you say: forgetfulness occurred despite perfection of understanding, he said: such as forgetfulness in prostration is not our concern because it requires exhausting one's capacity, as in ijtihad, and there is no exhaustion here. Second, he adduced the hadith of the Prophet (peace be upon him): 'You bring disputes to me...' up to the end, which is in the two Sahihs: 'You bring disputes to me, and perhaps some of you are more eloquent in presenting their argument than others, so I judge according to what I hear. So if I give someone something of his brother's right, let him not take it, for I am giving him a piece of Hellfire.' It was answered that the discussion is about deriving general rulings, not about applying them to particulars. No, the hadith indicates error in the second, not the first. And if one clings to the textual indication and the refinement of the effective cause, it is not far. The deniers said: First, uncertainty about correctness defeats the purpose of sending prophets, which is that people should believe them and act. We say: absolute defeat is denied; defeat would occur if there were doubt about the message, not about ijtihad. I say: in the chain, the confirmation removes doubt, so no defeat; defeat would occur if doubt remained. They said: Second, if error were permitted, the Lawgiver would command following error, because we are commanded to follow him in all matters. We say: we, the mujtahids, are to him as the common people are to the mujtahid. Rather, this analogy is not valid, because the common person may reject this mujtahid and choose another, but we cannot choose another prophet. Since the common people are commanded to follow the erring mujtahid because he is better than them, then commanding to follow error from the master of mankind is more appropriate. They said: Third, his ijtihad is more immune to error than consensus, because he is better than the people of consensus. I say: if this were true, consensus would not take precedence over textual evidence when they conflict. This is nothing, because consensus takes precedence not because it is more immune, but because consensus indicates the existence of an abrogator or weakness in the text, or that it is interpreted; otherwise contradiction between two definitive proofs would occur. Rather, the truth is that priority is an unsubstantiated claim. His being better than the people of consensus does not necessitate superiority in every partial aspect; partial superiority does not negate overall superiority. Do you not see how Amir al-Mu'minin Umar excelled in the captives of Badr? So understand. A branch of this branch: if error in ijtihad is permitted for prophets and acting upon an erroneous ruling...
Source pages p. 412

Commentary
This passage is a dispute (usul_dispute) on whether prophets can err in their ijtihad (independent reasoning). The Hanafi position, presented as the chosen view (al-mukhtar), affirms that error is possible. The author first lists proofs: the incidents of Dawood and Sulayman, and the hadith about judging based on appearances. He responds to objections: that doubt would undermine the purpose of prophethood, that following error would be required, and that prophetic ijtihad is more immune than consensus. The refutation clarifies that the hadith deals with application, not derivation; that doubt is about rulings, not about the message; that following a prophet's error is different from following a mujtahid's; and that immunity is not absolute. The branch (far') at the end discusses action based on erroneous prophetic ijtihad.

Key Terms
خطأ (khatta'): Error in ijtihad, a mistaken ruling despite exhaustive reasoning
استنباط الكليات (istinbat al-kulliyat): Derivation of general legal principles from textual sources
تطبيق الجزئيات (tatbiq al-juz'iyyat): Application of general principles to specific cases

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi response to the objection that if prophets can err, we would be commanded to follow error?
A) That error is in application, not derivation, so the command to follow does not apply.
B) That following a prophet is obligatory even if he errs because he is superior and we have no alternative.
C) That the command to follow applies only to revelation, not ijtihad.
D) That the objection is invalid because prophets never err.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, prophetic ijtihad is not infallible; error is possible in ruling derivation, but the prophet's superiority and the impossibility of choosing another prophet make following his rulings obligatory even if erroneous, until corrected by revelation.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement

Abu Bakr's Judgment on the Salab: Ijtihad or Application?
Refuting the claim that Abu Bakr engaged in ijtihad in the Prophet's presence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the claim that Abu Bakr's judgment on the salab was ijtihad and explain why the Hanafi school considers it application of a clear text.

Core Idea
Ijtihad cannot occur in the presence of the Prophet’s explicit text; Abu Bakr applied the ruling, he did not reason independently.

This passage examines a key incident often cited to argue that ijtihad was practiced in the Prophet’s presence. The author refutes this by showing that Abu Bakr al-Siddiq was simply implementing the Prophet’s clear statement, ‘Whoever kills a slain person, his spoils belong to him.’ Understanding this distinction is crucial for the Hanafi principle that ijtihad is invalid when a text exists.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim being refuted
Some scholars claim that Abu Bakr's judgment was ijtihad, proving that ijtihad can occur in the Prophet's presence. The author aims to refute this.
The opposing view holds that Abu Bakr independently reasoned about the spoils.
2
Locate the explicit text applied by Abu Bakr
The Prophet ﷺ said: 'Whoever kills a slain person, his spoils belong to him.' Abu Bakr knew this ruling and applied it directly to the case.
The hadith is quoted in the passage as the basis for Abu Bakr's decision.
3
Analyze why the author says Abu Bakr was certain
Abu Bakr was knowledgeable and certain that the Prophet does not place rights except in their proper places. He emphasized by oath that the spoils belong to the killer.
Certainty of the ruling precludes any need for ijtihad.
4
Compare Hanafi and Shafi’i interpretations
The Hanafi school views the hadith as a specific permission from the Prophet as imam, while al-Shafi'i considers it general legislation. This difference affects whether ijtihad could be involved.
The Hanafi position limits the ruling to the specific context, whereas the Shafi'i would allow it as a general rule.
5
Conclude the Hanafi position
The incident does not support ijtihad in the Prophet's presence; it shows adherence to his explicit command. Ijtihad is invalid when a clear text (nass) exists.
This is a fundamental usul principle for the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والسلام لقد حكمت بحكم الله وفي لفظ البخاري قال : قضيت بحكم الله (وأما قول) أفضل الصديقين بعد الأنبياء عليهم السلام ورضي الله عنه أبي بكر الصديق حين قتل أبو قتادة الأنصاري مشركاً، وقال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم: «من قتل قتيلاً فله سلبه فقام وقال : قتلت قتيلاً، فقال رجل : صدق وسيلة عندي فأرضه يا رسول الله «لاها الله ، قسم (إذ لا يعمد إلى أسد من أسود الله يقاتل عن الله تعالى ورسوله : «فيعطيك سلبه» رواه البخاري في حديث طويل في قصة حنين فأقول في كونه اجتهاداً) كما زعم البعض واستدل على وقوع الاجتهاد عند الحضرة (نظر) لأنه قاله بعد قوله عليه وآله الصلاة والسلام: «من قتل قتيلاً فله سلبه» فقد تعلق حق القائل بسلب المقتول سواء كان هذا شرعاً عاماً، كما زعم الشافعي رضي الله عنه أو أذناً وعدة لكونه إماماً كما هو عندنا (وقد كان عالماً) موقناً بأنه عليه) وآله وأصحابه الصلاة والسلام لا يضع الحقوق إلا في مواضعها، ومن ثمة أكد هو رضي عنه بالقسم فلم يكن احتمال الخطأ عنده رضي الله عنه (كما في التحرير) إنه كان يعلم لو كان رده (وما دل هذا على ثبوت الخيرة له بين الرجوع والاجتهاد كما في المختصر تدبر ) بل الرجوع هو الصواب المختار عند الإمكان قبل فوت الحادثة .
And peace. He said: 'I have judged by the judgment of Allah.' In the wording of al-Bukhari, he said: 'I decreed by the judgment of Allah.' (As for the statement) of the best of the Siddiqs after the Prophets, peace be upon them and may Allah be pleased with him, Abu Bakr al-Siddiq, when Abu Qatadah al-Ansari killed a polytheist, and the Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and his family and companions and grant them peace, said: 'Whoever kills a slain person, his spoils belong to him.' So he stood and said: 'I killed a slain person.' A man said: 'He is truthful, his weapon is with me, so give him satisfaction, O Messenger of Allah.' 'No, by Allah, a division (since one does not intend a lion of the lions of Allah who fights for Allah and His Messenger), so he gives you his spoils.' Narrated by al-Bukhari in a long hadith in the story of Hunayn. So I say regarding it being ijtihad, as some have claimed and used as evidence for the occurrence of ijtihad in the presence of the Prophet, there is consideration. Because he said it after his statement, peace and blessings be upon him: 'Whoever kills a slain person, his spoils belong to him.' So the right of the speaker became attached to the spoils of the killed, whether this is general legislation, as al-Shafi'i, may Allah be pleased with him, claimed, or permission and promise due to his being an imam, as it is with us. And he was knowledgeable, certain that he, peace and blessings be upon him and his family and companions, does not place rights except in their places. Hence he, may Allah be pleased with him, emphasized by oath, so there was no possibility of error in his view, may Allah be pleased with him. As in al-Tahrir, he knew if he had returned it. And this does not indicate the establishment of choice for him between returning and ijtihad, as in al-Mukhtasar, reflect. Rather, returning is the correct choice when possible before the case is lost.
Source pages p. 414

Commentary
This passage is a ruling dispute. The author refutes the claim that Abu Bakr al-Siddiq's judgment regarding the spoils (salab) of a slain polytheist during the Battle of Hunayn was an instance of ijtihad in the presence of the Prophet ﷺ. The opponent cites this incident to prove that ijtihad is permissible even when the Prophet is present. The Hanafi position, as articulated here, is that Abu Bakr was not exercising ijtihad; he was merely applying the explicit ruling of the Prophet ﷺ: 'Whoever kills a slain person, his spoils belong to him.' The author argues that Abu Bakr, being a knowledgeable Companion, was certain that the Prophet's ruling applied to this case. The mention of al-Shafi'i indicates a school difference: al-Shafi'i considers the ruling a general legislation, while the Hanafis view it as a specific permission or promise from the Prophet as an imam. The conclusion is that the incident does not support the permissibility of ijtihad in the Prophet's presence; rather, it shows adherence to his explicit command.

Key Terms
اجتهاد (ijtihad): Independent reasoning to derive a ruling when no clear text exists.
سلب (salab): The spoils taken from a slain enemy fighter.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is Abu Bakr's judgment not considered ijtihad?
A) Because he was not a mujtahid.
B) Because he was applying the Prophet's explicit ruling about the spoils.
C) Because the Prophet corrected him afterwards.
D) Because ijtihad is not allowed in the Hanafi school.

Key Takeaway
Abu Bakr's application of the Prophet’s ruling on salab demonstrates that ijtihad ceases when a clear text (nass) is present.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement

The Hanafi Categorization of the Mistaken Mujtahid
Defining the status of mistaken mujtahids

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for a mistaken mujtahid in rational (ʿaqliyat) and legal (sharʿiyat) matters, distinguishing between deniers of definitive truths (kufr) and deniers of speculative matters (fisq).

Core Idea
In Hanafi usul, the mistaken mujtahid is not always excused: denial of definitive truths is kufr, denial of speculative matters is fisq.

This passage from Fawatih al-Rahamut lays out the classical Hanafi position on the status of a mujtahid who errs. It distinguishes between two domains: rational matters (ʿaqliyat) and legal matters (sharʿiyat). Within each, it further divides based on whether the error involves denying foundational truths or merely speculative points. Understanding this taxonomy is essential for correctly categorizing mistakes in ijtihad.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two domains of ijtihad
Recognize that the passage first addresses rational matters (ʿaqliyat) such as knowledge of God and attributes, then legal matters (sharʿiyat) derived from revelation.
Rational matters are also called 'aqliyat; legal matters are shari'at.
2
Apply the ruling for a mistaken mujtahid in ʿaqliyat
If the mujtahid denies Islam itself (e.g., rejects the fundamentals of the religion), he is a disbeliever (kafir) and sinful. If he denies a secondary matter (e.g., createdness of the Qur'an, beatific vision), he is sinful but not a disbeliever.
Note the nuance: denial of the entire religion versus denial of a specific theological position.
3
Classify legal matters into definite (qatʿi) and speculative (dhanni)
The passage divides shari'at into necessities (e.g., the five pillars, authority of the Qur'an) and speculative matters (e.g., consensus, solitary hadith, analogy). Denial of the former leads to kufr and sin; denial of the latter only sin.
Here 'definite' (qatʿ) means absolutely certain, admitting no remote possibility.
4
Understand the definition of 'definite' in this context
The text clarifies that by 'definite' it means the more specific sense: that which cannot admit its opposite even with a remote probability. This is stricter than general certainty.
This definition is crucial for distinguishing between necessary and speculative knowledge.
5
Apply the ruling to examples
For instance, denying the obligation of prayer makes one a disbeliever, while denying the authority of analogy makes one only sinful. The text provides these examples.
Practice by testing with other examples: obligation of zakat vs. the hukm of a particular analogy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة: المصيب من المجتهدين أي الباذلين جهدهم في العقليات واحد: وإلا اجتمع النقيضان لكون كل من القدم والحدوث مثلاً مطابقاً للواقع وخلاف العنبري المعتزلي فيه بظاهره غیر معقول بل بتأويل كما سيجيء إن شاء الله تعالى (والمخطىء فيها) أي في العقليات (إن كان نافياً لملة الإسلام فكافر وآثم على اختلاف في شرائطه كما مر) من بلوغ الدعوة عند الأشعرية ومختار المصنف ومضي مدة التأمل والتمييز عند أكثر الماتردية (وإن لم يكن) نافياً لملة الإسلام كخلق) (القرآن أي القول به ونفي الرؤية والميزان. وأمثال ذلك (فآثم لا كافر، ومن ثمة أي من أجل أنه عند مشايخنا غير كافر أوّلوا ما روي (عن) الإمام (الشافعي) رضي الله عنه مثل ما روي عن الإمام أبي حنيفة رضي الله عنه (من تكفير قائله) في أصول الإمام فخر الإسلام. قول أبي حنيفة رضي الله عنه : من قال بخلق القرآن فهو كافر بالله بكفران النعمة) حيث أبى على المنعم ما ليس هو أهله والشرعيات القطعيات كذلك) أي مثل العقليات فمنكر الضروريات الدينية ( منها كالأركان الأربعة التي بنى الإسلام عليها الصلاة والزكاة والصوم والحج (وحجية القرآن ونحوهما كافر آثم، ومنكر النظريات) منها (كحجية الإجماع وخبر الواحد وعدوّا منها حجية القياس أيضاً (آثم فقط) غير كافر، والمراد بالقطع المعنى الأخص ، وهو ما لا يحتمل النقيض ولو احتمالاً بعيداً، ولو غير ناشيء عن الدليل (وقال) أبو مسلم (الجاحظ) المعتزلي (لا إثم على المجتهد المخطىء) الباذل جهده في طلب الحق أصلاً وإن جرى عليه في الدنيا حكم الكفر) لنفيه ملة الإسلام (بخلاف) الكافر (المعاند) الذي يعلم الحقية ثم ينكر عناداً كاليهود وكفار قريش، وكذا من لم يجتهد لمعرفة الحق (وقيل: هو مراد العنبري بقوله : كل مجتهد ولو في العقليات مصيب، قال التفتازاني : إنه أراد من لا يكون نافياً لملة ،الإسلام بل كان من أهل القبلة وكيف يدعي من ينتمي إلى الإسلام
Issue: The correct one among the mujtahids, i.e., those who exert their effort in rational matters, is one; otherwise opposites would unite, because each of pre-eternity and origination, for example, corresponds to reality. The opposition of al-'Anbari al-Mu'tazili in it in its apparent meaning is unreasonable, but by interpretation, as will come, if Allah wills. And the mistaken one in them, i.e., in rational matters, if he is a denier of the religion of Islam, is a disbeliever and sinful, with difference in its conditions as already mentioned, from the reaching of the call according to the Ash'aris and the choice of the author, and the passage of time for reflection and discrimination according to most of the Maturidis. And if he is not a denier of the religion of Islam, like the creation of the Qur'an, i.e., the statement of it, and negation of vision and the scale, and similar to that, then he is sinful, not a disbeliever. Hence, because according to our scholars he is not a disbeliever, they interpreted what was narrated from Imam al-Shafi'i, may Allah be pleased with him, similar to what was narrated from Imam Abu Hanifa, may Allah be pleased with him, of declaring such a speaker a disbeliever, in the principles of Imam Fakhr al-Islam. The statement of Abu Hanifa, may Allah be pleased with him: 'Whoever says the Qur'an is created is a disbeliever in Allah with the disbelief of ingratitude for grace,' because he refused the Giver of grace what is not worthy of Him. And the definite matters of the Sharia are likewise, i.e., like rational matters. So the denier of religious necessities, from them like the four pillars upon which Islam is built: prayer, zakat, fasting, and hajj, and the authority of the Qur'an and similar, is a disbeliever and sinful. And the denier of speculative matters, from them like the authority of consensus and the solitary report, and they exclude from it the authority of analogy as well, is only sinful, not a disbeliever. And by 'definite' is meant the more specific meaning, which is that which does not admit opposite even if a remote probability, even if not arising from evidence. And Abu Muslim al-Jahiz al-Mu'tazili said: 'There is no sin on the mistaken mujtahid,' the one who exerts his effort in seeking truth at all, even if the ruling of disbelief applies to him in this world because he denies the religion of Islam, unlike the obstinate disbeliever who knows the truth and then denies out of obstinacy like the Jews and the disbelievers of Quraysh, and likewise the one who did not exert effort to know the truth. And it is said: this is what al-'Anbari intended by his statement: 'Every mujtahid, even in rational matters, is correct.' Al-Taftazani said: He meant one who is not a denier of the religion of Islam but was among the people of the Qibla. How can one who claims to belong to Islam?
Source pages p. 414

Commentary
This passage is a ruling definition. It systematically categorizes mistaken mujtahids. In rational matters (ʿaqliyat), the correct view is one. A mistaken mujtahid who denies Islam itself is a disbeliever and sinful. Those who deny secondary matters like the createdness of the Qur'an or the beatific vision are sinful but not disbelievers. The author notes that reports of Imam Abu Hanifa declaring such people disbelievers are interpreted metaphorically. For Sharia matters (sharʿiyat), deniers of necessary religious knowledge (e.g., the five pillars, authority of the Qur'an) are disbelievers and sinful; deniers of speculative matters (e.g., consensus, solitary hadith) are only sinful. The term 'definite' (qaṭʿ) here means absolutely certain, admitting no remote possibility. The passage then introduces the view of al-Jahiz, who holds that the mistaken mujtahid is not sinful at all, and the interpretation of al-ʿAnbari's statement that every mujtahid is correct, limited to those within the fold of Islam.

Key Terms
عقليات ('aqliyat): Rational matters, e.g., knowledge of God and attributes.
شرعيات (shar'iyat): Legal matters derived from Sharia.
قطع (qat'): Absolute certainty with no possibility of opposite, even remote.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, what is the ruling on a mujtahid who denies the obligation of prayer?
A) He is a disbeliever and sinful.
B) He is only sinful, not a disbeliever.
C) He is not sinful because he exerted effort.
D) He is a disbeliever but not sinful.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, the mistaken mujtahid is judged based on what he denies: definitive truths lead to kufr, speculative matters to fisq.

The Mu'tazili Objection and the Hanafi Response on the Mistaken Mujtahid
Presenting the Mu'tazili objection and the Hanafi response

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Mu'tazili position (al-Jahiz) that the mistaken mujtahid bears no sin, and evaluate the Hanafi response based on consensus and divine threats.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazili claim of blanket exoneration contradicts the salaf consensus and the Qur'anic warnings against disbelief.

This lesson focuses on the ikhtilaf (scholarly difference) regarding the mistaken mujtahid. After outlining the Hanafi ruling, the passage introduces the view of Abu Muslim al-Jahiz al-Mu'tazili, who holds that the mujtahid who errs is completely excused from sin. The Hanafi response, implied by context, is that such a position is unreasonable because it contradicts the clear texts that threaten punishment for those who reject essential truths.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Mu'tazili claim
Abu Muslim al-Jahiz states: 'There is no sin on the mistaken mujtahid' even if he is judged a disbeliever in this world for denying Islam. Excuse is based on effort.
This is a more radical position: even the one who denies the entire religion is not sinful in the afterlife.
2
Distinguish the Mu'tazili view from the Hanafi
The Hanafi position earlier said that a mistaken mujtahid who denies Islam itself is a disbeliever and sinful. The Mu'tazili says no sin at all.
The difference is not about worldly judgment but about ultimate responsibility before Allah.
3
Examine the Hanafi rebuttal implied by the text
The passage does not directly rebut al-Jahiz, but the context of the salaf consensus (cited earlier) and the threat of punishment for deniers of necessities shows that the Mu'tazili view is rejected.
The rebuttal is based on textual evidence (Qur'an, sunnah) and logical necessity.
4
Analyze the interpretation of al-'Anbari's statement
Al-'Anbari is reported to have said every mujtahid is correct even in rational matters. Al-Taftazani interprets that he meant only those who are not deniers of Islam (ahl al-qibla). This narrows the disagreement.
The Hanafi interpretation attempts to reconcile with al-'Anbari by limiting his claim to non-rejecters.
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
Under the Mu'tazili view, no mistaken mujtahid is punished in the afterlife. Under the Hanafi view, some are punished (those who deny definitive truths). This affects the gravity of ijtihad in theological matters.
This difference matters for how scholars approach takfir and the categorization of sects.

This passage is a ruling definition. It systematically categorizes mistaken mujtahids. In rational matters (ʿaqliyat), the correct view is one. A mistaken mujtahid who denies Islam itself is a disbeliever and sinful. Those who deny secondary matters like the createdness of the Qur'an or the beatific vision are sinful but not disbelievers. إثم (ithm): Sin, moral culpability.

The Fate of Disbelieving Mujtahids: Qat'i Generality of Quranic Verses
Hanafi proof that disbelieving mujtahids are in hell

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that general Quranic verses (amm) are qat'i to evaluate the dispute over the fate of disbelieving mujtahids.

Core Idea
A general Quranic verse includes all disbelievers; ijtihad does not exempt anyone from its scope.

This passage addresses a crucial usul dispute: whether a disbeliever who engages in ijtihad (exertion to derive rulings) can be exempted from eternal punishment. The Hanafi school argues that they are in Hell absolutely, based on the definitive generality of Quranic verses and the consensus of the salaf. Understanding how the Hanafi school uses the principle that 'amm is qat'i' is essential for grasping the school's approach to textual interpretation and the limits of ijtihad in matters of core belief.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify what the Hanafi school holds about disbelieving mujtahids: they are eternally in the Fire, regardless of whether their disbelief arose from ijtihad or obstinacy.
This is the conclusion the passage defends.
2
Identify the opposing claim
Recognize the counterargument: the Quranic verses might be specified (takhsis) to non-mujtahid disbelievers, leaving room for exemption for mujtahids.
This claim relies on the possibility that the general wording does not cover every disbeliever.
3
Examine the Hanafi proof: Ijma and Quran
Note the two proofs: consensus of the salaf on general condemnation, and multiple Quranic verses that use general terms like 'those who disbelieve' without exception.
The consensus strengthens the interpretation that the verses are not subject to specification.
4
Apply the usul rule: Amm is Qat'i
Understand the Hanafi principle that a general (amm) text is definitive (qat'i) in its generality unless proven otherwise. Therefore, the bare possibility of specification is insufficient to override it.
This is the key methodological move: probability alone cannot challenge a definitive text.
5
Conclude: Probability repelled
Combine the qat'i nature of the wording with the multitude of similar verses and hadith, which together eliminate any credible probability of a different meaning.
The passage also distinguishes between broader and narrower senses of qat'i to reinforce this.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
خاتمة في الاجتهاد والتقليد
دخول الكفرة في الجنة لا يتصور منه وهذا مخالف لنقل أكثر الثقات، وإن كان الأليق هذا لنا :أولاً : إجماع السابقين على هذا الخلاف من الصحابة والتابعين ومن تبعهم كلهم (على أنهم من أهل النار (مطلقاً سواء اجتهدوا أم لا، وهذا أولى مما في بعض الكتب أنهم أجمعوا على قتلهم من غير فرق بين أن كفروا اجتهاداً أم عناداً. فإن هذا لا يبطل قولهم، لأنهم قائلون بجريان أحكام الكفر عليهم في هذه الدار (و) لنا (ثانياً) مثل قوله تعالى: ﴿فَوَيْلٌ لِلَّذِينَ كَفَرُوا مِنَ النار﴾ وقوله تعالى: ﴿وَهُمْ عَذَابٌ عَظِيمٌ﴾ [البقرة: ٧] [آل عمران: ١٧٦] [النور: ٢٣] و[الجاثية : ١٠] وقوله تعالى: ﴿وَهُوَ فِي الْآخِرَةِ مِنَ الْخَسِرِينَ﴾ [آل عمران: ٨٥] فإن قيل : لعل الآيات مخصصات بغير المجتهدين منهم قال : (والتخصيص بغير (المجتهد) بل احتماله (مدفوع بالصيغة) فإنها قطعية في العموم كما مر . كذا في شرح «التحرير» و«الحاشية»، وهذا غير واف لأن العام قطع بالقطع بالمعنى الأعم، وهو القاطع عن الاحتمال ناشيء عن الدليل، والذي يهمنا ههنا القطع بالمعنى الأخص القاطع للاحتمال مطلقاً لأن هذه المسألة من هذا القبيل، فالأولى أن يقال : هذه الآيات وردت بهذه الألفاظ وبألفاظ أخرى تؤدي معناها، وقد كثرت غير محصورة، وكذا الأحاديث المؤيدة لها، ومثل هذا ينقطع عن احتمال غير المعنى المفهوم في الخطابات كما لا يخفى (أو) مدفوع (بالإجماع على التعميم فافهم وتثبت
Conclusion on Ijtihad and Taqlid. The entry of disbelievers into Paradise is not imaginable, and this contradicts the transmission of the most reliable narrators, although the more appropriate [view] is this: For us, first: the consensus of the predecessors on this disagreement among the Companions, Successors, and those who followed them all that they are among the people of the Fire absolutely, whether they engaged in ijtihad or not. And this is more appropriate than what is in some books that they agreed on killing them without distinction between whether they disbelieved through ijtihad or obstinacy. For this does not invalidate their statement because they hold that the rulings of disbelief apply to them in this world. And for us, second: like His statement: 'So woe to those who disbelieve from the Fire' and His statement: 'And for them is a great punishment' and His statement: 'And he in the Hereafter will be among the losers.' If it is said: Perhaps the verses are specified to other than the mujtahids among them, he said: 'And specification to other than the mujtahid, rather its probability is repelled by the wording, for it is definitive in generality as previously mentioned.' Thus in the commentary of al-Tahrir and al-Hashiya. And this is insufficient because the general is definitive in the broader sense, which precludes probability arising from evidence, and what concerns us here is the definitive in the narrower sense that precludes probability absolutely because this issue is of that type. So it is better to say: These verses have come with these wordings and with other wordings that convey their meaning, and they are numerous beyond count, and likewise the hadith supporting them, and such a thing cuts off the possibility of a meaning other than the understood meaning in the discourses, as is not hidden. Or it is repelled by consensus on generality. So understand and be firm.
Source pages p. 415

Commentary
This passage is a Hanafi dispute regarding the afterlife fate of disbelievers who were mujtahids. The Hanafi position asserts that they are in the Fire regardless of their ijtihad, based on two proofs: first, the consensus (ijma) of the salaf that disbelievers are eternal inhabitants of Hell, and second, Quranic verses that are general and include all disbelievers. The opposing view claims that the verses might be specified to non-mujtahids, but the Hanafi response refutes this by arguing that the general wording is qat'i (definitive) in its generality, and the multitude of proofs eliminates any possibility of specification. The text also discusses the distinction between qat'i in the broader sense (which allows for probability arising from evidence) and qat'i in the narrower sense (which precludes probability absolutely), concluding that the issue belongs to the latter category.

Key Terms
اجتهاد (ijtihad): The exertion of effort by a qualified scholar to derive a legal ruling from the sources.
قطعي (qat'i): Definitive; a ruling or evidence that admits no probability of the opposite.
عام (amm): A general term that includes all individuals of its category.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the verses use the word 'kafir' which only applies to obstinate disbelievers
B) Because the general wording is qat'i and the multitude of proofs eliminates any probability of specification
C) Because the verses were revealed in Medina and specify Meccan disbelievers
D) Because mujtahids are not mentioned in the verses

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, general Quranic verses are qat'i in their scope; thus, ijtihad cannot exempt anyone from their clear ruling, including disbelieving mujtahids who are eternally in Hell.

The Jahiziyya Argument on Taklif and Ijtihad
Jahiziyya arguments on taklif and Hanafi responses

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Jahiziyya arguments against taklif in beliefs and evaluate the Hanafi responses based on the clarity of evidence and the obligation of correct reasoning.

Core Idea
Error in belief from ijtihad is not excused because divine evidence is clear; deficiency is the mujtahid's own fault.

This passage presents three arguments by al-Jahiz and his followers (a Mu'tazili group) that seem to exempt a mujtahid who errs in belief from sin. The Hanafi school responds by emphasizing the clarity of the proofs for God's existence and prophethood, and the obligation to engage in correct reasoning (nazar sahih). Understanding this debate sharpens your ability to see how usul principles—like the nature of taklif and the role of nazar—are applied to defend core doctrinal obligations.

Guided Path
1
State the Jahiziyya position and their first argument
Identify the first argument: belief is not directly commanded, only ijtihad is required. The Jahiziyya claim the mujtahid has done his duty.
First argument hinges on the premise that belief is not within human power.
2
Analyze the Hanafi response to the first argument
The Hanafi response rebuts that the mujtahid is obligated to correct reasoning (nazar sahih) in definitive matters. Failure indicates deficiency.
The secret (sirr) is that the verses are clear; failure shows blindness due to shortcoming.
3
Examine the second argument and Hanafi response
Second argument: obliging belief contrary to one's ijtihad is impossible. Hanafi response: impossibility is due to the mujtahid's own choice of unsound premises.
If sound premises were used, truth would be attained; the fault lies with the mujtahid.
4
Examine the third argument and Hanafi response
Third argument: Allah only commands ease, so error from ijtihad should be excused. Hanafi response: mercy is shown by making signs clear; error arises from overpowering intellect with desire.
The signs are so clear that no valid excuse remains; deficiency is the cause.
5
Synthesize the Hanafi principle and thamarah al-khilaf
Conclude that in Hanafi usul, the clarity of evidence removes any excuse for error in belief after proper nazar. The practical consequence is that disbelieving mujtahids are sinful and punished.
This upholds accountability in foundational beliefs.

Source Text

الجاحظ وأتباعه (قالوا :أولاً : لا تكليف في أمثال قوله : آمنوا بالذات إلا بالاجتهاد) للإيمان لا بنفس الإيمان لأن الاعتقاد كيف لا يصح التكليف به لكونه غير مقدور للعبد (وقد فعل) المجتهد ما (قلنا : لا نسلم أنه مكلف بمطلق الاجتهاد لأنه مكلف بالنظر الصحيح في المواد القطعية المفروضة، فإذا لم يؤد نظره إلى المطلوب علم أنه مقصر) فيه ولم ينظر فيما يجب أن ينظر فيه، والسر فيه أن الآيات الدالة على وجود الصانع المتقن البريء عن النقصانات جلية مما لا سبيل إلى أن يمتري أو يماري وكذا المعجزات الدالة على النبوة فإذا لم يصل بنظره إلى المطلوب علم أنه مقصر في النظر وعمى عن الآيات الدالة على الوحدانية والرسالة بتقصير منه قطعاً فتثبت (و) قالوا : (ثانياً : التكليف بنقيض (الاجتهاد أي بتصديقه (تكليف بما لا يطاق لأن تصديق خلاف ما ظن أو قطع جهلاً مركباً محال وما أدى إليه ضروري مضطر فيه (فيمتنع) التكليف بتصديق نقيض ما اجتهد فلا يأثم قلنا) : ذلك امتناع بشرع الوصف) وصف النظر في مقدمات غير صحيحة واعتقاد نتيجته ولا يلزم منه الامتناع في زمانه، ولو كان (عادياً) فإنه لو عدم هذا النظر ووجد بدله نظر في مقدمات صحيحة حصل له العلم القطعي خلاف ما حصل بالاجتهاد الكاسد حتى يكون غير مقدور فلا يكلف به (هذا) وقالوا ثالثاً : إن الله تعالى لم يكلف إلا بما هو أيسر، ألا ترى كيف اعتبر مشقة إخراج الرجل من فشرع المسح، واعتبر خوف المرض فشرع التيمم واعتبر مشقة السفر فشرع الإفطار، وهكذا. ورأى حاجة المفاليس، فشرع السلم ومن تكون رحمته بهذه المثابة، واعتبر هذه التضررات العذر عذر يسمع عدم ايصال نظره إلى ما هو الواقع ويوقع في العذاب الدائم من لم يوقع في عذاب تبرد الرجل عند إخراج الرجل من الخف قلنا إيجاب معرفة نفسه أيضاً

يسمع
عذر
من جملة رحمته حتى لا يبقى الإنسان مثل الوحوش، ومن جملة رحمته أن جعل آيات وحدانيته ورسالة رسوله واضحة لا يتطرق فيها شبهة من الشبهات فإذاً لا يمكن أن ينظر الرجل في الآيات من غير مكابرة الهوى على العقل، فلا يؤدي النظر إلى المطلوب الواقعي، وعدم الوصول إليه بقصور منه، فلا العذر في يسمع هذا الواضح، وكيف يسمع وإنه إنما نشأ حماقته استعمال العقل الموهوب له وميله لمكابرة العقل على الهوى، ولا وعدم الحماقة بحال فافهم واستقم كما أمرت فلا عذر لأحد في الجهل بالخالق أصلاً
The Jahiz and his followers said: First: There is no obligation in such commands as 'Believe in the Essence' except through ijtihad... for belief itself, because it is not within the servant's power. And the mujtahid has done what he was obligated to do. We said: We do not accept that he is obligated to ijtihad in general, because he is obligated to correct reasoning in the definitive matters presumed. So if his reasoning does not lead to the desired result, it is known that he is deficient in it and did not consider what he should consider. The secret is that the verses indicating the existence of the perfect and faultless Creator are so clear that there is no way to doubt or dispute, and likewise the miracles indicating prophethood. So if he does not reach the desired result through his reasoning, it is known that he is deficient in reasoning and blind to the verses indicating monotheism and prophethood due to his own deficiency, definitively. So it is established. And they said: Second: Obligation to the opposite of ijtihad, i.e., to believe in it, is an obligation beyond capacity, because believing contrary to what one thought or definitively knew, due to compound ignorance, is impossible. And what ijtihad leads to is necessary and compelled, so it is impossible to oblige belief in the opposite of what one's ijtihad leads to, so he is not sinful. We said: That impossibility is due to his choosing a description, i.e., the description of reasoning in unsound premises and believing its result. But impossibility does not necessarily exist at his time, even if it is habitual. For if this reasoning were absent and replaced by reasoning in sound premises, he would have attained definitive knowledge contrary to what he attained through faulty ijtihad, so that it would be beyond his capacity and he would not be obligated to it. This is it. And they said third: Indeed Allah does not oblige except what is easier. Do you not see how He considered the hardship of removing the foot [from leather socks] and legislated wiping, and considered the fear of illness and legislated tayammum, and considered the hardship of travel and legislated breaking the fast, and so on. And He saw the need of the poor and legislated salam. And He whose mercy is such, considered these harms as excuses, and the excuse of not having his reasoning reach the actual reality would be heard, and would not cause him to fall into eternal punishment. We said: The obligation to know one's self is also... impossible that He does not hear the excuse from His mercy, so that man does not remain like wild beasts. And from His mercy is that He made the signs of His oneness and the message of His Messenger clear, no suspicion can penetrate them. So a man cannot look at the signs without overpowering his intellect with desire, so his reasoning does not lead to the desired reality, and not reaching it is due to his deficiency. So the excuse is not heard for this clear [sign]. And how can it be heard when it arose from his stupidity in using the intellect given to him and his inclination to overpower the intellect with desire? And there is no stupidity in any case. So understand and be steadfast as commanded. So there is no excuse for anyone in ignorance of the Creator at all.
Source pages p. 415

Commentary
This passage presents the three arguments of al-Jahiz and his followers (a Mu'tazili group) against taklif (obligation) in matters of belief without ijtihad, and the Hanafi responses. The first argument: belief is not directly obligated because it is not in one's power; only ijtihad is required. Hanafi response: the mujtahid is obligated to correct reasoning in definitive matters; if he fails, it is due to his own deficiency. The second argument: obliging belief contrary to one's ijtihad is impossible. Hanafi response: the impossibility is due to his own fault in using unsound premises; if he had used sound premises, he would have attained the truth. The third argument: Allah only obliges easy things, so if ijtihad leads to error, the mujtahid should be excused. Hanafi response: the signs of Allah are clear, so error arises from overpowering intellect with desire, which is not excusable. The passage concludes that there is no excuse for ignorance of the Creator at all.

Key Terms
تكليف (taklif): The imposition of a legal obligation by Allah upon a responsible person.
نظر (nazar): Reasoning or intellectual examination aimed at acquiring knowledge.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi response in this passage, why is a mujtahid who errs in belief still sinful?
A) Because he did not ijtihad at all
B) Because he used unsound premises and did not consider the clear evidence
C) Because Allah did not make belief obligatory
D) Because ijtihad is not valid in belief matters

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, the clarity of divine evidence removes any excuse for error in belief after proper nazar; the mujtahid is accountable for deficient reasoning.
