Refuting the Claim that Every Ruling Has a Definitive Proof
Refutation of the claim that every ruling has a definitive proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between qat'i bil ma'na al-a'mm and qat'i bil ma'na al-akhass, and apply the Hanafi argument against the Mu'tazili claim.

Core Idea
Not every ruling is definitively proven; the broader qat'i allows ijtihad, the narrower qat'i is rare.

In this passage, the author refutes the Mu'tazili scholars Bishr al-Hafi and Abu Bakr al-Asamm, who claimed that every ruling has a definitive proof. The Hanafi response hinges on two meanings of 'qat'i': one that does not preclude ijtihad and one that is so certain that error is impossible. The refutation preserves the validity of ijtihad and the reality of speculative evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim
State the claim made by Bishr and Abu Bakr: every ruling has a definitive proof.
The claim is that no ruling is speculative.
2
Distinguish the two senses of qat'i
Define qat'i bil ma'na al-a'mm (definitive in the broader sense: known with certainty but proof allows remote probability) and qat'i bil ma'na al-akhass (definitive in the narrower sense: absolutely certain, no possibility of opposite).
This distinction is key to the Hanafi response.
3
Apply the Hanafi response
For each sense, explain why the claim is either harmless or false. If broader sense, it does not affect sin. If narrower, the claim is false because many rulings are speculative.
The Hanafi response saves the validity of ijtihad.
4
Note the allied groups
Identify the Zahiri and Imami positions. The Imami view is based on belief in an infallible imam, already refuted in the discussion on ijma.
The refutation is comprehensive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يعبأ بتأثيم بشر ، و أبي بكر (الأصم) المعتزلين وهما غير إمامي الهدى من الأولياء الكرام بشر الحافي والشيخ أبي بكر الأصم قدس سرهما. فإنهما قالا لا عن دليل، بل زعما منهما أن كل حكم عليه دليل قطعي البتة إن أراد القطعي بالمعنى الأعم، فلا ينفع، فإنه لا يوجب الإثم وإن أراد القطعي بالمعنى الأخص فالدعوى خلاف الضرورة مكابرة قيل : وعليه الظاهرية (والإمامية أما عند الإمامية فظاهر فإنه لا يخلو على ظنهم الكاسد كل عصر عن إمام معصوم قوله ،قطعي كقول الرسول صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم، وقد أبطلنا رأيهم في أصل الإجماع فتذكر
And no attention is paid to the censure of Bishr and Abu Bakr al-Asamm, the two Mu'tazilites, who are not imams of guidance among the noble saints, Bishr al-Hafi and Sheikh Abu Bakr al-Asamm, may Allah sanctify them. For they said, not based on evidence, but rather they claimed that every ruling has a definitive proof absolutely. If they mean the definitive in the broader sense, it is of no use, for it does not necessitate sin. And if they mean the definitive in the narrower sense, the claim is contrary to necessity and is obstinate. It is said: The Zahiris and the Imamis [follow this view]. As for the Imamis, it is clear, for according to their corrupt conjecture, no era is devoid of an infallible imam whose statement is like the statement of the Messenger, peace be upon him. And we have already refuted their view in the principle of consensus. So remember.
Source pages p. 416

Commentary
This passage refutes the claim made by Bishr al-Hafi and Abu Bakr al-Asamm, two early Mu'tazili scholars, that every ruling has a definitive (qat'i) proof. The text argues that if they mean qat'i in the broader sense (i.e., that the ruling is known with certainty but may be based on evidence that allows for remote probability), then this does not affect the issue because it does not make the mistaken mujtahid sinful. If they mean qat'i in the narrower sense (absolute certainty with no room for any probability), then the claim is false and obstinate, as many rulings are speculative. The passage also notes that the Zahiri and Imami schools hold a similar view, but the Imami view is based on their belief in an infallible imam, which has already been refuted in the discussion on ijma.

Key Terms
قطعي بالمعنى الأعم (qat'i bil ma'na al-a'mm): Definitive in the broader sense: a ruling that is known with certainty but its proof may allow for a remote probability.
قطعي بالمعنى الأخص (qat'i bil ma'na al-akhass): Definitive in the narrower sense: a ruling that is absolutely certain with no possibility of the opposite.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi response in this passage, what is the problem with the claim that every ruling has a definitive proof?
A) It is always true
B) If it means broader qat'i, it does not affect sin; if narrower, it is false
C) It was only claimed by the Imamis
D) It contradicts the Quran

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that many rulings are based on speculative evidence, and the claim that every ruling is definitively proven is either trivial or false.

The View that Every Mujtahid is Correct
Presentation of the view that every mujtahid is correct in ijtihad matters

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the key positions in the dispute over whether every mujtahid is correct and evaluate the attribution of this view to al-Ash'ari.

Core Idea
The claim that every mujtahid is correct is attributed to some scholars, but the Hanafi position holds that only one mujtahid attains the true ruling.

This passage introduces an important dispute in usul al-fiqh: whether every mujtahid in ijtihad matters is correct (musib). The text lists scholars who supposedly hold this view, but also cautions about the strength of the attribution to al-Ash'ari. It sets up the Hanafi position that only one mujtahid is correct. Understanding this ikhtilaf is essential for grasping the epistemological basis of ijtihad.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key claim
Recognize the view presented: 'Every mujtahid in an ijtihad matter is correct'. This applies to rulings where ijtihad is permissible (ma yasughu fihi al-ijtihad).
2
List the scholars attributed to this view
The text mentions Qadi Abu Bakr (al-Baqillani), al-Ash'ari (with a disputed attribution), al-Ghazali, and al-Muzani. Note the geographical distinction: Iraqis vs. Khorasanis regarding al-Ash'ari.
The text hints that the attribution to al-Ash'ari is weak.
3
Contrast with the Hanafi position
Implicitly, the Hanafi position is that only one mujtahid is correct. This is the majority view among Hanafis and others.
The dispute revolves around whether the mujtahid's ruling is the actual divine ruling or not.
4
Analyze the thamarah al-khilaf
If every mujtahid is correct, contradictory rulings are both valid before Allah. If only one is correct, some mujtahids err but are rewarded. This affects how we view the status of ijtihad rulings.
This has implications for the permissibility of following any mujtahid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة: كل مجتهد في المسألة الاجتهادية) أي فيما يسوغ فيه الاجتهاد (مصيب عند القاضي أبي بكر (و) الشيخ (الأشعري) كما قال أهل العراق وقال أهل خراسان: لم يثبت عن الأشعري، كذا في الحاشية ونسب إلى الإمام حجة الإسلام (الغزالي) قدس سره (والمزني) من كبار أصحاب الشافعي رضي الله عنه (وغيرهما) ولا يذهب عليك ما في القول من الإشارة إلى ضعف هذه النسبة فلا تفعل، وهؤلاء ظنوا أن لا حكم الله تعالى في تلك
Issue: Every mujtahid in an ijtihad matter, i.e., in what permits ijtihad, is correct according to Qadi Abu Bakr and Sheikh al-Ash'ari, as the people of Iraq said. And the people of Khorasan said: It is not established from al-Ash'ari. Thus in the Hashiya. And it is attributed to Imam Hujjat al-Islam al-Ghazali, may Allah sanctify him, and al-Muzani, one of the major companions of al-Shafi'i, may Allah be pleased with him, and others. And it should not escape you that there is in the statement an indication of the weakness of this attribution, so do not do it. These people thought that there is no ruling of Allah, the Most High, in those [matters]...
Source pages p. 416

Commentary
This passage introduces a known dispute: whether every mujtahid is correct (musib) in ijtihad matters. The view is attributed to Qadi Abu Bakr al-Baqillani, al-Ash'ari (though the Khorasanis say it is not established from him), al-Ghazali, and al-Muzani. The Hanafi position, which is that only one mujtahid is correct (the view of the majority of Hanafis and others), is implied as the opposing view. The text hints at the weakness of the attribution to al-Ash'ari, cautioning the reader not to be misled. The passage ends mid-sentence, indicating that the discussion continues in the next part of the text.

Key Terms
مصيب (musib): Correct; the view that every mujtahid attains the correct ruling in ijtihad matters.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is definitively established

D) It is rejected by al-Ghazali

Key Takeaway
The view that every mujtahid is correct is attributed to several scholars, but the Hanafi school holds that only one mujtahid is correct, and attributions to key figures like al-Ash'ari must be verified.

The Core Dispute: Taswib vs Takhti' in Ijtihad
Debate on whether truth in fiqh is single or multiple

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on taswib vs takhti' and explain the meaning of 'the mistaken mujtahid is correct'.

Core Idea
Truth in fiqh is one; the mujtahid may hit or miss it, but both are rewarded.

This passage addresses one of the most fundamental debates in usul al-fiqh: when mujtahids disagree, is there a single correct ruling in reality, or are all rulings correct? The Hanafi school adopts the view of takhti' (single truth) while the Mu'tazila lean toward taswib (multiple truths). Understanding this dispute clarifies the reward structure of ijtihad and the nature of legal truth.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two opposing views
Locate the two positions in the text: taswib (every mujtahid is correct) attributed to al-Jubba'i of the Mu'tazila, and takhti' (only one correct ruling) which is the chosen view (al-mukhtar).
The Hanafi position is takhti'.
2
Understand the Hanafi position in detail
The text states that Allah has a single predetermined ruling (haqq mu'ayyan) for each action. The mujtahid who hits it gets two rewards; the one who misses gets one reward for his effort.
This is summarised as: 'the mistaken mujtahid is correct initially (rewarded) but mistaken finally (in reality)'.
3
Grasp the consequence of taswib
If every mujtahid were correct, contradictory rulings would all be true in reality. The text gives the example of wiping the head: Hanafi says quarter, Shafi'i says three hairs, Maliki says whole head. All cannot be the actual divine obligation.
This shows the absurdity of taswib.
4
Recognise the scope of the dispute
The author clarifies that this debate is only in fiqh matters related to actions (الفقهيات المتعلقة بالأعمال), not in doctrinal or usul matters.
The dispute does not apply to matters of belief where truth is obviously one.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

العلم حدوث المعلوم، وذلك لأن الكلام وإن كان قديماً لكن التعلقات بحدوث الاجتهادات فافهم. وبعض منهم قالوا الحكم من الأزل هو ما أدى إليه رأي المجتهد (وعليه الجبائي) من المعتزلة ونسبته إلى جميع المعتزلة لم تصح كيف والحسن أو القبح عندهم في مرتبة الذات) فما فيه حسن واقعي هو الواجب لا يمكن أن يكون محرماً، وما فيه القبح الواقعي فهو محرم لا غير، ولا ينقلب الحسن والقبح الذاتيان. وإذا كان كل مجتهد مصيباً صواباً فمن الله ، وإن يكن خطأ فمن الشيطان) لم يعلم إسناده (ومثله قول ابن مسعود في المفوضة قد تقدّم، لكن لفظ الخطأ ليس إلا في المروي من كتب الأصول (ومنه قول) أمير المؤمنين ( علي لـ أمير المؤمنين (عمر) كرّم الله وجههما (في المجهضة) - بضم الميم وكسر الهاء - وهي المرأة التي أحضرت عند أمير المؤمنين فألقت بطنها، فقال لعبد الرحمن بن عوف : ما تقول؟ قال : إنما أنت مؤدب لا عليك فسأل أمير المؤمنين علياً فقال: (إن اجتهد أخطأ وإن لم يجتهد فقد غشك أي خانك، أرى عليك غرة عبد أو أمة (والضمير لعبد الرحمن بن عوف رضي الله تعالى عنه في رواية البيهقي) كذا في التقرير ، وما في شرح الشرح وغيره : إن اجتهدا - بصيغة التثنية والضمير - لأمير المؤمنين عثمان وعبد الرحمن بن عوف، فلم يثبت (و) لنا :(ثانياً : المجتهد طالب لمطلوب خبري ينظر لأجله (وهو يحتمل الصواب والخطأ) فإنه إن كان مطابقاً ووصل المجتهد إليه فقد أصاب وإلا فقط أخطأ كما في العقليات (والقول) في الجواب بأن مطلوبه ما يغلب على ظنه من الدليل أي شيء كان (لا ينفع) للدفع لأن الظن الحاصل من الدليل إنما يتعلق بالنسبة (الحاكية ،البتة فلا بد من مطلوب خبري قابل للصدق والكذب، فقد وجد الخطأ ممن لم يصل إليه وما) في شرح المختصر أن تلك النسبة ليس التذكير باعتبار الخبر (كونه حكم الله تعالى بل كونه أليق بما عهد من الشارع اعتباره وليس هذا أمراً فقهياً فأقول فيه إنه يجري الكلام في الأليقية فيجوز الخطأ فيها بل يقع لتخالف المجتهدين، فيكون بعض ما أدى إليه رأي مجتهد ليس أليق بما عهد من الشارع اعتبار، وهو حكم الله عندهم وحق (فيلزم على رأيهم أن بعض حكم الله تعالى في الواقع مما لا يليق بما عهد من الشارع اعتباره (في الواقع، وذلك باطل كما ترى فافهم). ولهم أن يقولوا في الجواب عن هذا أن المطلوب كونه حكم الله تعالى في حقي، وهو وإن كان بالنظر إلى نفس المفهوم قابلاً للصدق والكذب، إلا أن يكون صادقاً لكون حكم الله تعالى بحسب الظن فتدبر (واستدل) على المختار (أولاً) : لو كان الحكم الإلهي (تابعاً لظنه لاجتمع النقيضان، لأنه أي المجتهد (بظنه يقطع أنه حكم الله ، والظن باق كما كان من قبل (ضرورة، ولذا صح) له (الرجوع عنه . ولو لم يكن الظن باقياً بل صار مقطوعاً لما صح الرجوع، لأن المقطوع غير محل للاجتهاد والرجوع (فيكون عالماً به) أي بالحكم (حين كونه ظانا) به، فاجتمع الظن
Conclusion on ijtihad and taqlid of incidents. Except that when the opinion of the mujtahid reaches a matter, it is the ruling according to Allah Almighty. And this does not contradict the pre-eternity of speech, as some mistakenly thought, claiming that its pre-eternity necessitates the pre-eternity of the ruling like the pre-eternity of knowledge. That is, just as the pre-eternity of knowledge does not contradict the occurrence of the known, because speech is pre-eternal but its attachments occur with the occurrence of ijtihad. So understand. Some of them said that the ruling from pre-eternity is what the mujtahid's opinion leads to. This is the view of al-Jubba'i from the Mu'tazila. Attributing this to all Mu'tazila is not correct, because for them beauty and ugliness are in the realm of essence. So what has real beauty is the obligatory, it cannot be prohibited, and what has real ugliness is prohibited only, and the essential beauty and ugliness do not change. If every mujtahid is correct, then truth according to them is multiple. So for each one whose ijtihad leads to a ruling, it is the ruling, and when another opinion leads to another, it is the ruling for him. Thus according to the Hanafis, the obligatory (fard) is wiping a quarter of the head in reality, according to the Shafi'i, three hairs, according to the Malikis, wiping the whole head. But they differed whether those rights are equal, as is apparent on that estimation, or one of them is more deserving, which is the saying about the more similar, attributed to some of them. The chosen view is that Allah has a specific ruling in the actions of servants, He obligated seeking it and set up evidence for it, not as some claimed that He did not set up evidence for it, but the servant reaches it by mere agreement. Then this evidence is speculative according to most, definitive according to those who preceded. So whoever hits it gets two rewards: the reward of ijtihad and the reward of hitting the mark. There is no basis for this reward except divine mercy, because his hitting the mark is not a volitional action; what is volitional is his exertion of effort. If it coincidentally happens that his consideration leads to suitable premises, he hits. But the text indicates that he gets two rewards, so it must be accepted. Whoever errs gets one reward for obeying the command of ijtihad by exerting capacity, and no reward for the error. For error, even if not punished, has no reward. This is the meaning of the Hanafi saying that the mistaken mujtahid is correct initially, i.e., rewarded for his action, and mistaken finally. And this view, that the truth is one, is correct according to the four imams, and Imam Abu Hanifa expressed it by saying: Every mujtahid is correct in that he is correct in exerting his effort so that he is rewarded for it, and the truth with Allah is one; he may hit it or not. Know that the mentioned dispute is only in fiqh matters related to actions. So it does not apply to [the claim] that our saying 'Not every mujtahid is correct' is correct or wrong; on both counts there is no universal obligation. As for the first, it is apparent; as for the second, this is an error. So the mujtahid whose consideration reaches it is in error, so the claim is proven. The reason it does not apply is apparent to us. First: The Companions frequently used the word 'error' in ijtihad and did not deny it, and it recurred to the point that experiential knowledge arose that all were agreed upon it. So they declared Ibn Abbas in error for not adopting the doctrine of 'awl, and he considered them in error, saying: 'Whoever wishes, I will invoke the curse of Allah upon him: Allah did not make in property a half and a half and a third.' This wording is in books of inheritance. Said ibn Mansur narrated from Ibn Abbas: 'Do you think that He who counted the sand of 'Alij as a number, made in property a half, a third, and a fourth? It is only two halves, three thirds, and four fourths.' You know that the Hanafis deny this and say there is an internal inconsistency. Moreover, this saying is also far from him, because the proponents of 'awl do not say this. Rather they say: Since Allah did not make the half, third, and fourth absolute, and specified shares in this manner, we know that these are shares for each when alone, and when combined, each decreases proportionally. Then what is narrated from him that he gives priority to the one given precedence and disregards the rest: if he means the one given precedence in inheritance when there is conflict, it is unknown and not intended; if he means the precedence of the spouses and mother and the postponement of sisters and daughters as narrated in al-Bayhaqi and al-Hakim, it is pure arbitrariness. Entitlement for all is by text on equality. Moreover, the veiling of children and sisters by spouses is rejected by reason. The truth is that Ibn Abbas is innocent of such sayings. Among them is the saying of the best of the two truthful ones after the prophets (peace be upon them), Abu Bakr al-Siddiq (may Allah be pleased with him) regarding the kalala: 'I say with my opinion; if it is correct, it is from Allah; if it is wrong, it is from Satan.' Its chain is not known. Similarly, the saying of Ibn Mas'ud regarding al-mufawwada has preceded, but the word 'error' is only in what is narrated in books of usul. Among them is the saying of the Commander of the Faithful Ali to the Commander of the Faithful Umar (may Allah honor both) regarding al-mujihha—with a damma on the mim and kasra on the ha—which is the woman who miscarried before the Commander of the Faithful. He said to Abd al-Rahman ibn Awf: 'What do you say?' He said: 'You are only a disciplinarian, no blame on you.' Then the Commander of the Faithful Ali said: 'If he exerted ijtihad and erred, and if he did not exert ijtihad, he has cheated you, i.e., betrayed you. I see upon you a slave or a slave-girl as blood money.' The pronoun is for Abd al-Rahman ibn Awf in the narration of al-Bayhaqi. So it is in al-Taqrir. What is in Sharh al-Sharh and others: 'If both exerted ijtihad' in dual form, with the pronoun for Uthman and Abd al-Rahman, is not confirmed. Second: The mujtahid is a seeker of an informative sought object for which he considers. It admits of truth and falsehood. If it is matched and the mujtahid reaches it, he is correct; otherwise, he is mistaken, as in intellectual matters. The reply that his sought object is what his dominant conjecture from the evidence leads to, whatever it may be, is not useful to rebut, because the conjecture arising from the evidence only pertains to the relation that informs absolutely. So an informative sought object must exist, subject to truth and falsehood. Error is found in one who did not reach it. What is in Sharh al-Mukhtasar that that relation is not considered as being Allah's ruling, but rather as being more fitting to what is known from the Lawgiver's consideration, and this is not a fiqh matter. I say about it that discussion runs about fittingness, and error is possible in it, indeed occurs due to the disagreement of mujtahids. So some of what a mujtahid's opinion leads to is not more fitting to what is known from the Lawgiver's consideration, and it is Allah's ruling according to them and correct. So it necessarily follows from their view that some of Allah's rulings in reality are not fitting to what is known from the Lawgiver's consideration in reality, and that is false as you see. So understand. They could reply that the sought object is its being Allah's ruling in my case, and although it is subject to truth and falsehood considering the concept itself, yet it is true because Allah's ruling is according to conjecture. So ponder. And [the author] argued for the chosen view, first: If the divine ruling were dependent on his conjecture, then two opposites would be combined. Because the mujtahid by his conjecture is certain that it is Allah's ruling, while the conjecture remains as it was before necessarily. Hence it is valid for him to retract it. If the conjecture did not remain but became certain, retraction would not be valid, because the certain is not subject to ijtihad and retraction. Then he would be knowledgeable of it at the time of being a conjecturer, so conjecture and knowledge are combined.
Source pages p. 417

Commentary
This passage is a usul dispute on whether every mujtahid is correct (taswib) or whether there is a single correct ruling (takhti'). The author begins by stating that when a mujtahid's opinion is reached, that is the ruling for him in relation to Allah, but this does not contradict the pre-eternity of divine speech. He then presents the Mu'tazili view attributed to al-Jubba'i that the ruling from pre-eternity is whatever the mujtahid concludes, but notes that this cannot be generalised to all Mu'tazila because their core principle of objective good and bad prevents it. If every mujtahid is correct, truth would be multiple, leading to contradictory obligations—e.g., Hanafis must wipe a quarter of the head, Shafi'is three hairs, Malikis the whole head—all being correct. The author then declares the chosen view (al-mukhtar): that there is a single predetermined ruling (haqq mu'ayyan) that Allah has obligated to seek and for which He set evidence. This evidence may be speculative (zanni) but is definitive (qat'i) for those who preceded. The mujtahid who hits that ruling gets two rewards (effort and correctness), and the one who misses gets one reward for effort. This is the meaning of the Hanafi statement that the mistaken mujtahid is correct 'initially' (ibtida'an) in that he is rewarded for his action. The author asserts that the truth being one is the correct view according to the four imams, and quotes Abu Hanifa: 'Every mujtahid is correct' meaning in his exertion, but the truth with Allah is one. He then clarifies that this dispute pertains only to fiqh matters regarding actions, not to doctrinal matters. The author provides two main proofs for the Hanafi position. First, he cites historical examples from the Sahaba: they used the term 'error' in ijtihad frequently, as with Ibn Abbas being declared wrong on the issue of 'awl (a case in inheritance where shares exceed the estate), and the well-known statement of Abu Bakr regarding the kalala, and Ali's ruling on the miscarrying woman. These show that the Companions acknowledged the possibility of error in ijtihad, implying a single correct ruling. Second, he presents a logical argument: the mujtahid seeks an informative object (matalub khabari) that admits truth and falsehood. The object of his search must be something in reality, not merely what his conjecture produces. If his conjecture leads him, he may be certain it is Allah's ruling, yet his conjecture remains, allowing retraction. This shows that his state is one of conjecture, not knowledge, and therefore it is possible for him to be mistaken. The author also discusses and refutes the opposing view that the ruling is what is 'more fitting' (aliq) to the Lawgiver's intent, showing that this too admits error and leads to absurdities. Finally, he presents a concise rational proof: if the divine ruling followed the mujtahid's conjecture, then the mujtahid would be both certain and conjecturing simultaneously, which is impossible.

Key Terms
الاجتهاد (al-ijtihād): Exertion of effort by a mujtahid to derive a legal ruling from the evidence
المجتهد (al-mujtahid): One who performs ijtihad; qualified to derive rulings directly from primary sources
الحق المعين (al-ḥaqq al-muʿayyan): The single predetermined ruling that Allah intends for each action
التصويب (al-taṣwīb): The view that every mujtahid is correct in his ruling
التخطئة (al-takhṭiʾa): The view that only one mujtahid is correct in reality, others are mistaken

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position, what does 'the mistaken mujtahid is correct' mean?
A) His ruling is correct in reality even if it contradicts another mujtahid.
B) He is rewarded for his effort of ijtihad, though his ruling may be wrong in reality.
C) He is correct because error is impossible in ijtihad.
D) He is correct only if his ruling matches that of the majority.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that truth in fiqh is one; the mujtahid who errs is rewarded for his effort, but his ruling does not correspond to the single divine ruling.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
intermediate
8 min
Proofs for the Hanafi Position: Companion Practice and Logical Argument
Proofs for takhti' from Sahabi practice and logic

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two main proofs for the Hanafi position (Sahabi practice and logical argument) and evaluate the logical argument.

Core Idea
Companion use of 'error' proves a single truth; certainty and conjecture cannot coexist.

In this portion of the passage, the author presents the evidence that supports the Hanafi view of takhti'. The first proof draws on the practice of the Companions, who frequently labelled ijtihad as 'error', implying a single correct answer. The second proof is a logical argument: if the ruling were dependent on the mujtahid's conjecture, he would simultaneously be certain and conjecturing, which is impossible. Together, these proofs solidify the Hanafi position.

Guided Path
1
Analyse the Sahabi examples
Identify the three Companions cited: Ibn Abbas on 'awl, Abu Bakr on kalala, and Ali on the miscarrying woman. Note how they used the term 'error' in the context of ijtihad.
The author argues that this widespread practice shows they believed in a single truth.
2
Understand the logical argument
The logical proof: if the divine ruling followed the mujtahid's conjecture, then when he is certain (a matter is a ruling), his conjecture would still be present, causing two opposites (certainty and conjecture) to coexist, which is impossible.
This shows that the ruling cannot be merely the mujtahid's opinion; it must be an objective reality.
3
See how these proofs refute taswib
The Sahabi examples demonstrate that error is acknowledged, meaning a single correct ruling exists. The logical argument shows that the ruling cannot be merely subjective.
Together they provide strong support for takhti'.

والكذب، فقد وجد الخطأ ممن لم يصل إليه وما) في شرح المختصر أن تلك النسبة ليس التذكير باعتبار الخبر (كونه حكم الله تعالى بل كونه أليق بما عهد من الشارع اعتباره وليس هذا أمراً فقهياً فأقول فيه إنه يجري الكلام في الأليقية فيجوز الخطأ فيها بل يقع لتخالف المجتهدين، فيكون بعض ما أدى إليه رأي مجتهد ليس أليق بما عهد من الشارع اعتبار، وهو حكم الله عندهم وحق (فيلزم على رأيهم أن بعض حكم الله تعالى في الواقع مما لا يليق بما عهد من الشارع اعتباره (في الواقع، وذلك باطل كما ترى فافهم). ولهم أن يقولوا في الجواب عن هذا أن المطلوب كونه حكم الله تعالى في حقي، وهو وإن كان بالنظر إلى نفس المفهوم قابلاً للصدق والكذب، إلا أن يكون صادقاً لكون حكم الله تعالى بحسب الظن فتدبر (واستدل) على المختار (أولاً) : لو كان الحكم الإلهي (تابعاً لظنه لاجتمع النقيضان، لأنه أي المجتهد (بظنه يقطع أنه حكم الله ، والظن باق كما كان من قبل (ضرورة، ولذا صح) له (الرجوع عنه . ولو لم يكن الظن باقياً بل صار مقطوعاً لما صح الرجوع، لأن المقطوع غير محل للاجتهاد والرجوع (فيكون عالماً به) أي بالحكم (حين كونه ظانا) به، فاجتمع الظن
Conclusion on ijtihad and taqlid of incidents. Except that when the opinion of the mujtahid reaches a matter, it is the ruling according to Allah Almighty. And this does not contradict the pre-eternity of speech, as some mistakenly thought, claiming that its pre-eternity necessitates the pre-eternity of the ruling like the pre-eternity of knowledge. That is, just as the pre-eternity of knowledge does not contradict the occurrence of the known, because speech is pre-eternal but its attachments occur with the occurrence of ijtihad. So understand. Some of them said that the ruling from pre-eternity is what the mujtahid's opinion leads to. This is the view of al-Jubba'i from the Mu'tazila. Attributing this to all Mu'tazila is not correct, because for them beauty and ugliness are in the realm of essence. So what has real beauty is the obligatory, it cannot be prohibited, and what has real ugliness is prohibited only, and the essential beauty and ugliness do not change. If every mujtahid is correct, then truth according to them is multiple. So for each one whose ijtihad leads to a ruling, it is the ruling, and when another opinion leads to another, it is the ruling for him. Thus according to the Hanafis, the obligatory (fard) is wiping a quarter of the head in reality, according to the Shafi'i, three hairs, according to the Malikis, wiping the whole head. But they differed whether those rights are equal, as is apparent on that estimation, or one of them is more deserving, which is the saying about the more similar, attributed to some of them. The chosen view is that Allah has a specific ruling in the actions of servants, He obligated seeking it and set up evidence for it, not as some claimed that He did not set up evidence for it, but the servant reaches it by mere agreement. Then this evidence is speculative according to most, definitive according to those who preceded. So whoever hits it gets two rewards: the reward of ijtihad and the reward of hitting the mark. There is no basis for this reward except divine mercy, because his hitting the mark is not a volitional action; what is volitional is his exertion of effort. If it coincidentally happens that his consideration leads to suitable premises, he hits. But the text indicates that he gets two rewards, so it must be accepted. Whoever errs gets one reward for obeying the command of ijtihad by exerting capacity, and no reward for the error. For error, even if not punished, has no reward. This is the meaning of the Hanafi saying that the mistaken mujtahid is correct initially, i.e., rewarded for his action, and mistaken finally. And this view, that the truth is one, is correct according to the four imams, and Imam Abu Hanifa expressed it by saying: Every mujtahid is correct in that he is correct in exerting his effort so that he is rewarded for it, and the truth with Allah is one; he may hit it or not. Know that the mentioned dispute is only in fiqh matters related to actions. So it does not apply to [the claim] that our saying 'Not every mujtahid is correct' is correct or wrong; on both counts there is no universal obligation. As for the first, it is apparent; as for the second, this is an error. So the mujtahid whose consideration reaches it is in error, so the claim is proven. The reason it does not apply is apparent to us. First: The Companions frequently used the word 'error' in ijtihad and did not deny it, and it recurred to the point that experiential knowledge arose that all were agreed upon it. So they declared Ibn Abbas in error for not adopting the doctrine of 'awl, and he considered them in error, saying: 'Whoever wishes, I will invoke the curse of Allah upon him: Allah did not make in property a half and a half and a third.' This wording is in books of inheritance. Said ibn Mansur narrated from Ibn Abbas: 'Do you think that He who counted the sand of 'Alij as a number, made in property a half, a third, and a fourth? It is only two halves, three thirds, and four fourths.' You know that the Hanafis deny this and say there is an internal inconsistency. Moreover, this saying is also far from him, because the proponents of 'awl do not say this. Rather they say: Since Allah did not make the half, third, and fourth absolute, and specified shares in this manner, we know that these are shares for each when alone, and when combined, each decreases proportionally. Then what is narrated from him that he gives priority to the one given precedence and disregards the rest: if he means the one given precedence in inheritance when there is conflict, it is unknown and not intended; if he means the precedence of the spouses and mother and the postponement of sisters and daughters as narrated in al-Bayhaqi and al-Hakim, it is pure arbitrariness. Entitlement for all is by text on equality. Moreover, the veiling of children and sisters by spouses is rejected by reason. The truth is that Ibn Abbas is innocent of such sayings. Among them is the saying of the best of the two truthful ones after the prophets (peace be upon them), Abu Bakr al-Siddiq (may Allah be pleased with him) regarding the kalala: 'I say with my opinion; if it is correct, it is from Allah; if it is wrong, it is from Satan.' Its chain is not known. Similarly, the saying of Ibn Mas'ud regarding al-mufawwada has preceded, but the word 'error' is only in what is narrated in books of usul. Among them is the saying of the Commander of the Faithful Ali to the Commander of the Faithful Umar (may Allah honor both) regarding al-mujihha—with a damma on the mim and kasra on the ha—which is the woman who miscarried before the Commander of the Faithful. He said to Abd al-Rahman ibn Awf: 'What do you say?' He said: 'You are only a disciplinarian, no blame on you.' Then the Commander of the Faithful Ali said: 'If he exerted ijtihad and erred, and if he did not exert ijtihad, he has cheated you, i.e., betrayed you. I see upon you a slave or a slave-girl as blood money.' The pronoun is for Abd al-Rahman ibn Awf in the narration of al-Bayhaqi. So it is in al-Taqrir. What is in Sharh al-Sharh and others: 'If both exerted ijtihad' in dual form, with the pronoun for Uthman and Abd al-Rahman, is not confirmed. Second: The mujtahid is a seeker of an informative sought object for which he considers. It admits of truth and falsehood. If it is matched and the mujtahid reaches it, he is correct; otherwise, he is mistaken, as in intellectual matters. The reply that his sought object is what his dominant conjecture from the evidence leads to, whatever it may be, is not useful to rebut, because the conjecture arising from the evidence only pertains to the relation that informs absolutely. So an informative sought object must exist, subject to truth and falsehood. Error is found in one who did not reach it. What is in Sharh al-Mukhtasar that that relation is not considered as being Allah's ruling, but rather as being more fitting to what is known from the Lawgiver's consideration, and this is not a fiqh matter. I say about it that discussion runs about fittingness, and error is possible in it, indeed occurs due to the disagreement of mujtahids. So some of what a mujtahid's opinion leads to is not more fitting to what is known from the Lawgiver's consideration, and it is Allah's ruling according to them and correct. So it necessarily follows from their view that some of Allah's rulings in reality are not fitting to what is known from the Lawgiver's consideration in reality, and that is false as you see. So understand. They could reply that the sought object is its being Allah's ruling in my case, and although it is subject to truth and falsehood considering the concept itself, yet it is true because Allah's ruling is according to conjecture. So ponder. And [the author] argued for the chosen view, first: If the divine ruling were dependent on his conjecture, then two opposites would be combined. Because the mujtahid by his conjecture is certain that it is Allah's ruling, while the conjecture remains as it was before necessarily. Hence it is valid for him to retract it. If the conjecture did not remain but became certain, retraction would not be valid, because the certain is not subject to ijtihad and retraction. Then he would be knowledgeable of it at the time of being a conjecturer, so conjecture and knowledge are combined.
Source pages p. 417

Commentary
This passage is a usul dispute on whether every mujtahid is correct (taswib) or whether there is a single correct ruling (takhti'). The author begins by stating that when a mujtahid's opinion is reached, that is the ruling for him in relation to Allah, but this does not contradict the pre-eternity of divine speech. He then presents the Mu'tazili view attributed to al-Jubba'i that the ruling from pre-eternity is whatever the mujtahid concludes, but notes that this cannot be generalised to all Mu'tazila because their core principle of objective good and bad prevents it. If every mujtahid is correct, truth would be multiple, leading to contradictory obligations—e.g., Hanafis must wipe a quarter of the head, Shafi'is three hairs, Malikis the whole head—all being correct. The author then declares the chosen view (al-mukhtar): that there is a single predetermined ruling (haqq mu'ayyan) that Allah has obligated to seek and for which He set evidence. This evidence may be speculative (zanni) but is definitive (qat'i) for those who preceded. The mujtahid who hits that ruling gets two rewards (effort and correctness), and the one who misses gets one reward for effort. This is the meaning of the Hanafi statement that the mistaken mujtahid is correct 'initially' (ibtida'an) in that he is rewarded for his action. The author asserts that the truth being one is the correct view according to the four imams, and quotes Abu Hanifa: 'Every mujtahid is correct' meaning in his exertion, but the truth with Allah is one. He then clarifies that this dispute pertains only to fiqh matters regarding actions, not to doctrinal matters. The author provides two main proofs for the Hanafi position. First, he cites historical examples from the Sahaba: they used the term 'error' in ijtihad frequently, as with Ibn Abbas being declared wrong on the issue of 'awl (a case in inheritance where shares exceed the estate), and the well-known statement of Abu Bakr regarding the kalala, and Ali's ruling on the miscarrying woman. These show that the Companions acknowledged the possibility of error in ijtihad, implying a single correct ruling. Second, he presents a logical argument: the mujtahid seeks an informative object (matalub khabari) that admits truth and falsehood. The object of his search must be something in reality, not merely what his conjecture produces. If his conjecture leads him, he may be certain it is Allah's ruling, yet his conjecture remains, allowing retraction. This shows that his state is one of conjecture, not knowledge, and therefore it is possible for him to be mistaken. The author also discusses and refutes the opposing view that the ruling is what is 'more fitting' (aliq) to the Lawgiver's intent, showing that this too admits error and leads to absurdities. Finally, he presents a concise rational proof: if the divine ruling followed the mujtahid's conjecture, then the mujtahid would be both certain and conjecturing simultaneously, which is impossible.

Key Terms
الاجتهاد (al-ijtihād): Exertion of effort by a mujtahid to derive a legal ruling from the evidence
المجتهد (al-mujtahid): One who performs ijtihad; qualified to derive rulings directly from primary sources
الحق المعين (al-ḥaqq al-muʿayyan): The single predetermined ruling that Allah intends for each action
التصويب (al-taṣwīb): The view that every mujtahid is correct in his ruling
التخطئة (al-takhṭiʾa): The view that only one mujtahid is correct in reality, others are mistaken

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is the logical argument used by the Hanafis to refute taswib?
A) If the ruling were dependent on conjecture, then the mujtahid would be both certain and conjecturing simultaneously.
B) The Sahaba never used the word 'error' in ijtihad.
C) The Imam must be infallible.
D) All mujtahids are correct because consensus is impossible.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi position of takhti' is supported by the Companions' practice of calling ijtihad error and a logical argument against subjective rulings.

The Equality-of-Evidence Argument: Taswīb vs. Takhtī'
Presenting and rebutting the equality-of-evidence argument

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the second proof for taswīb and explain the Hanafi rebuttal that subjective preponderance suffices for a mujtahid's correct opinion.

Core Idea
Subjective preponderance makes each mujtahid's ruling true for him; objective truth belongs only to one.

The musawwibun argue that if two proofs are equal, they cancel, making truth arbitrary; if one is stronger, only that one is correct. The Hanafi response refines this: preponderance depends on the mujtahid's own estimation, so each can be correct in his own sight. This lesson unpacks the argument and its rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proof
The musawwibun claim that if two mujtahids' proofs are equal, they cancel out; if not equal, only the preponderant proof yields correctness.
This is a logical dilemma: either equal (both false) or unequal (only one correct).
2
Locate the Hanafi rebuttal
The rebuttal states that preponderance (rujhān) is subjective—dependent on the mujtahid's own opinion. Each mujtahid considers his proof preponderant, so both can be true for them personally.
The key term is 'tabi' li-zann al-mujtahid'.
3
Analyse the annotator's refinement
The annotator adds that even if the preponderance is mistaken in reality, it does not entail error in the ruling, because the mujtahid's opinion leads to certainty (in his own mind).
This strengthens the rebuttal by severing the link between objective error and subjective correctness.
4
Determine the practical consequence
In practice, a judge must follow the preponderant view among conflicting mujtahids, but each mujtahid remains valid in his own ijtihad.
This is the thamarah al-khilaf: the ruling for the judge vs. the individual mujtahid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و) استدل على المختار :(ثانياً : إن تساوى دليلاهما أي الاجتهادين المتخالفين تساقطا، فالحكم بالحقية تحكم ، وإلا فالصواب هو الراجح فلا حقية لكل (وأجيب بأن الرجحان) عندهم (تابع لظن المجتهد فيقول : كلاهما راجحان في ظن المجتهدين، فمدلولاهما مظنونان لهما، فهما حقان عليهما في نفس الأمر (أقول: على أن الخطأ الرجحان لا يستلزم الخطأ في الحكم فلا نسلم أن الصواب هو الراجح، (لأن الرجحان ولو ظنه غير مطابق للواقع يفضي إلى الظن، وهو إلى القطع، تدبر.
And (they) argued for the chosen position: Secondly: If the two proofs (i.e. of the two differing ijtihads) are equal, they cancel each other, so judging truthfulness is arbitrary; otherwise the correct is the preponderant, so no truthfulness for each. And it was answered that preponderance, according to them, depends on the mujtahid's opinion; so they both say: both are preponderant in the opinion of the mujtahids, so their implications are matters of opinion for them, so both are true for them in reality. I say: But error in preponderance does not entail error in the ruling, so we do not concede that the correct is only the preponderant. Because preponderance, even if its opinion does not correspond to reality, leads to opinion, and that leads to certainty. Reflect.
Source pages p. 420

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof of the musawwibun (those who hold that every mujtahid is correct) and its rebuttal by the mukhatti'un (those who hold only one is correct). The proof is: if the two mujtahids' proofs are equal in strength, they cancel out, making a claim of absolute truth arbitrary; if one is stronger, then only that stronger one is correct, so not both. The rebuttal: preponderance (tarjih) is based on the mujtahid's subjective opinion; each mujtahid considers his proof preponderant, so for each, his conclusion is correct in his own sight. The annotator (I say) adds a refinement: even if the preponderance is mistaken in reality, it still leads to a valid opinion that in turn leads to certainty for the mujtahid, so error does not necessarily follow.

Key Terms
الرجحان (al-rujhān): Preponderance or superiority of one proof over another in the mujtahid's estimation.
حقية (ḥaqqiyya): Truthfulness or correctness of a ruling in itself.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi rebuttal of the equality-of-evidence argument, what does 'preponderance' (rujhān) depend on?
A) Objective strength of the evidence
B) The mujtahid's subjective opinion
C) Consensus of the community
D) The ruling of the judge

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi rebuttal shows that subjective preponderance allows each mujtahid to be correct in his own estimation, while objective truth belongs only to one.

The Hanafi Position on Ijtihad: Only One Mujtahid is Correct
Refuting musawwibun arguments and affirming that only one mujtahid is correct.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi argument for the mukhattiun position and rebut the musawwibun objections using the dalil from the hadith and logical reasoning.

Core Idea
Only one mujtahid attains the truth; the mistaken is rewarded and his ruling binds him.

This passage addresses a foundational dispute in usul al-fiqh: when mujtahids differ, is every opinion correct in reality (musawwibun) or only one (mukhattiun)? The Hanafi school firmly adopts the latter view. You will examine the musawwibun's two main arguments and the Hanafi responses, including a key example about acting on ijtihad despite error.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
State the musawwibun view (every mujtahid is correct in reality) and the Hanafi mukhattiun view (only one is correct).
2
Understand the first objection
The musawwibun argue that if only one is correct, the mistaken mujtahid would be required to act on both error and truth, which is contradictory.
3
Follow the Hanafi response
Hanafis choose the alternative that acting on error is obligatory when commanded by the Lawgiver, citing the consensus case of a hidden decisive proof.
4
Analyze the second argument
The hadith 'My companions are like stars...' is cited as proof that following any companion is guidance, implying all are correct.
5
Examine the rebuttal
Hanafis respond that the hadith is weak (zanni al-thubut) and that acting on error can be guidance if the Lawgiver commands it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يترجح أحد الاجتهادين فيعمل. وأما عند هؤلاء فاجتهاد كل مطابق للواقع فيجتمع الحل والحرمة، أو الحل لاثنين في زمان واحد قطعاً، بخلاف ما نحن فيه. فإنّ أحد الاجتهادين خطأ في الواقع، وإنما كان لنا العمل بكل انفراداً، وإذا اجتمعا فبترجيح آخر فافهم. (وأما الجواب بأن الحل إنما هو بالإضافة إلى أحدهما والحرمة بالإضافة إلى الآخر فلا استحالة فيه (كما في الشرح، فأقول : لا يخفى وهنه، لأن ذلك : أي حل المرأة والحرمة متعاكس) فعند أحدهما الفعل له فيها حلال وللآخر حرام، وعند الآخر بالعكس، والمفروض أن كليهما صوابان مطابقان للواقع فيجتمع الحل لهما في زمان واحد في الصورة الثانية (تدبر) المصوّبون (قالوا أولاً : لو كان المصيب (واحداً من المجتهدين المختلفين (وجب النقيضان على المخطىء إن وجب الصواب عليه أيضاً كما وجب ما أدى إليه اجتهاده ( وإلا يجب الصواب (وجب العمل بالخطأ وحرم بالصواب) وهو خلاف المعقول والأظهر أن يقال: إن وجب على الخاطيء العمل بالصواب فهو تكليف بما لا وسع له فيه وبما لا علم له به، وإلا وجب العمل بالخطأ وحرم بالصواب (وأجيب باختيار) الشق الثاني ومنع بطلان (التالي) وهو وجوب العمل بالخطأ كما فيما لو خفي عليه (قاطع موجود واجتهد بخلافه وجب العمل به إلى ظهوره مع أنه خطأ (اتفاقاً) والسر فيه أن الأمر برحمته الأزلية سهل وهي قصر العمل على الظن مطابقاً أو غير مطابق، ومطمح نظره إلى الإخلاص والإطاعة بالقلب فافهم. (و) قالوا (ثانياً) قال صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم (أصحابي كالنجوم فبأيهم اقتديتم اهتديتم عزي إسناده إلى ابن عدي، فإنه دل على أن الاقتداء بكل هداية فيكون صواباً لأن الاقتداء بالخطأ ضلال، وأجيب بأنه هدي من وجه لإيجاب الشارع العمل به فلا نسلم أن الاقتداء بالخطأ مطلقاً ضلال، بل بالخطأ الذي لم يوجب الشارع العمل به، وهذا الخطأ قد أوجب العمل به، فالاقتداء به هداية، ثم الحديث قد ضعف أيضاً.
One of the two ijtihads is preponderant, so one acts. But according to these (the musawwibun), each ijtihad corresponds to reality, so the permissible and the forbidden would coexist, or the permissible for two individuals at one time is definitively impossible, unlike our case. Indeed, one of the two ijtihads is wrong in reality, and it is only for us to act on each individually, and if they coincide, then by another preponderance, so understand. (As for the answer that permissibility is relative to one and prohibition relative to the other, there is no impossibility in it (as in the commentary), I say: its weakness is not hidden, because that—i.e., the permissibility of the woman and the prohibition—are opposite). So for one of them, the action is permissible for him regarding her, and for the other forbidden, and for the other the opposite, and the assumption is that both are correct and corresponding to reality, so the permissibility for both at one time in the second scenario (ponder). The musawwibun (said first: If the correct one is only one of the differing mujtahids, then two contradictories would be incumbent on the mistaken if correctness is also incumbent on him as what his ijtihad led to is incumbent, or if correctness is not incumbent, then acting on error becomes obligatory and acting on correctness becomes forbidden, which is contrary to reason. And the more apparent is to say: If it is incumbent on the mistaken to act on correctness, it is a burden beyond his capacity and without knowledge, otherwise acting on error becomes obligatory and acting on correctness forbidden. And it was answered by choosing the second alternative and denying the invalidity of the consequent, which is the obligation to act on error, as in the case where a decisive proof is hidden from him and he exerts ijtihad contrary to it, it is obligatory to act on it until its appearance even though it is wrong, by consensus. The secret is that the command is easy by His eternal mercy, and it is limited to acting on opinion, whether it corresponds or not, and his aim is sincerity and obedience of the heart, so understand. (And) they said (secondly): He (peace be upon him) said: 'My companions are like stars; whichever of them you follow, you will be guided.' Its chain is attributed to Ibn Adi, for it indicates that following each is guidance, so it is correct because following error is misguidance. And it was answered that it is guidance in one respect due to the Lawgiver making it obligatory to act on it, so we do not concede that following error is absolutely misguidance, but rather error that the Lawgiver did not make obligatory to act on; and this error He has made obligatory to act on, so following it is guidance. Then the hadith is also weak.)
Source pages p. 421

Commentary
This passage is part of the conclusion on ijtihad and taqlid, discussing the dispute between the musawwibun (those who hold every mujtahid is correct) and the mukhattiun (those who hold only one is correct, the Hanafi view). The author presents the musawwibun's objections and refutes them. First, they argue that if only one mujtahid is correct, then the mistaken mujtahid would be required to act on both correctness and error simultaneously, which is impossible. The Hanafi response is that the mistaken mujtahid is only required to act on his own ijtihad, not on the actual correctness, because the Shari'ah commands acting on one's best effort. The example is given of a person who is unaware of a decisive proof and exerts ijtihad contrary to it; he must act on his ijtihad until the proof appears, even though it is wrong. This is a consensus. The second objection uses the hadith about the companions being like stars, but the Hanafis respond that the hadith is weak and that following error can be guidance when the Lawgiver commands it. The overall Hanafi position is that only one mujtahid hits the actual truth, but each is rewarded for his ijtihad.

Key Terms
المصيب (al-musīb): The one who attains the correct ruling in reality according to Allah.
المخطئ (al-mukhti’): The one whose ijtihad does not correspond to reality but is still rewarded.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view presented in this passage, what is the ruling for a mujtahid whose ijtihad does not correspond to reality?
A) He is sinful and must abandon his ruling.
B) He is rewarded and must act on his ijtihad until evidence appears.
C) He is neither rewarded nor punished.
D) He must follow the correct ruling from another mujtahid.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school affirms that ijtihad can be mistaken, yet the mujtahid is rewarded and his ruling binds him, preserving the singularity of divine law.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
intermediate

The Legal Status of Rebels (Bughat): Consensus as Proof and Hanafi Rulings
Ruling on rebels based on dialogue of Ali with Khawarij

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that a rebel (baghi) with a corrupt interpretation (ta'wil fasid) is not liable for worldly penalties, citing the consensus of the Companions as proof, and distinguish the positions of Abu Hanifa/Muhammad vs. Abu Yusuf on inheritance.

Core Idea
Consensus of Companions exempts rebel from worldly penalties; inheritance depends on intention.

In this lesson, we examine the legal status of rebels (bughat) in Hanafi fiqh, focusing on the dialogue of Imam Ali with the Khawarij and the subsequent consensus of the Companions. The passage from Fawatih al-Rahmut illustrates how the Companions, through their actions and agreements, established that rebels who fight with a corrupt interpretation are not subject to worldly liability for damages or retaliation. This principle then informs the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding whether such a rebel inherits from his just victim. By studying this passage, you will learn how ijma serves as a binding proof and how it is applied to derive specific rulings.

Guided Path
1
Narrate the historical context
Understand the background of Ali's debate with the Khawarij and the Battle of Nahrawan.
The Khawarij rejected arbitration after Siffin.
2
Identify the key claims of the Khawarij
Their objections to Ali's arbitration included using human judgment, fighting without taking captives/booty, and erasing his title.
3
Analyze Ali's refutation using Quranic verses
Ali used verses on hunting (5:95) and marital arbitration (4:35) to prove that arbitration can be valid in more important matters.
This shows how Quranic proofs can refute corrupt interpretations.
4
Extract the legal principle from the Companions' consensus
The Companions did not hold the Nahrawanis liable for damages, establishing that rebels with ta'wil fasid are exempt from worldly penalties.
The consensus is stated: 'ala hadha in'aqada ijma' al-sahabah.
5
Apply the principle to the inheritance dispute
Understand the ikhtilaf: Abu Hanifa and Muhammad allow inheritance if the rebel deemed blood lawful; Abu Yusuf denies it because the act is forbidden.
The preferred view is that of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, aligning with the consensus.

Conclusion on ijtihad and taqlid. The sunnah of Allah? Indeed the religion of Allah, and Allah the Most High said: 'The judgment is only for Allah' [6:57, 10:40,67]. I said: And what? They said: He fought and did not take captives nor booty; if they were disbelievers, their wealth would have been permissible to him, and if they were believers, their blood would have been forbidden to him. I said: And what? They said: And he erased his name from 'Commander of the Faithful'; if he is not the Commander of the Faithful, then he is the commander of the disbelievers. I said: Do you see if I recite to you from the clear Book of Allah and relate to you from His Prophet what you do not doubt, will you retract? They said: Yes. I said: As for your saying that he appointed men as arbiters in the religion of Allah, Allah the Most High says: 'O you who believe, do not kill game while you are in ihram... until His saying: 'judged by two just men among you' [5:95], and He said concerning the woman and her husband: 'And if you fear a breach between them, send an arbiter from his family and an arbiter from her family' [4:35]. I adjure you by Allah, is the judgment of men in preserving their blood and lives and reconciling between them better, or in a rabbit worth a quarter of a dirham? They said: In the judgment of their blood and reconciling between them. He said: Have I come out of this? They said: O Allah, yes. And as for your saying that he fought and did not take captives nor booty, do you revile your mother Aisha and consider lawful from her what you consider lawful from others? Then you have disbelieved. And if you resolve that she is not your mother, then you have disbelieved and left Islam. Indeed Allah the Most High says: 'The Prophet is closer to the believers than their selves, and his wives are their mothers' [33:6]. And you are vacillating between two errors, so choose whichever you wish. Have I come out of this? They said: O Allah, yes. And as for your saying that he erased his name from 'Commander of the Faithful', indeed the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, called Quraysh on the day of Hudaybiya to write a treaty between him and them, and he said: Write: This is what Muhammad, the Messenger of Allah, has agreed. They said: By Allah, if we knew that you are the Messenger of Allah, we would not have prevented you from the House nor fought you, but write: Muhammad ibn Abdullah. So he said: By Allah, I am the Messenger of Allah even if you deny me. Write, O Ali: Muhammad ibn Abdullah, and the Messenger of Allah was better than Ali. Have I come out of this? They said: O Allah, yes. So from them retracted, and four thousand remained and were killed. And if one does not have protection [in numbers], the legal judgment is applied to him: he is killed for killing and prevented from inheritance, and he is held liable for what he destroyed of our wealth. And with protection, the legal judgment does not apply except for sin in the Hereafter, so he is not liable for what he destroyed of life or wealth to the just people, if he is captured or repents, like a harbi after embracing Islam is not liable for what he destroyed of the Muslim's wealth during the time of brigandage. And upon this the consensus of the Companions was established, for they did not hold liable the people of Nahrawan nor the killers of the Commander of the Faithful Uthman, may Allah be pleased with him, for anything. And the just person inherits from his rebel heir if he kills him following the Imam, by agreement, because this killing is an act of worship, which does not entail deprivation of inheritance. And similarly the opposite, i.e., the rebel inherits if he kills his just heir, but only if he considered his blood lawful according to Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad, may Allah have mercy on them, contrary to Imam Abu Yusuf. Twenty thousand. The world. Yusuf: because he committed forbidden killing, so he is penalized by deprivation, and rebellion only increased him in evil and sin. Their argument: since their corrupt interpretation was taken into consideration in not executing retaliation in this world by consensus of the Companions, it is known that he did not intend by his killing any punishment except the Fire, so he is not penalized by deprivation. Understand. And he does not own his wealth by way of booty, because the abode is shared in Islam. And upon this the Commander of the Faithful Ali and all the Companions, may Allah be pleased with them, agreed. And it is narrated that the Commander of the Faithful Ali, when he defeated Talha and his companions, commanded his crier to proclaim: Do not kill one who advances nor one who retreats, do not open a door, and do not consider lawful any private part nor any wealth. And there is an element of obscurity in this, because the justice of Talha is definitive, and what he did was only by ijtihad, not by a corrupt interpretation. Therefore, when its corruption became apparent, he retracted from his position and renewed the pledge before he died, as in al-Isti'ab and others. So it is possible that the proclamation was because he was rewarded in the act, and an ijtihad act does not nullify the inviolability of wealth. Understand. So it is better to argue that the Commander of the Faithful forbade taking spoils of the wealth of the people of Nahrawan who did what they did due to a corrupt interpretation.
Source pages p. 423

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the legal status of rebels (bughat) in Hanafi fiqh, derived from the dialogue of Imam Ali with the Khawarij and the consensus of the Companions. The author first narrates Ali's debate with the Khawarij, who objected to his arbitration in the Battle of Siffin. Ali refutes them using Quranic verses that permit arbitration in hunting and marital disputes, and then exposes the inconsistency in their stance on the status of Aisha and the Prophet's treaty at Hudaybiya. After the retraction of 4,000 Khawarij, the remaining fought and were killed. The key ruling: a rebel without military strength (man'a) is subject to worldly penalties — killed for causing death, barred from inheritance, and liable for damages. But if the rebel has strength, he is only accountable for sin in the Hereafter, not for damages or retaliation, if captured or repentant. This is based on the consensus of the Companions who did not impose liability on the Nahrawanis or the killers of Uthman. The just person inherits from his rebel relative if he kills him following the imam, as it is an act of worship. Conversely, the rebel inherits from his just relative if he killed him while deeming his blood lawful (according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, but not Abu Yusuf). Abu Yusuf argues that the killing is forbidden, so deprivation applies. Abu Hanifa and Muhammad argue that since the rebel's corrupt interpretation was considered by the Companions in not exacting retaliation, his only punishment is the Fire, not deprivation of inheritance. The passage also notes that Ali did not allow taking spoils from Talha's forces because Talha's ijtihad was not corrupt; he later retracted. The better argument is that Ali forbade spoils from the Nahrawanis due to their corrupt interpretation.

Key Terms
باغي (baghi): A rebel who opposes the just Imam with a corrupt interpretation but is not treated as an ordinary criminal.
تأويل فاسد (ta'wil fasid): An interpretation that is legally invalid and corrupt, but still may excuse worldly liability if sincerely held.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad, if a rebel (baghi) kills his just relative while considering his blood lawful, what is the ruling on inheritance?
A) The rebel is deprived of inheritance because the killing is forbidden.
B) The rebel inherits because his corrupt interpretation excuses worldly punishment.
C) The just person's estate goes to the state.
D) Inheritance is suspended until the rebel repents.

Key Takeaway
The Companions' consensus exempts rebels with corrupt interpretations from worldly liability, but the inheritance ruling depends on whether the rebel considered the blood lawful.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
advanced

Prohibition of Taqlid for a Mujtahid After Ijtihad and the Dispute on Before Ijtihad
Usul dispute on taqlid before and after ijtihad

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the consensus on a mujtahid's taqlid after ijtihad, explain the majority Hanafi view on taqlid before ijtihad, and evaluate the key arguments for and against using the methods of usul al-fiqh.

Core Idea
A mujtahid who knows Allah's ruling cannot abandon it for another's opinion; the dispute is whether he may follow others before he has performed ijtihad.

This passage from Fawatih al-Rahamut addresses a critical issue in Hanafi usul: what happens when a qualified scholar (mujtahid) arrives at a ruling? Can he then follow another scholar? The text establishes a consensus that after ijtihad and knowledge of the ruling, taqlid is prohibited. The more debated question is whether taqlid is allowed before ijtihad. We will walk through the various positions, proofs, and counter-arguments, focusing on the Hanafi majority view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the consensus and its scope
The passage begins by stating that after a mujtahid performs ijtihad and knows the ruling, he is prohibited from taqlid by consensus. Understand the reason: he has attained knowledge (or at least strong conjecture) of Allah's ruling and cannot abandon it for another's opinion.
This is a rare case of ijma' (consensus) on a usul matter.
2
Examine the objection regarding Abu Hanifa's ruling on judges
An objection is raised: Did not Imam Abu Hanifa allow a judge to enforce a ruling contrary to his own ijtihad? The passage clarifies that this does not break the consensus because enforcement of a judgment does not imply the judge personally considers it permissible to follow another's opinion.
The judge may be bound by procedural rules; personal belief is distinct from judicial enforcement.
3
Map the opinions on taqlid before ijtihad
List the reported positions: (1) Absolutely permissible, (2) Absolutely prohibited (majority), (3) Prohibited unless fear of missing the time, (4) Prohibited for fatwa but permitted for personal action, (5) Narrations from Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, (6) Al-Shafi'i and al-Jubba'i allow only if the followed person is a Companion, (7) Some restrict to Abu Bakr and Umar.
The majority (الأكثر) is the Hanafi position that prohibits absolutely.
4
Analyze the majority's three proofs and the responses
The majority gives three proofs: (a) Permissibility requires evidence, and the default is prohibition; (b) Ijtihad is the original, taqlid is a substitute (like wudu and tayammum); (c) If allowed before ijtihad, it would be allowed after, but it is not. For each, the text provides a counter-reply. Evaluate these arguments and identify which proof is strongest in the Hanafi tradition.
The analogy to tayammum is a key Hanafi argument; the response challenges the substitutive relationship.
5
Assess the evidence from hadith and conclude
The opposing school cites the hadith 'My Companions are like stars' to justify taqlid of companions. The Hanafi response: (a) the hadith is weak, (b) even if authentic, it only addresses laymen, not jurists, and (c) it only permits following companions, not others. Thus, the majority view that taqlid before ijtihad is prohibited for a mujtahid stands.
The conclusion (خاتمة) reinforces that following non-companions is not permitted.

Source Text

يقول رجل : أتباع الشافعي قالوا قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم: أصحابي كالنجوم فبأيهم اقتديتم اهتديتم قلنا هذا الحديث مضعف ولو ثبت فخطاب للمقلد كما مر على أنه مستلزم لجزء المدعي وهو جواز Issue: The mujtahid after his ijtihad and knowledge of the ruling is prohibited from taqlid in it by consensus, because what he knows to be Allah's ruling he does not abandon for anyone's statement. If you say: Did not Imam Abu Hanifa rule the enforcement of a judgment contrary to his own ijtihad? So where is the consensus? He said: What is correct from Abu Hanifa's school is that if the mujtahid judge gives a judgment contrary to his own opinion while remembering his own ijtihad, according to one narration it is enforced, according to another it is not; and in the case of forgetfulness, it is enforced by one narration, contrary to his two companions in both aspects. So it does not contradict the consensus because enforcement upon the assumption of the act does not entail its being permissible. Thus the prohibition is agreed upon. Know that what is mentioned in al-Hidayah and others is that the fatwa is according to their (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad's) opinion in both situations. As for before ijtihad, it is said: absolutely permissible; and the majority said: absolutely prohibited; and it is said: prohibited unless one fears missing the opportunity, and Ibn Surayj held this, and I think it is an interpretation of the previous statement. And it is said: prohibited with respect to giving fatwa, not for his own practice. From Imam Abu Hanifa there are two narrations: in one it is permissible, in another it is not. From Imam Muhammad: he may follow one who is more knowledgeable than him, which is a kind of ijtihad, for it does not occur except by reflecting on men to know the most knowledgeable. Imam al-Shafi'i and al-Jubba'i the Mu'tazili said: it is permissible if the one followed is a Companion. And it is said: permissible if a Companion or a Successor. And it is said: one may follow the two shaykhs, the best of the truthful after the prophets, Abu Bakr al-Siddiq and Amir al-Mu'minin Umar, may Allah be pleased with them, only, not others. The majority hold prohibition. First: permissibility is a legal ruling, so it requires evidence, because a ruling is not established without evidence, and it is not found, so permissibility does not exist, because whatever there is no evidence for in the Law must be negated legally. It is answered: the evidence is original permissibility, for it is known from the Law that what has no evidence for it is permissible, unlike your prohibition, which must have specific evidence. Second (by the majority): ijtihad is the original like wudu, and taqlid is a substitute like tayammum; the substitute is not resorted to except when the original is impossible, so taqlid is not chosen except when ijtihad is impossible. It is said: we do not concede that taqlid is a substitute; rather each of them is an original for action, as in Sharh al-Mukhtasar. I say: it is clear that this is mere debate, for one who is capable of certainty is prohibited from conjecture; similarly, one capable of stronger conjecture is prohibited from weaker conjecture, and the distinction is arbitrary. The conjecture obtained through ijtihad is stronger than the conjecture from another's statement; rather, conjecture from another's statement may not be obtained at all in taqlid. And the substitutiveness is established by the general meaning of Allah's statement: 'So take heed, O people of insight' (59:2); for heed is obligatory by this text upon everyone, so permitting taqlid is a substitute for it and a concession for ease. Third (by the majority): if taqlid were permissible before ijtihad, it would be permissible after, since there is no preventing factor except the ability of ijtihad, and that exists in both cases, and permissibility after is invalid by consensus, so likewise before. It is answered: that the preventing factor is ijtihad is denied; rather the preventing factor is the actual attainment of the stronger of the two conjectures; indeed it involves abandoning what he considers to be a divine ruling. A man says: The followers of al-Shafi'i said: The Messenger of Allah said: 'My Companions are like the stars; by whichever of them you follow, you will be guided.' We say: This hadith is weak, and if it were established, it is addressed to the follower, as previously mentioned, and it entails only part of the claim, i.e., the permissibility of following a Companion, not the other part. Conclusion: In ijtihad and taqlid, it is the impermissibility of following other than a Companion.
Source pages p. 426

Commentary
This passage is a comprehensive discussion on the ruling of taqlid for a mujtahid. It begins by stating the consensus that a mujtahid, after performing ijtihad and knowing the ruling, is prohibited from following anyone else's opinion. This is because he has attained certain knowledge of Allah's ruling through his own ijtihad, and he cannot abandon it for another's statement. An objection is raised regarding Imam Abu Hanifa's position on a judge who gives a ruling contrary to his own ijtihad. The author clarifies that this does not contradict the consensus because enforcement of the judgment under certain circumstances does not imply that the act of following another is permissible for the judge; it is a separate matter. The fatwa in such cases is according to the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). Then the discussion shifts to the situation before a person has performed ijtihad. There are several opinions: some say it is absolutely permissible, the majority say it is absolutely prohibited, some say prohibited unless there is fear of missing the time (like in the case of a time-sensitive matter), and others distinguish between giving fatwa and acting for oneself. Imam Abu Hanifa has two narrations, and Imam Muhammad allows following someone more knowledgeable as a form of ijtihad. Al-Shafi'i and al-Jubba'i allow it only if the one followed is a Companion, and some restrict it to the two caliphs. The majority provide three proofs for prohibition: first, permissibility is a legal ruling requiring evidence, and lacking evidence, the default is prohibition; second, analogy comparing ijtihad to wudu (original) and taqlid to tayammum (substitute) so the substitute is only allowed when the original is impossible; third, if it were allowed before ijtihad, it would be allowed after, but it is not. Replies are given to each: the default is permissibility not prohibition; the analogy is contested because both ijtihad and taqlid could be considered originals; and the third is answered by saying the preventer is not the ability of ijtihad but the actual presence of a stronger conjecture. The passage concludes that even if the hadith about companions being like stars is accepted, it only proves following companions, not others. Thus, the overall ruling is that a mujtahid cannot do taqlid after knowing the ruling, and regarding before ijtihad, the majority view prohibits it absolutely.

Key Terms
الاجتهاد (al-ijtihad): The exertion of a qualified scholar's mental faculties to derive a legal ruling from the primary sources (Quran, Sunnah, Ijma', Qiyas).
التقليد (al-taqlid): Following the opinion of another scholar without knowledge of the evidence or the method of derivation.
المجتهد (al-mujtahid): A scholar who possesses the qualifications to perform ijtihad independently.
البدل (al-badal): A substitute; in this context, taqlid is considered a substitute for ijtihad when ijtihad is impossible.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the consensus ruling on taqlid for a mujtahid after he has performed ijtihad and knows the ruling?
A) It is permissible under certain conditions.
B) It is prohibited by consensus.
C) It is allowed only if he follows a Companion.
D) It is allowed only in matters of fatwa.

Key Takeaway
A mujtahid who has arrived at a ruling through ijtihad cannot abandon it for another's opinion; before ijtihad, the majority Hanafi view prohibits taqlid absolutely, requiring the scholar to exert his own ijtihad.
