A Mujtahid Cannot Hold Contradictory Rulings
Explaining that contradictory rulings from a mujtahid are impossible without retraction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the principle that a mujtahid cannot hold contradictory rulings and identify the causes of apparent contradictions in transmitted reports.

Core Idea
A mujtahid's ruling is based on his conjecture; two contradictories cannot both be conjectured, so contradiction is impossible without retraction.

This passage tackles a foundational usul principle: can a mujtahid have two contradictory statements on the same issue? The answer is no, because each statement must be based on his personal conjecture (zann), and two contradictories cannot both be conjectured. The apparent contradictions seen in transmission (like Imam al-Shafi'i's 'two sayings') are explained by retraction, narrator error, or contextual differences (e.g., azima vs. rukhsa). Understanding this principle is key to evaluating conflicting transmissions from a single mujtahid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core claim
The passage states that a mujtahid cannot have two contradictory statements on the same issue (or two issues with a common element). The reason: his statement is only his if his conjecture (zann) attaches to it. Two contradictories cannot both be objects of conjecture.
This is a logical necessity based on the nature of zann.
2
Understand the retraction exception
The only way to have two statements is if he retracts the first; the second replaces the first, so no actual contradiction remains.
Retraction (ruju') is a legitimate change of opinion.
3
Analyze the 'ikhtilaf al-riwaya'
Differences in narration from a single mujtahid are not from him but from narrators. Four possible causes: (a) mistake in hearing, (b) one narrator unaware of retraction while another knows, (c) two answers from different methods (qiyas vs. istihsan), (d) two statements from different perspectives (azima vs. rukhsa).
These explanations preserve the principle while accounting for transmitted reports.
4
Resolve the case of al-Shafi'i
When al-Shafi'i reportedly said he had two statements in seventeen issues, the author offers reinterpretations: (i) the school having two statements, (ii) texts being equivocal, (iii) the theory of takhyir (choice) when evidence is balanced. The author dismisses some interpretations as weak.
The point is to show that al-Shafi'i's own view does not contradict the principle.
5
Note the link to the musawwiba theory
The takhyir interpretation resembles the view that every mujtahid is correct (musawwiba). The passage ends with a hint about the implications if truth is one.
This connects to a broader debate in usul about the nature of ijtihad.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة لا يصح لمجتهد في مسألة أو مسألتين ولا فرق بينهما لوحدة الجامع قولان للتناقض لأنه لا يكون قولاً له إلا إذا تعلق ظنه به فلو كان له قولان متناقضان كان النقيضان مظنونين إلا بالرجوع عن أحدهما وحينئذ لا تناقض فإن قلت: كيف يصح هذا وقد اختلفت الروايات عن مجتهد واحد قال واختلاف الرواية ليس منه لأنه هذا الاختلاف من جهة الناقل وخطئه وذلك إما لغلط في السماع أو لعدم العلم بالرجوع منه وعلم الآخر فروى كل بحسب علمه أو يكون هناك جوابان أحدهما جواب القياس والآخر جواب الاستحسان فنقل كل ما علم أو يكون هناك قولان من جهتين كالعزيمة والرخصة فكل نقل واحداً واحداً وإذ قال الشافعي رحمه الله تعالى في سبع عشرة مسألة فيها قولان فحمل على أن للعلماء قولين لا أن له قولين وفائدة الحكاية إياهما عدم الإجماع والتسوية أو حمل على احتمالهما عندهم لتعادل الدليلين فيحتمل قولان ولا يخفى بعده فإنه ليس مقتضى التعادل والمتبادر أن هناك قولين موجودين أو حمل على أن لي فيها قولان بناء على القول بالتخيير عند التعادل فالمكلف مخير في العمل بأيهما شاء لا بناء على الوقف عند التعادل ولا يذهب عليك أنه أشبه بالمصوبة أي بمذهبهم فإنه على تقدير أن يكون الحق واحداً يلزم
Issue: It is not valid for a mujtahid to have two contradictory statements in the same issue or two issues, and there is no difference between them due to the unity of the common element, because a statement is only his statement if his conjecture attaches to it. So if he had two contradictory statements, then the two contradictories would both be conjectured, except by retraction from one, and then there is no contradiction. If you say: How can this be correct when narrations differ from a single mujtahid? He said: The difference in narration is not from him, for this difference arises from the narrator and his error, either due to a mistake in hearing or lack of knowledge of his retraction while another knows, so each narrated according to his knowledge; or there are two answers: one is the answer of qiyas, the other the answer of istihsan, so each narrated what he knew; or there are two statements from two perspectives like azima and rukhsa, so each narrated one. When al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him, said that in seventeen issues he had two statements, it is interpreted to mean that the scholars have two statements, not that he has two statements. The benefit of reporting them is the absence of consensus and equal status; or it is interpreted as possible for them due to equivalence of evidence, so two statements are possible, but its distance from the truth is clear, because equivalence does not necessitate two actual statements, but the immediate understanding is that there are two existing statements; or it is interpreted that he had two statements based on the opinion of choice when evidence is equivalent, so the legally responsible person is free to choose whichever he wishes, not based on suspension when evidence is equivalent. It should not escape you that this resembles the school of those who uphold correctness (musawwiba), i.e., their school, for if the truth is one, then ... (incomplete).
Source pages p. 427

Commentary
This passage addresses the issue of a mujtahid appearing to have contradictory statements on the same legal issue. The principle is that a mujtahid cannot validly hold two opposite opinions simultaneously because his statement is based on his personal conjecture (zann). If he has two contradictory statements, that would entail that both contradictories are conjectured, which is impossible. The only way this can happen is if he retracts one of them; then there is no contradiction. The apparent contradiction arises from external factors: (1) narrators may have made mistakes in transmission, e.g., some heard a statement before retraction and others after; (2) the mujtahid may have given two different answers, one based on qiyas (analogy) and one on istihsan (equity), so narrators reported what they heard; (3) the statements may relate to different contexts, such as azima (strict ruling) and rukhsa (concession). The case of Imam al-Shafi'i, who reportedly had two statements in seventeen issues, is analyzed. It is not that he personally held two contradictory opinions; rather, scholars attribute two statements to the school, or the texts were equivocal, or it's based on the theory of takhyir (choice) when evidence is balanced, where one is free to choose. The author notes that this resembles the musawwiba school, which holds that every mujtahid is correct. The passage ends with a discussion on the implications if truth is single.

Key Terms
النقيضان (al-naqidan): Two contradictories that cannot both be true or both false; here referring to contradictory legal rulings.
الرجوع (al-ruju'): Retraction of a previous legal opinion by the mujtahid, which resolves the apparent contradiction.
اختلاف الرواية (ikhtilaf al-riwaya): Difference in narrations attributed to a mujtahid, often due to transmission errors or incomplete knowledge.
العزيمة والرخصة (al-azima wa al-rukhsa): The strict ruling and the concessionary ruling; a mujtahid may have both based on different conditions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the primary reason a mujtahid cannot have two contradictory statements in the same issue?
A) The principle of ijma prevents it.
B) Because his statement is based on his conjecture, and two contradictories cannot both be conjectured.
C) Because he always has to follow his teacher.
D) Because the Quran forbids contradiction.

Key Takeaway
A mujtahid cannot hold contradictory opinions simultaneously; apparent contradictions are resolved through retraction, narrator error, or contextual differences.

The Hanafi Position on Taqlid for Mujtahids
Presents arguments for and against taqlid with Hanafi responses

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two arguments for absolute taqlid and explain the Hanafi response to each, demonstrating how the school distinguishes between laypeople and mujtahids in the application of Quran 21:7.

Core Idea
A mujtahid cannot follow another's fatwa because his own ijtihad yields stronger conjecture.

This passage from 'Fawatih al-Rahamut' presents a key dispute in usul al-fiqh: can a mujtahid perform taqlid of a non-Sahabi? The Hanafi school holds it impermissible, while some scholars permit it absolutely. We will examine the two proofs cited by the proponents of absolute taqlid and the Hanafi refutations, focusing on how the school reads the Quranic verse and evaluates epistemic strength.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling: taqlid of a non-Sahabi is impermissible. Note that this applies specifically to those capable of ijtihad.
2
Examine the first opposing argument
The opponents cite Quran 21:7: 'Ask the people of remembrance if you do not know.' They argue that this includes anyone before ijtihad, thus permitting taqlid for all.
3
Analyse the Hanafi textual response
The Hanafi response distinguishes the addressee: the verse is for laypeople (those lacking knowledge and intellect), not for mujtahids. The condition 'if you do not know' restricts the command to non-scholars.
This is a classic example of takhsis (specification) of a general command.
4
Examine the second opposing argument
The opponents claim that both ijtihad and fatwa produce only conjecture (zann), so there is no reason to allow ijtihad while forbidding taqlid.
5
Analyse the Hanafi epistemic response
The Hanafi response asserts that the conjecture from one's own ijtihad is stronger than conjecture from following another's fatwa; thus they are not equal in benefit.
This reflects the Hanafi emphasis on the strength of personal legal reasoning.
6
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: a mujtahid may not rely on another's fatwa; he must exert his own ijtihad.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
خاتمة في الاجتهاد والتقليد وهو عدم جواز تقليد غير الصحابي . المجوزون مطلقاً قالوا: أولاً قال الله تعالى: ﴿فَسَلُوا أَهْلَ الذِّكْرِ إِن كُنتُمْ لَا تَعْلَمُونَ﴾ [الأنبياء: ٧] وهو قبله لا يعلم فدخل قبل الاجتهاد فيمن لا يعلم والجواب : الخطاب مع المقلدين بدليل إن كنتم لا تعلمون لأن المعنى : إن لم تكونوا أصحاب علم وعقل فاسألوا أقول : وبدليل فاسألوا بصيغة الأمر إذ لا وجوب للتقليد على المجتهد اتفاقاً هذا وقالوا : ثانياً : غاية الاجتهاد الظن، وهو حاصل بالفتوى فلا وجه لمنع أحدهما دون الآخر وأجيب بأن الظن الحاصل باجتهاده أقوى فليس هو والتقليد سواء في الفائدة.
Conclusion on ijtihad and taqlid: It is the impermissibility of taqlid of non-Sahabi. Those who permit absolutely said: First, Allah said: 'So ask the people of the Remembrance if you do not know.' [21:7] And the one addressed before it does not know, so it includes the one before ijtihad among those who do not know. The answer: The address is to those who imitate, evidenced by 'if you do not know' because the meaning is: If you are not people of knowledge and intellect, then ask. I say: And evidenced by 'ask' in the imperative form, as there is no obligation of taqlid upon the mujtahid by consensus. This. And they said: Secondly, the ultimate result of ijtihad is conjecture, and it is obtained by fatwa, so there is no reason to prevent one without the other. And it was answered that the conjecture obtained by his ijtihad is stronger, so he and the one doing taqlid are not equal in benefit.
Source pages p. 427

Commentary
This passage opens a conclusion on ijtihad and taqlid. The author states the position that taqlid of a non-Sahabi is impermissible. Then he presents the arguments of those who permit taqlid absolutely (المجوزون مطلقاً). Their first proof is the Quranic verse commanding those who do not know to ask the people of remembrance. They argue that this includes the one who has not engaged in ijtihad, so it permits taqlid. The Hanafi response is that this verse addresses laypeople (مقلدين), not mujtahids, as indicated by the condition 'if you do not know' meaning if you lack knowledge and intellect. Additionally, the imperative 'ask' does not impose an obligation of taqlid on the mujtahid by consensus. Their second argument is that ijtihad produces only conjecture (zann), and fatwa also produces conjecture, so there is no basis to allow ijtihad while forbidding taqlid. The Hanafi answer is that the conjecture from one's own ijtihad is stronger and therefore not equal to the conjecture from taqlid in terms of benefit. The Hanafi position is clear: a mujtahid cannot rely on taqlid.

Key Terms
تقليد (taqlid): Following the opinion of another mujtahid without knowing the evidence.
اجتهاد (ijtihad): Exertion of effort to derive a legal ruling from the sources.
ظن (zann): Conjecture or probabilistic knowledge, as opposed to certainty.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Mujtahids
B) Laypeople
C) Both
D) Non-Sahabi

Key Takeaway
Taqlid of a non-Sahabi is impermissible for a mujtahid because his own ijtihad yields stronger conjecture than following another's fatwa.

The Impossibility of Two Contradictory Opinions from a Mujtahid
Usul rule: impossibility of two contradictory opinions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a mujtahid cannot have two contradictory opinions and distinguish between actual contradiction and apparent contradiction due to transmission errors, takhyeer, or taraddud.

Core Idea
A mujtahid's single conjecture cannot hold both opposites; apparent contradictions are explained by transmission errors or hesitation.

You may have heard that some scholars, like Imam al-Shafi'i, had two opinions on certain issues. Does this contradict the logical principle that a mujtahid at a given moment can only hold one conjecture? In this lesson, we explore the Hanafi usul position that true contradiction is impossible, and we analyze the various interpretations that resolve the apparent conflict.

Guided Path
1
Understand the core principle
A mujtahid's opinion (qawl) is defined as a ruling to which his conjecture (zann) attaches. Two contradictory opinions would require both contraries to be conjectured simultaneously, which is logically impossible.
This is based on the nature of zann: it is a preponderant belief, and two opposite beliefs cannot coexist.
2
Recognize the objection from transmitted rulings
Objection: contradictory rulings are reported from the same mujtahid. The passage explains that such differences arise from the narrator's error or from different contexts (qiyas vs. istihsan, 'azima vs. rukhsa).
The transmitter may have missed a retraction or only heard one answer.
3
Analyze the six interpretations for al-Shafi'i's two opinions
The passage lists six ways to interpret reports of al-Shafi'i having two opinions in seventeen issues. These range from attributing the opinions to different scholars to explaining them as hesitation (taraddud).
Each interpretation preserves the principle that a mujtahid cannot have contradictory opinions.
4
Evaluate the preferred explanation
The preferred view (chosen by Imam al-Haramayn and al-Ghazali) is that such reports indicate taraddud (hesitation) between two possible rulings, not actual adoption of both. This avoids the logical problem.
Taraddud is a state of uncertainty, not a definitive opinion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة: لا يصح لمجتهد في مسألة أو مسألتين ولا فرق بينهما لوحدة الجامع قولان: للتناقض لأنه لا يكون قولاً له إلا إذا تعلق ظنه به فلو كان له قولان متناقضان كان النقيضان مظنونين إلا بالرجوع عن أحدهما وحينئذ لا تناقض. فإن قلت: كيف يصح هذا وقد اختلفت الروايات عن مجتهد واحد قال واختلاف الرواية ليس منه لأنه أي هذا الاختلاف من جهة الناقل وخطئه، وذلك إما لغلط في السماع أو لعدم العلم بالرجوع منه وعلم الآخر، فروى كل بحسب علمه، أو يكون هناك جوابان: أحدهما: جواب القياس، والآخر: جواب الاستحسان، فنقل كل ما علم أو يكون هناك قولان من جهتين كالعزيمة والرخصة، فكل نقل واحداً واحداً. وإذ قال الشافعي رحمه الله تعالى في سبع عشرة مسألة فيها قولان، فحمل على أن للعلماء قولين لا أن له قولين وفائدة الحكاية إياهما عدم الإجماع والتسوية، أو حمل على احتمالهما عندهم لتعادل الدليلين فيحتمل قولان ولا يخفى بعده فإنه ليس مقتضى التعادل والمتبادر أن هناك قولين موجودين أو حمل على أن لي فيها قولان بناء على القول بالتخيير عند التعادل فالمكلف مخير في العمل بأيهما شاء لا بناء على الوقف عند التعادل ولا يذهب عليك أنه أشبه بالمصوبة أي بمذهبهم، فإنه على تقدير أن يكون الحق واحداً يلزم التخيير بين ما هو حكم الله تعالى وبين ما ليس حكمه وقد مر في بحث التعارض، ثم إنه لا يستقيم على قول المصوبة أيضاً، فإنهم إنما قالوا بالتصويب إذا أدّى إليه رأي مجتهد وظنه، فإذا تعارض دليلان عند مجتهد فما تعلق ظنه بطرف فلا يكون الآخر صواباً فليس في التخيير إلا تخيير بين ما يحتمل أنه حكم الله، وما ليس حكمه فافهم. أو حمل على أن في الزمان المتقدم لي قولان فللمجتهد في المذهب الترجيح بالمرجحات أو حمل على أنه يختلج لي قولان، وحاصله التردد بينهما واختاره الإمام إمام الحرمين والإمام حجة الإسلام الغزالي قدس سره وهذا التوجيه أشبه بالصواب.
Issue: It is not valid for a mujtahid [to have] in one issue or two issues, and there is no difference between them due to the unity of the common ground, two opinions: because of contradiction, for a statement is only considered his opinion if his conjecture attaches to it. So if he had two contradictory opinions, both contraries would be conjectured, unless he retracts one, in which case there is no contradiction. If you say: How can this be correct when narrations differ from a single mujtahid? He said: The difference in narration is not from him, because this difference arises from the transmitter and his error. That is either due to a mistake in hearing, or because of lack of knowledge of his retraction while another knows it, so each narrates according to his knowledge. Or there are two answers: one is the answer of qiyas, the other the answer of istihsan, so each transmitted what he knew. Or there are two opinions from two perspectives, like determination (azima) and concession (rukhsa), so each transmitted one of them. And when al-Shafi'i (may Allah have mercy on him) had two opinions in seventeen issues, it is interpreted that there are two opinions among scholars, not that he himself had two opinions. The benefit of narrating them is to preclude consensus and to allow both. Or it is interpreted as the possibility of both opinions in their view due to the equivalence of the two proofs, yielding two possible opinions, though this is unlikely because the equivalence does not entail the actual existence of two opinions. Or it is interpreted that he had two opinions in the sense of choosing between them (takhyeer) when the proofs are equivalent, so the mukallaf is free to act on whichever he wishes, not based on suspension (waqf). And it should not escape you that this is more akin to the musawwiba (those who hold that every mujtahid is correct) because if the truth is one, then choosing between what is Allah's law and what is not His law would be necessary. This has been discussed in the section on contradiction. Moreover, it is not valid even according to the musawwiba, for they only say correctness when the mujtahid's opinion and conjecture lead to that. If two proofs conflict before a mujtahid, his conjecture attaches to one side, so the other is not correct. In takhyeer there is only a choice between what may be Allah's law and what is not His law. So understand. Or it is interpreted that in an earlier time he had two opinions, so the mujtahid in the madhhab can weigh them with preponderating factors. Or it is interpreted that he is conflicted (khatalaja) between two opinions, essentially hesitation (taraddud) between them. This is chosen by Imam al-Haramayn and Imam al-Ghazali (may Allah sanctify their souls), and this explanation is closer to correctness.
Source pages p. 427

Commentary
This passage addresses the impossibility of a single mujtahid having two contradictory opinions on the same issue. The basic principle is that a mujtahid's opinion is only his opinion if his conjecture (zann) attaches to it; if he has two contradictory opinions, that would mean both opposites are conjectured, which is logically impossible unless he retracts one. The passage then addresses the objection that contradictory rulings are reported from the same mujtahid. It offers several explanations: (1) The contradiction is due to errors in transmission—narrators may have mistaken his retraction, or transmitted different aspects such as qiyas vs. istihsan, or 'azima vs. rukhsa. (2) Regarding Imam al-Shafi'i's two opinions in seventeen issues, six possible interpretations are given: (a) the two opinions belong to different scholars, not to al-Shafi'i himself; (b) they represent two possibilities due to equivalent proofs; (c) they reflect the theory of takhyeer (choice) when proofs are balanced; (d) they are from different time periods; (e) they are simply hesitation (taraddud) in his mind. The preferred view is the last: that it indicates hesitation, not actual contradictory opinions. This accords with the principle that a mujtahid's conjecture leads to a single ruling at a given time.

Key Terms
قولان (qawlan): Two opinions; in this context, contradictory views attributed to one mujtahid.
تردد (taraddud): Hesitation or uncertainty between two positions, not actual adoption.
تخيير (takhyeer): Choice; the view that when proofs are equivalent, the mukallaf may choose between two rulings.
ظن (zann): Conjecture; the preponderant belief of a mujtahid that a certain ruling is correct.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why cannot a mujtahid have two contradictory opinions on the same issue at the same time?
A) It would violate consensus
B) Both contraries cannot be conjectured simultaneously
C) A mujtahid can only have one opinion
D) It would lead to infinite regress

Key Takeaway
A mujtahid cannot have two contradictory opinions; apparent conflicts are resolved by transmission errors, contextual differences, or hesitation (taraddud).

The Hanafi Position on Taqlid in Matters of Belief (Aqliyat)
Discusses the permissibility of taqlid in matters of belief (aqliyat)

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on taqlid in matters of belief (aqliyat) and evaluate the opposing arguments using the text's dalil and rebuttal.

Core Idea
Taqlid in beliefs cannot yield certainty, but a muqallid's faith may still be valid.

This passage addresses a crucial usul al-fiqh dispute: can a person rely on taqlid for core theological beliefs? The Hanafi school, while affirming the validity of a muqallid's faith, holds that taqlid alone does not produce the certainty required. You will explore the arguments from both sides and the nuanced Hanafi resolution.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contested issue
Determine exactly what is being disputed: the permissibility of taqlid in rational matters (aqliyat) such as the existence of God.
The issue is not about the validity of faith, but about the method of acquiring it.
2
State the Hanafi position and its primary evidence
The Hanafi majority holds that taqlid is not permissible (la yajuz), based on the definitive consensus (ijma) that knowledge of God must be certain and cannot rely on fallible reports.
The evidence is from ijma, a dalil naqli.
3
Understand the opposing view
Some scholars (al-'Anbari, some Shafi'is) permit taqlid, arguing that if reflection (nazar) were obligatory, the Companions would have explicitly engaged in it and commanded it, but no such transmission exists.
Their proof is based on the absence of transmitted practice.
4
Follow the dialectical exchange
The Hanafi response counters that the Companions did reflect, but their reflection was not formal theological argumentation; it was a natural, immediate certainty arising from pure hearts and witnessing revelation. Hence, the absence of explicit command does not negate reflection.
The Hanafi reply redefines nazar as 'what brings tranquility to the heart'.
5
Recognize the resolution
The text concludes that reflection is not universally obligatory; it depends on the individual. Those who have attained certainty through other means—like a pure disposition or imitation of a reliable source—need not engage in formal reflection.
Thus, the Hanafi position is nuanced: taqlid is prohibited as a method, but if it results in genuine faith, that faith is valid.

Source Text

خاتمة في الاجتهاد والتقليد (مسألة: لا يجوز التقليد في العقليات كوجود الباري : ونحوه عند الأكثر) وهذا لا ينافي مر من إجماع الأئمة الأربعة على صحة إيمان :المقلد : لأن التقليد الممنوع هو أن يعتمد على قول النظر الصحيح ليحصل العلم، وهذا إنما يتم لو قلنا: إن كل تقليد يفيد العلم، بل مجوز والتقليد لعل مطمح نظرهم أن التقليد قد يفيد الجزم، ثم إن كان مقلداً لمن له علم واقعي يكون جزمه علماً، فقد حصل بتقليد بعض الكملة العلم القاطع، ولعل إنكار هذا إنكار للقطعيات بل الحق في هذا المقام الواجب تحصيل العلم فقط، وهو قد يحصل بصفاء السريرة ضرورة، فلا يجب عليه النظر قطعاً كما حكى الشيخ الأكبر صاحب «الفتوحات» قدس سره نظر كان أولى، وقد يحصل بنظر ، وهذا أكثر في الرجال، فالنظر واجب عليهم فقط فافهم وتثبت مجوزو التقليد :(قالوا : لو وجب النظر لفعله الصحابة وأمروا به، وذلك منتف وإلا نقل كما في الفروع بل دواعي هذا النقل (تحرير الأدلة على قواعد المنطق) فإنه ليس التصديق موقوفاً إلا للبعض الأندر (بل) المراد (ما يفيد الطمأنينة) للقلب بحيث يصير أطوع لحصول العلم (كما قال الأعرابي : البعرة تدل على البعير، وأثر الاقدام على المسير، فسماء ذات أبراج وأرض ذات فجاج، أما يدلان على اللطيف الخبير وحاصل الجواب منع عدم نظرهم بالكلية، ومنع عدم ،أمرهم فإن المراد بالنظر قدر ما يطمئن به القلب بحصول التصديق، وهو كان حاصلاً لهم كما يشهد به قصة الأعرابي، وأما دعوى أنه لو لم يكن منهم لزم الجهل بوجه فغير ظاهر الصحة فإن كمال استعدادهم وتوجه الرسول صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم لعلهما يوجبان العلم الضروري.
Conclusion on ijtihad and taqlid. Issue: Taqlid in rational matters, such as the existence of the Creator and the like, is not permissible according to the majority. This does not contradict what has passed of the consensus of the four imams on the soundness of the faith of the muqallid, because the prohibited taqlid is that one relies on the statement of another and says according to his statement, and this does not negate the soundness of faith and affirmation if it occurs due to his statement, but it is firmly established such that if his statement disappeared, he would remain upon affirmation. So understand. Al-'Anbari and some Shafi'is said: It is permissible. And a group said: Taqlid is obligatory and reflection is prohibited. Our proof: Definitive consensus on the obligation of knowledge of Allah, His attributes, and the message of His Messenger, which is definitive, matching affirmation that does not admit any doubt, and it is not attained by taqlid due to the possibility of the informant lying since he is not infallible. Yes, if after taqlid a definitive affirmation occurs, as a decisive result from famous premises, then one becomes heedless and the affirmation remains, it is acceptable. And because contradiction would follow from the taqlid of two persons regarding the world's temporal creation and pre-eternity, for example, since each would be knowledge; therefore correct reflection is necessary to attain knowledge. This is only valid if we say that every taqlid yields knowledge, but it is merely permissible. Perhaps the aim of their view is that taqlid may yield certainty; then if one imitates someone who has real knowledge, his certainty becomes knowledge; thus by imitating some perfect ones, definitive knowledge is obtained. Perhaps denying this is denial of certainties. Rather, the truth in this matter is that only attainment of knowledge is obligatory, and it may be obtained by purity of conscience necessarily, so reflection is not obligatory upon him at all, as narrated by the greatest shaykh, the author of al-Futuhat, from the best of the truthful after the prophets, the leader of the pious, the imam of the saints, the Commander of the Faithful, Abu Bakr al-Siddiq (RA). And it may be obtained by some through imitation of one they know to be higher than them, and this does not need reflection, though if he reflected, it would be better. And it may be obtained by reflection, and this is more common among men, so reflection is obligatory only upon them. So understand and be firm. Those who permit taqlid said: If reflection were obligatory, the Companions would have done it and commanded it, but this is not reported, otherwise it would have been transmitted as in subsidiary matters; indeed the motives for such transmission are stronger due to everyone's engagement with it. We replied: If there was no reflection from them, it would lead to attributing ignorance of Allah and His attributes to them, which is false by consensus and necessarily from religion. Therefore, they did reflect and commanded it. As for transmission, it is a branch of extensive reflection and research as is the function of theology, and they were independent of extensive engagement in research due to purity of minds and witnessing revelation. And we do not concede the lack of command, i.e., their commanding those who followed them; rather, they were aware that it would be attained by the followers. For the intended reflection here is not the organization of proofs on the rules of logic, for affirmation is not dependent except for a rare few, but rather what yields tranquility for the heart, making it more obedient for the attainment of knowledge, as the Bedouin said: 'The camel dung indicates the camel, and the footprints indicate the journey; a sky with constellations and an earth with ravines — do they not indicate the All-Knowing, All-Aware?' The summary of the response is denial of the total absence of their reflection, and denial of the absence of their command, for the intended reflection is to the extent that the heart is tranquil with the attainment of affirmation, and this was attained by them, as evidenced by the story of the Bedouin. As for the claim that if it were not from them it would necessitate ignorance, it is not apparently correct, for their perfect preparation and the attention of the Messenger (saw) perhaps necessitated necessary knowledge.
Source pages p. 433

Commentary
This concluding section addresses a key usul dispute: can one engage in taqlid in matters of belief (aqliyat like the existence of God)? The majority hold it impermissible, but some Shafi'is and others allow it. The Hanafi position, as articulated here, is nuanced: the faith of a muqallid is valid (as established earlier), but taqlid alone does not produce the required certainty (yaqin). The author argues that definitive knowledge of God requires reflection or a direct certainty that may come through pure disposition or absorption of truth from a reliable source. The example of Abu Bakr al-Siddiq is given: he attained necessary knowledge without formal reflection. The debate is engaged through a dialectical exchange: opponents of taqlid argue from the impossibility of contradictory taqlid results, while proponents argue from the actual practice of the first generations. The resolution is that reflection (nazar) is obligatory only where it is the means to certainty; for those who have already attained certainty through other means, it is not obligatory. This is a sophisticated usul analysis that distinguishes between the theoretical obligation and practical necessity.

Key Terms
العقليات (al-aqliyat): المسائل العقلية كوجود الباري وصفاته
النظر (al-nazar): ما يفيد الطمأنينة للقلب بحصول التصديق، وليس تحرير الأدلة على قواعد المنطق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the majority view on taqlid in rational matters (aqliyat)?
A) Permissible
B) Obligatory
C) Not permissible
D) Recommended

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school prohibits taqlid in matters of belief but affirms that the faith of a muqallid is valid, emphasizing that certainty can be attained through multiple paths.

The Obligation of Taqlid: A Hanafi Defense of Nazar
Anecdote and logical argument on the dispute over taqlid vs nazar

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi position on the obligation of taqlid and apply the argument of infinite regress (tasalsul) to rebut the opposing view.

Core Idea
Taqlid is not obligatory because the muqallad is not a follower; obligating it leads to infinite regress.

This lesson examines a classic usul dispute: is it obligatory for the non-mujtahid to blindly follow (taqlid) or may he engage in independent reasoning (nazar)? The Hanafi position, defended here, holds that taqlid is not obligatory. Through a remarkable anecdote about Shaykh Abd al-Qadir al-Jilani and a logical argument against infinite regress, the author shows that nazar can yield certainty while taqlid does not guarantee it.

Guided Path
1
Analyze the anecdote
Read the story of Shaykh Abd al-Qadir and identify what it illustrates about knowledge acquisition: ‘ilm daruri arises without explicit reasoning.
The story is used to show that necessary knowledge can occur without taqlid.
2
Identify the opposing claim
Those who obligate taqlid (muqallidun) argue that nazar is prone to doubt and error, while taqlid is safe, so it must be obligatory.
They equate safety with obligation.
3
Unpack the Hanafi rebuttal
The author counters that the one being followed (muqallad) is not himself a follower; otherwise, obligating taqlid leads to infinite regress (tasalsul).
This is a standard logical argument in usul.
4
Address the refinement
A potential objection: the chain could end with the Prophet (PBUH). The author replies that following the Prophet is not taqlid but rather necessary knowledge from companionship.
The Prophet's knowledge is daruri, not nazari.
5
Evaluate the preferred answer
The best response is that taqlid does not guarantee certainty, whereas nazar based on certain premises produces certain knowledge. Unsafety in nazar comes from the reasoner's shortcoming, not the method.
Thus the argument for obligating taqlid fails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشيخ محيي الدين عبد القادر الجيلاني قدس سره الشريف في ساعة أو في نصف ساعة، وكان قدس سره في أرض العراق، وحضر أبو العباس الخضر عليه السلام، والبدلاء الآخرون، فصلى عليه ودفنه، وأمر الخضر أن يأتي رجلاً وسماه له من القسطنطينية، وكان هو كافراً غليظاً، فأتى به الخضر عنده، فلقنه الشيخ كلمتي الشهادتين وقص الشوارب، وأوصله إلى مقام البدلاء وأقامه مقام الميت وأخبر البدلاء الحاضرين فقالوا : سمعاً وطاعة، وقد تواتر أمثال هذه الحكايات منه رضي الله تعالى عنه تواتراً معنوياً، فانظر بعين الإنصاف أين كان ههنا النظر إنما حدث في قلبه علم ضروري. موجبو التقليد (قالوا : النظر مظنة الوقوع في الشبه (والضلال فإن طريق النظر غير آمن والتقليد طريق آمن فلا بد من إيجابه (قلنا هذا (منقوض بالمقلد) على صيغة المفعول، أي قلد من به فإنه غير مقلد للغير ( وإلا) يكن كذلك، بل كان هو مقلداً أيضاً (تسلسل، لأن الأخذ عن المؤيد بالوحي ليس تقليداً، بل) هو (علم نظري) فلو كان المقلد به مقلداً لكان له مقلد به أيضاً هذا وفيه نوع شبهة، فإنه يجوز أن تنتهي السلسلة إلى الرسول صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم، ولا نسلم أن المأخوذ منه علم ،نظري، بل يكون ضرورياً حاصلاً من بركة الصحبة الشريفة، والأولى في الجواب أن يقال : ليس التقليد طريقاً إلى تحصيل العلم اليقيني وإن كان قد يحصل به أيضاً بخلاف النظر فإنه إذا وقع في مقدمات مقطوعة لزم حصول العلم، وإنما يكون غير مأمون بتقصير منه كإنكار البديهيات وغيره فافهم .
Shaykh Muhyi al-Din Abd al-Qadir al-Jilani, may Allah sanctify his noble secret, in an hour or half an hour, while he was in the land of Iraq, Abu al-Abbas al-Khidr, peace be upon him, and the other substitutes (abdāl) attended, prayed over him and buried him. Al-Khidr was ordered to bring a man from Constantinople, who was a coarse disbeliever, and he brought him. The shaykh taught him the two testimonies of faith, trimmed his mustache, and elevated him to the rank of the substitutes, appointing him in the place of the deceased. The present substitutes said: 'We hear and obey.' Such stories from him, may Allah be pleased with him, have been recurrently transmitted in meaning. Look with the eye of fairness: where was there consideration (nazar) here? Rather, necessary knowledge arose in his heart. Those who obligate taqlid said: 'Consideration is prone to fall into doubts and misguidance, for the path of consideration is not safe, while the path of taqlid is safe, so it must be obligatory.' We said: 'This is refuted by the one who is the object of taqlid (muqallad), meaning the one who is followed, for he is not a follower of others. Otherwise, if it were not so but rather he himself were also a follower, there would be infinite regress (tasalsul), because taking from the one supported by revelation is not taqlid but rather theoretical knowledge. For if the one followed were also a follower, he would have someone else followed, etc.' There is a type of doubt in this, for it is possible that the chain ends with the Messenger, peace and blessings be upon him and his family and companions. We do not grant that the source is theoretical knowledge; rather, it is necessary knowledge obtained from the blessing of noble companionship. The best answer is to say: Taqlid is not a path to attaining certainty of knowledge, even though it may sometimes produce it, unlike consideration, for if it falls upon certain premises, knowledge necessarily results. It is only unsafe due to a shortcoming, like denying self-evident truths and the like. So understand.
Source pages p. 434

Commentary
This passage opens with a miraculous story of Shaykh Abd al-Qadir al-Jilani to illustrate that knowledge can be necessary (darurī) without theoretical reasoning. The author then presents the argument of those (muqallidūn) who obligate taqlid: they claim that independent reasoning (nazar) is unsafe and leads to error, so taqlid (following a qualified scholar) is obligatory. The author rebuts this by pointing out that the one being followed (muqallad) is not himself a follower; otherwise infinite regress would occur. A first objection is that the chain could end with the Prophet (PBUH), but the author refines the answer: taking from the Prophet is not taqlid but rather necessary knowledge from companionship. Instead, the proper response is that taqlid does not guarantee certain knowledge, whereas nazar based on certain premises does yield certainty. Thus the argument for obligating taqlid fails. This sets the stage for a more nuanced discussion on the obligation of taqlid for different categories of people.

Key Terms
علم ضروري (‘ilm ḍarūrī): Knowledge that arises necessarily without deliberate reasoning, often through divine grace or companionship with saints.
مقلد (muqallad): The person who is followed in taqlid, i.e., the authoritative scholar.
تسلسل (tasalsul): Infinite regress, an invalid chain of dependencies.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the initial argument of those who obligate taqlid lead to infinite regress?
A) Because the Prophet himself did taqlid.
B) Because the one followed (muqallad) would also need a follower, leading to an endless chain.
C) Because taqlid is impossible.
D) Because nazar is forbidden.

Key Takeaway
Taqlid is not obligatory because it leads to infinite regress and does not guarantee certainty; nazar with sound premises produces certain knowledge.

Is Tarjīḥ Required for the Layperson? The Hanafi Position
Dispute on whether a layperson must prefer one mujtahid over another

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on tarjīḥ for the layperson and evaluate the opposing argument using ijmāʿ and qiyās.

Core Idea
The layperson need not prefer one mujtahid; consensus and hardship override analogy.

This lesson examines a key usul dispute: when a layperson (ʿāmmī) faces conflicting rulings from different mujtahids, must he prefer one over the other? The passage presents the Hanafi view that no tarjīḥ is required, and responds to opponents who analogize to the mujtahid's duty. Understanding this debate clarifies the nature of taqlid itself and how the school balances evidence (dalīl) with practical ease (yusr).

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the chosen view (mukhtār) in the passage: the layperson is not required to perform tarjīḥ. This is based on the impossibility of taqlid if tarjīḥ were required and the great hardship it would cause.
The phrase 'المختار (بتعذر الترجيح بين المجتهدين للعامي)' clearly states this.
2
Identify the opponent's argument
The opponents (al-shāriṭūn) argue by analogy: the statements of mujtahids for the muqallid are like evidences for the mujtahid. Therefore, the muqallid must perform tarjīḥ among them, just as the mujtahid must prefer stronger evidence.
They say: 'أقوالهم للمقلد كالأدلة للمجتهد في حق وجوب العمل'.
3
Analyse the opponent's proof
Opponents use qiyās: as the mujtahid must prefer the stronger evidence, the muqallid must prefer the more knowledgeable mujtahid. They argue that the more learned scholar is more likely to be correct.
The qiyās is based on the similarity of 'obligation to act'.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response consists of two main rebuttals: (1) Consensus (ijmāʿ) takes precedence over qiyās – there is an established consensus that the layperson is not required to prefer. (2) There is a clear distinction (farq) between a mujtahid and a muqallid: tarjīḥ is easy for the mujtahid due to his expertise, but difficult for the layperson, leading to hardship.
The passage also adds that tarjīḥ may occur through taharrī (procedural preference) but that does not make it obligatory.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: under the Hanafi view, a layperson may follow any reliable mujtahid without preference. Under the opposing view, he would need to investigate and prefer the more knowledgeable, which could be burdensome.
This is why the Hanafi view is muftā bihī: it facilitates taqlid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
خاتمة في الاجتهاد والتقليد المختار (بتعذر الترجيح بين المجتهدين للعامي) فلو شرط ذلك لامتنع عادة التقليد، ولا أقل من الحرج العظيم (وأجيب بأنه يمكن الترجيح (بالتسامع ومشاهدة العلماء إليه. أقول : رجوع على أن غير المجتهد) الذي يجب عليه التقليد ربما كان عالماً ذا بصيرة) فيتمكن من الترجيح، وهذه العلاوة غير واردة، فإن مقصود المستدل أنه لو شرط الترجيح للتقليد في الكل كما هو مذهب الخصم لأدى إلى الحرج العظيم في العامي، ولا قائل بالفصل فتدبر. الشارطون (قالوا : أقوالهم للمقلد كالأدلة للمجتهد في حق وجوب العمل (فيجب) عليه (الترجيح) في أقوالهم عند التعارض كما يجب بأن الأعلم أقوى في إصابة الحق كما يجب الترجيح على المجتهد بأن هذا المفيد للظن القوي أقوى وأجيب بأن الإجماع مقدم على ما ذكرتم من القياس على المجتهد (و) أجيب أيضاً (بالفرق بين المقلد والمجتهد (فإنه) أي الترجيح (أسهل على المجتهد لكمال علمه وقوة ذهنه بخلاف العامي فإنه وإن (أمكن له في بعضهم (فربما لا يتيسر) فيقع في الحرج (أقول : على أن الترجيح قد يكون بالتحري كما قال علماؤنا في تعارض قياسين متعارضين فلا ينحصر في كونه أعلم ، ثم لك أن تجيب بوجه آخر، فإنه إنما يجب العمل على المجتهد بظنه والظن لا يحصل عند التعارض إلا بالترجيح بخلاف المقلد فإنه لا عبرة لظنه، وإنما العمل بقول من يحتمل وصوله إلى الحكم الواقعي وفيه فتوى الأفضل والمفضول سواء فافهم.
Conclusion on ijtihad and taqlid: The chosen view (that tarjīḥ is not possible for the layperson between mujtahids). For if that were conditioned, taqlid would normally be impossible, and at the very least there would be great hardship. It is answered that tarjīḥ is possible (through common hearsay and observing scholars). I say: This is a return to the fact that the non-mujtahid who must perform taqlid may be a knowledgeable person with insight, thus able to prefer. But this addition is not relevant, because the intent of the proponent is that if tarjīḥ were required for taqlid in every case as is the opponent's view, it would lead to great hardship for the layperson, and no one claims distinction. So reflect. Those who stipulate (tarjīḥ) said: Their statements for the muqallid are like evidence for the mujtahid in terms of obligation to act. So he must perform tarjīḥ among their statements when they conflict, just as it is obligatory for the mujtahid because the most knowledgeable is stronger in hitting the truth. It was answered that consensus takes precedence over what you mentioned of qiyas upon the mujtahid. And it was also answered by distinguishing between the muqallid and the mujtahid, for tarjīḥ is easier for the mujtahid due to his complete knowledge and strong intellect, unlike the layperson, for even if it is possible for him in some matters, perhaps it is not feasible, leading to hardship. I say: Moreover, tarjīḥ may be through taharrī as our scholars said regarding two conflicting qiyas, so it is not limited to being more knowledgeable. Then you can answer in another way: The mujtahid must act on his opinion, and opinion is not obtained in cases of conflict except through tarjīḥ, unlike the muqallid for whom his opinion is irrelevant. Rather, he acts on the saying of one who may have reached the actual ruling, and in that the fatwa of the superior and the inferior are equal. So understand.
Source pages p. 437

Commentary
This passage is a dispute concerning the obligation of tarjīḥ (preference) for a layperson (ʿāmmī) when faced with conflicting opinions of mujtahids. The Hanafi position, as stated in the mukhtār (chosen view), is that tarjīḥ is not required because it would render taqlid practically impossible or cause great hardship. The opponents (al-shāriṭūn) argue by analogy (qiyās) with a mujtahid, who must exercise tarjīḥ between evidences; similarly, the muqallid should prefer the more knowledgeable mujtahid. The Hanafi response is twofold: first, consensus (ijmāʿ) takes precedence over this qiyās; second, there is a clear difference between a mujtahid and a muqallid—tarjīḥ is easier for the former due to his expertise, whereas for the latter it may be impossible and cause hardship. The author adds that tarjīḥ can also occur through ijtihād-based preference (taḥarrī), not just knowledge. Furthermore, the muqallid’s own opinion carries no weight; he simply follows a mujtahid who might have reached the correct ruling. Thus, the superior and inferior mujtahid are equal in terms of permissibility of taqlid.

Key Terms
الترجيح (al-tarjīḥ): Preferring one mujtahid's opinion over another based on evidence or merit.
العامي (al-ʿāmmī): A layperson who is not a mujtahid and must follow taqlid.
المقلد (al-muqallid): One who performs taqlid, i.e., follows a mujtahid's opinion.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why do Hanafis reject the condition of tarjīḥ for a layperson in taqlid?
A) Because the layperson is capable of tarjīḥ
B) Because it would cause great hardship and contradict consensus
C) Because the mujtahid's opinion is always correct
D) Because the layperson must follow all mujtahids simultaneously

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school does not require a layperson to perform tarjīḥ among mujtahids due to consensus and to avoid hardship.
